Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARIA TERESA POLANCO- MONTERROSO
File: AXXX XX2 804 - Atlanta, GA
July 24, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Wayani J. Taylor, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Joy Lampley
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 In a decision dated May 27, 2016, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen removal proceedings, which had been conducted in absentia under section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A). The respondent timely appeals from that decision. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) seeks summary affirmance of the Immigration Judge’s decision. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact by an Immigration Judge under the clearly erroneous standard of review, but we may review de novo questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge should have granted reopening. The respondent concedes that she received notice of her hearing when she was personally served with the Notice to Appear (NTA) in November 2005, but the respondent asserts that due to her youth, she did not understand her obligation to appear in court (Respondent’s Br. at 2-3, 6). Furthermore, the respondent claims that four months prior to her hearing, she filed a Form EOIR-33, apprising the court of her change of address, upon the advice of an immigration consultant.
 
An order of removal following proceedings conducted in absentia pursuant to section 240(b)(5) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5), may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen which demonstrates that the alien failed to appear because of exceptional circumstances, because he or she did not receive proper notice of the time and place of the hearing, or because he or she was in federal or state custody and failed to appear through no fault of his or her own. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27 (BIA 1995); Matter of Gonzalez-Lopez, 20 I&N Dec. 644 (BIA 1993). A motion to reopen alleging lack of notice of the removal hearing, or that the respondent was in federal or state custody, may be filed at any time. However, a motion to reopen alleging exceptional circumstances, such as here, must be filed within 180 days after the entry of the removal order.
 
Upon review, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent’s motion to reopen alleging exceptional circumstances was filed beyond the 180 days allowed by statute. Thus, the motion was untimely. Even if timely-filed, the respondent’s motion does not establish the presence of exceptional circumstances to excuse her failure to appear at her hearing. Neither the respondent’s youth (she was 18 years old) nor ignorance about immigration proceedings would constitute an exceptional circumstance. Rather, it appears that the respondent believed that by filing a change of address, a new notice would be sent to her containing a new hearing date (Respondent’s Br. at 3-4, 6) (unnumbered).1
 
*2 We likewise find no merit to the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim (Exh. 5) (respondent’s affidavit fails to name the immigration consultant). See generally Montano Cisneros v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 514 F3d. 1224, 1226 (11th Cir. 2008) (stating but not explicitly holding that Lozada applies to claims of ineffective assistance rendered by non-attorneys). In claims asserting ineffective assistance of counsel, this Circuit requires a showing of prejudice which the respondent has not made in this case. See Dakane v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 399 F.3d 1269, 1274 (11th Cir. 2005) (agreeing with the Ninth Circuit that prejudice must be established). For all of these reasons, reopening was properly denied.
 
With regard to sua sponte reopening, we determine that sua sponte reopening is not merited as a matter of discretion. The facts in this case do not present an “exceptional situation,” particularly in consideration of the lack of due diligence by the respondent to clarify her immigration status in the past 9 years. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997); see also Matter of Jean, 23 I&N Dec. 373 (A.G. 2002). In addition, while the respondent desires reopening in order to pursue adjustment of status based on her marriage to a United States citizen, the respondent does not appear statutorily eligible for relief due to the manner of her entry into the United States.2 See section 245(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(a). Since the respondent was not physically present in the United States for at least one year prior to the issuance of the NTA, she also appears statutorily ineligible for the privilege of voluntary departure. See section 240B(b)(1)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b)(1)(A).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Ana Mann
FOR THE BOARD

2

APPLICATIONS: Reopening; stay of removal
 
*1 This case was last before us on February 28, 2017, when we entered a final administrative order dismissing the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s denial of his second motion to reopen his in absentia removal proceedings. On April 5, 2017, the respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, submitted the instant motion to reopen pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2, based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. He requests a stay of removal. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not responded to the motion. The motion exceeds the number limitations for motions to reopen, and it will be denied. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2); section 240(c)(7)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §1229a(c)(7)(A). The respondent’s stay request will also be denied.
 
The respondent contends that reopening sua sponte is warranted. He declares that his former counsel told him that he would request a change of venue and that he did not have to appear for his hearing. See Exhibit A, ¶¶ 4-5. The respondent states that he is aware that he could file a bar complaint against his former counsel but has not done so because his former counsel has already conceded that his representation was ineffective. Id, ¶ 9.
 
The respondent offers his affidavit and a printout of emails that purport to be between his current counsel and his former counsel. See Exhibits A and B. One of the emails indicates that his former counsel stated that a Lozada motion is appropriate at this time and that he will do a letter or sign an affidavit to help the respondent’s current counsel. See Exhibit B at 1. However, the respondent has not offered a letter or affidavit from his former counsel. The evidence is not adequate to demonstrate that the respondent’s former counsel conceded that his representation was ineffective. Further, the evidence is not sufficient to demonstrate that the respondent complied with the requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), as required by the United States Court of Appeals for the Seventh Circuit in whose jurisdiction the instant case arises. See Lin Xing Jiang v. Holder, 639 F.3d 751, 754-55 (7th Cir. 2012) (the alien must satisfy the criteria set forth in Lozada to support a claim of ineffectiveness of counsel); Marinov v. Holder, 687 F.3d 365, 368-69 (7th Cir. 2012) (satisfying the Lozada requirements is a necessary condition to obtaining reopening on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel).
 
*2 The respondent has not met his heavy burden of proving that his removal proceedings should be reopened. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 473 (BIA 1992). We find that an exercise of our sua sponte authority to reopen is not warranted. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, as the respondent’s motion exceeds the number limitations for motions to reopen, it will be denied, and his request for a stay of removal will also be denied.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen and request for a stay of removal are denied.
 
John Guendelsberger

2017 WL 4418379 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MICHELLE ANDREA JACOME A.K.A. MICHELLE ANDREA JACOME LOOR
File: AXXX XX7 829 - Charlotte, NC
July 10, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
P. Mercer Cauley, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Hilary Rainone
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; reconsideration
 
*1 On January 13, 2017, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s February 17, 2016, decision.1 The Immigration Judge determined, inter alia, that the respondent is removable as an alien inadmissible at the time of entry or adjustment of status under section 212(a)(6)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1282(a)(6)(C)(i)(2016), for procuring a visa or other documentation, or admission through fraud or willful mirepresentation of a material fact (See Exh. 1B). See section 237(a)(1)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(A) (2017). On February 13, 2017, the respondent filed a timely motion to reconsider with the Board, and on May 30, 2017, the respondent filed an amendment to the motion to reconsider. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006); section 240(c)(6) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(6); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b) (2017). On April 12, 2017, the respondent filed a motion to reopen seeking adjustment of status. See section 240(C)(7) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposes reopening and reconsideration, and the respondent has objected to the opposition by the DHS. The motions will be denied.
 
To the extent that the respondent contests the Immigration Judge’s findings and conclusions, we emphasize that a motion to reconsider is not an opportunity to raise issues that could have been raised on appeal, or to reiterate arguments previously considered and rejected on appeal. See Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991); Matter of Guevara, 20 I&N Dec. 238 (BIA 1991). The respondent has offered no persuasive arguments to convince us that we erred in fact or law in our prior order. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. With the following comments, the motion to reconsider will be denied.
 
The Immigration Judge found that the respondent’s claim that she was not yet separated from her second husband in May 2010 lacked credibility, and determined that the respondent was removable under section 212(a)(6)(C)(i) of the Act. The respondent now reiterates her argument that the Immigration Judge did not properly consider an order by the District Court of Mecklenburg County, North Carolina, amending the date of her separation from her second husband from 2009 to 2011. As the Immigration Judge noted, however, the district court’s amended order was based entirely on the facts provided by the respondent. Further, the district court’s order was but one factor that the Immigration Judge considered in rendering his adverse credibility determination. In our prior decision, we found no clear error in the Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including his finding with regard to credibility of the respondent’s testimony. The respondent has put forth no new arguments or evidence that would now persuade us to alter our prior finding.
 
*2 The respondent further argues that she was denied due process because the Immigration Judge prevented her ex-husband from testifying at her removal hearing. The respondent, however, was provided with an opportunity to present witness testimony, including the testimony of her ex-husband. The record reveals that on February 3, 2015, the Immigration Judge continued the proceedings to a date convenient to the respondent, the DHS attorney, and the Immigration Judge to enable the respondent to present her ex-husband as a witness (Tr. at 50-58). At a continued hearing on March 2, 2015, however, the respondent appeared before the Immigration Judge without her ex-husband (Tr. at 58). Moreover, the respondent’s attorney did not request a further continuance at that time. We also note that in her appeal brief the respondent stated that despite great effort, she was unable to bring her ex-husband to the Immigration Court because “he was simply not being cooperative and refused to appear” (Respondent’s Appeal Br. at 4). Under the circumstances, it was not unreasonable for the Immigration Judge to commence the respondent’s individual hearing. In sum, it has not been shown that the respondent’s ex-husband was prevented from testifying, and that the respondent suffered prejudice as a result.
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen will also be denied. The respondent seeks reopening to enable her to apply for adjustment of status based on a pending visa petition that her husband, whom she married on September 30, 2015, filed on her behalf. The respondent alleges ineffective assistance of counsel on the part of an attorney who, she claims, advised her husband that he did not need to file a visa petition on her behalf. The respondent, however, has not complied with any of the procedural requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim before this Board as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).2 See Barry v. Gonzales, 445 F.3d 741, 746 (4th Cir. 2006) (requiring that aliens raising ineffective assistance of counsel claims in immigration proceedings show at least substantial compliance with the Lozada requirements). Therefore, we will not consider the respondent’s allegation of ineffective assistance of counsel further.3
 
Moreover, it has been determined in these proceedings that the respondent, who was found to be inadmissible at the time of adjustment of status for seeking admission through fraud or willful misrepresentation of a material fact, does not merit a waiver of inadmissibility as a matter of discretion. The respondent has offered no new evidence with this motion to persuade us now that reopening for further consideration of possible discretionary relief is warranted. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). See also Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 472-73 (BIA 1988) (explaining that a party who seeks a remand or to reopen proceedings to pursue relief bears a “heavy burden” of proving that if proceedings before the Immigration Judge were reopened, with all the attendant delays, the new evidence would likely change the result in the case).
 
*3 Accordingly, the respondent’s motions to reopen and reconsider will be denied. The respondent’s request for a stay of removal is also denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reconsider is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD

The respondent’s petition for judicial review of the Board’s decision is currently pending. Jacome v. Sessions, No. 17-1195 (4th Cir., filed Feb. 13, 2017).

2

The statements of the respondent current counsel regarding matters of which he or she has no personal knowledge are not evidence. See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1980).

3

The respondent has offered evident that her former attorney has been disbarred. It has not been shown that the adverse disciplinary action was related in any way to the respondent’s case.

2017 WL 4118890 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LORENZO CUETO-JIMENEZ
File: AXXX XX3 526 - Harlingen, TX
June 30, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Raed O. Gonzalez, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)] - In the United States in violation of law
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
On June 6, 2012, an Immigration Judge entered an in absentia order of removal against the respondent. On July 6, 2012, the respondent through counsel filed a motion to reopen to rescind his in absentia order of removal, which the Immigration Judge denied on August 31, 2012. The respondent did not appeal the denial of the motion.
 
On June 27, 2016, the respondent, through new counsel, filed a second motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel. On July 21, 2016, the Immigration Judge denied the motion to reopen. The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, now appeals. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings regarding witness credibility and what is likely to happen to the respondent, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of Z-Z-O-, 26 I&N Dec. 586 (BIA 2015). We review all other issues, including questions of law, discretion, and judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
In general, the Immigration and Nationality Act and the regulations provide that an alien may file one motion to reopen, and any such motion must be filed within 90 days of the entry of the final administrative order of removal. Section 240(c)(7)(A), (C)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(A), (C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b). The Act and regulations provide an exception to the general rule in the case of an alien who seeks to rescind an in absentia order of removal. An in absentia order of removal may be rescinded by a motion to reopen filed within 180 days after the date of the order of removal if the alien demonstrates that the failure to appear was due to exceptional circumstances,1 or a motion to reopen filed at any time if the alien demonstrates that he did not receive notice in accordance with section 239(a)(1) or (2) of the Act. See section 240(c)(5)(C) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(5)(C); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii).
 
The Board and the Immigration Courts also have reopened cases filed more than 90 days after a final administrative order in which an alien proves that his claim was prejudiced by ineffective assistance of counsel and when the motion was filed within a reasonable time after discovering the ineffective assistance. Our review of allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel is governed by our analysis in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See Matter of Compean, Bangaly, and J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009). In Matter of Lozada, we stated that a motion to reopen based upon ineffective assistance of counsel requires that (1) the motion be supported by an affidavit of the applicant setting forth in detail the agreement between the applicant and her legal representative and what actions were to be taken on her behalf; (2) the counsel whose reputation is being impugned be informed of the allegations against him or her and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) the motion reflect whether a complaint had been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities, and if not, why not.
 
*2 The regulations provide that a motion to reopen removal proceedings shall not be granted unless it appears to the Board that the evidence sought to be offered is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). A motion to reopen will not be granted unless the respondent establishes prima facie eligibility for the underlying relief sought. See INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94 (1988). An applicant demonstrates prima facie eligibility when the evidence reveals a reasonable likelihood that the statutory requirements for relief have been satisfied. Matter of L-O-G-, 21 I&N Dec. 413 (BIA 1996).
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the July 27, 2016, motion to reopen because it was time-barred and number-barred. The motion was filed more than 4 years after the in absentia order of removal, and was the respondent’s second motion to reopen. See section 240(c)(7)(A), (C) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(A), (C).
 
The respondent claims that at the time of the in absentia order of removal he was eligible for adjustment of status based on his January 25, 2012, marriage to United States citizen Ms. Ocanas (Respondent’s Br. at 4, 10). Thus, he asserts that his prior attorney’s ineffectiveness in failing to notify him of his June 6, 2012, hearing prejudiced his ability to obtain relief in the form of adjustment of status. In support of this claim, he submitted on appeal his and Ms. Ocanas’ marriage certificate (Respondent’s Br. at 10, Attached Tab A).
 
We are not persuaded by the respondent’s arguments. First, the respondent did not sufficiently demonstrate that he did not receive notice of the June 6, 2012, hearing. The Immigration Judge properly concluded that notice to counsel constitutes notice to the respondent (I.J. at 2). See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.26(c)(2), 1292.5(a); see also Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien). Although the respondent provided copies of returned mail from his prior attorney as evidence that she did not inform the respondent of the June 6, 2012, hearing, we have no way of knowing the contents of the returned mail, including whether the notice of the hearing was one of the returned mail items (Respondent’s Br. at 5-6, 13; Motion to Reopen at 5-6, 9, Attached Tab D).2
 
Second, the respondent did not show actual prejudice with respect to his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel (Respondent’s Br. at 6-7, 12). The respondent did not provide any evidence of his marriage to Ms. Ocanas with the July 27, 2016, motion to reopen, and the record as of the filing of that motion did not reflect that the respondent was ever married to Ms. Ocanas (I.J. at 2).3 Rather, the record reflected only that the respondent divorced Ms. Chavez on September 1, 2011, and married Ms. Sanchez on November 16, 2015 (I.J. at 2; Motion to Reopen at 11, Tabs G, P).
 
*3 Also, the 2012 marriage to Ms. Ocanas alone did not establish prima facie evidence of eligibility for adjustment of status under section 245(a) of the Act. The record does not contain evidence that Ms. Ocanas filed a Form I-130 (Petition for Alien Relative) on the respondent’s behalf such that he would have been eligible to receive an immigrant visa, or that the respondent otherwise had an immigrant visa immediately available to him at the time of the June 6, 2012, hearing (I.J. at 2). See section 245(a) of the Act. Thus, the respondent has not shown that any ineffectiveness by his prior attorney in not filing an adjustment of status application caused the respondent to suffer actual prejudice.
 
Finally, although the respondent did not appeal the Immigration Judge’s August 31, 2012, denial of the motion to reopen to rescind the in absentia order, the denial decision was correct. In the July 12, 2012, motion the respondent stated only that he was unable to attend the June 6, 2012, hearing because he was working off-shore. The Immigration Judge properly concluded that this did not constitute exceptional circumstances as contemplated by section 240(e)(1) of the Act. The respondent’s work schedule was not beyond his control, given that he had over 3 months to change his schedule or alert the Immigration Judge of the conflict and request a new hearing (I.J. dated August 31, 2012, at 1).
 
For these reasons we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the July 27, 2016, motion to reopen. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Section 240(e)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(e)(1), states that “‘exceptional circumstances’ refers to circumstances (such as battery or extreme cruelty to the alien or any child or parent of the alien, serious illness of the alien, or serious illness or death of the spouse, child, or parent of the alien, but not including less compelling circumstances) beyond the control of the alien.”

2

The Immigration Judge issued the June 6, 2012, decision after a hearing on February 1, 2012, which is 3 months before the earliest date stamped on the returned mail. Our review of the digital audio recording reflects that the respondent’s prior attorney was present at the February 1, 2012, hearing.

3

The respondent also did not provide any evidence of his marriage to Ms. Ocanas with his July 6, 2012, motion to reopen. As stated previously, that motion indicated only that the respondent was unable to attend the June 6, 2012, hearing because he was working off-shore.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: WENDY YOLIBETH BAUTISTA SANTOS
File: AXXX XX6 233 - Atlanta, GA
June 16, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Grace Ruth Kennedy, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Samuel “Alex” Brown
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Motion to reopen
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Honduras, appeals the Immigration Judge’s December 19, 2016, decision denying her motion to reopen her removal proceedings. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed
 
We review findings of fact determined by an Immigration Judge, including credibility findings, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The Immigration Judge issued a removal order on February 14, 2013, upon granting the DHS’ unopposed motion to pretermit the respondent’s application for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(h). In her motion to reopen filed on August 8, 2016, the respondent asserted that it was unfair that the prior decision was rendered without a hearing; she points out she had previously appeared several times before the Immigration Judge. The respondent also emphasized her equities in this country that include her six United States citizen children, some of whom have medical issues. She also submitted proof that her United States citizen mother filed a visa petition on her behalf which was approved on March 28, 2016, and had a priority date of April 30, 2015 (Resp. Br. at 3; attachment F).
 
The Immigration Judge found the motion to reopen to be untimely, which the respondent has not contested (I.J. at 2). 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1) (setting out a general 90-day filing deadline for motions to reopen). He further declined to sua sponte reopen her proceedings. The Immigration Judge recounted the history of the case, and inter alia, concluded that the respondent had ample opportunity to challenge the DHS’ motion to pretermit below. The Immigration Judge also found that the respondent did not exercise due diligence in that she waited over 3 years to file her motion after her removal, and more than one year after the visa petition was approved. He further noted that the motion was supported by attorney statements rather than an affidavit from the respondent.
 
We adopt and affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the motion to reopen. Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994). Upon consideration of the factors addressed by the Immigration Judge, we agree that the respondent has not shown an “exceptional situation” exists that would warrant the Immigration Court’s or the Board’s exercise of its discretion to reopen these proceedings sua sponte. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). We therefore will not disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision in this regard.1
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent contends her due process rights were violated because the Immigration Judge denied her motion to reopen without providing a hearing on this matter (Resp. Br. at 2-3). As the respondent was afforded the opportunity to review the record and provide evidence and legal arguments in support of her motion to reopen, she was given a fundamentally fair proceeding (I.J. at 2). See Dukane v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 399 F.3d 1269, 1273 (11th Cir. 2005). The respondent has not met her burden to demonstrate that she was deprived of liberty without due process of law, and that the asserted errors caused her substantial prejudice by establishing that the outcome of this case would have been different but for the alleged error. Indrawati v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 779 F.3d 1284, 1299 (11th Cir. 2015).
 
On appeal, the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge failed to consider the criteria for equitably tolling the filing deadline for her motion to reopen (Resp. Br. at 3-5). In connection with the Immigration Judge’s sua sponte authority to reopen her removal order, the Immigration Judge considered the respondent’s due diligence in filing her motion and whether some extraordinary circumstance stood in her way of a timely filing (I.J. at 2-3). See Ruiz-Turcios v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 717 F.3d 847, 851 (11th Cir. 2013). We do not disturb this determination. Moreover, the respondent has not identified any basis to toll the deadline due to ineffective assistance of counsel, or met the filing requirements for such a claim. See Ruiz-Turcios v. U.S. Att’y Gen., supra; Gbaya v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 342 F.3d 1219, 1221-23 (11th Cir. 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988). As the respondent has not presented any basis to disturb the decision of the Immigration Judge or reopen these proceedings, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Ellen C Liebowitz
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We note that sua sponte reopening is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship. See Matter of J-J-, supra.

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JUANA PASCUAL TOLEDO ANDRES ANGELINA MARILU CRISTOBAL TOLEDO
Files: AXXX XX4 335 - Los Angeles, CA
AXXX XX4 336
June 15, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Carlos Ramirez, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; rescission
 
*1 On March 10, 2017, the Board dismissed the respondents’ appeal from the Immigration Judge’s October 20, 2016, decision denying their motion to reopen and rescind orders of removal entered in absentia on July 21, 2016. On April 6, 2017, the lead respondent, Juana Pascual Toledo Andres (AXXX XXX XXX), and her minor child, Angelina Marilu Cristobal Toledo (AXXX XXX XXX), filed the instant motion to reopen and rescind with the Board. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(2), 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). The motion will be denied.
 
An in absentia order of removal may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen filed within 180 days after the date of the order of removal which demonstrates that the alien failed to appear because of exceptional circumstances or upon a motion filed at any time if she did not receive proper notice of the hearing or she was in Federal or States custody and failed to appear through no fault of her own. See section 240(b)(5) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5). The term “exceptional circumstances” refers to exceptional circumstances beyond the control of the alien, such as serious illness of the alien or death of an immediate relative, but not including less compelling circumstances. See section 240(e)(1) of the Act.
 
The record reflects that on March 25, 2016, the lead respondent (hereinafter referred to in the singular as “the respondent”) received oral and written notice of the hearing scheduled for July 21, 2016. In her motion to reopen and rescind before the Immigration Judge and on appeal, the respondent argued that she did not receive proper notice of an April 4, 2016, hearing. The Immigration Judge and the Board noted, however, that there was no hearing scheduled for that date in these proceedings.
 
In the instant motion, the respondent asserts that she did not appear at the July 21, 2016, hearing because the minor respondent was sick (Respondent’s Declaration). She further asserts that after she received the order of removal in the mail, she sought the assistance of an individual whom she believed to be an attorney to prepare and file the motion before the Immigration Judge (Respondent’s Declaration). She also claims that although she attempted to provide the nonattorney with a receipt for the child’s medication, he based the motion to reopen and rescind on a claim of improper notice (Respondent’s Declaration).
 
In her motion to reopen and rescind filed before the Board, the respondent raises an ineffective assistance of counsel claim against the nonattorney who allegedly prepared and filed the prior motion and appeal on her behalf.1 See Varela v. INS, 204 F.3d 1237, 1240 (9th Cir. 2000) (holding that equitable tolling is appropriate where the alien was “defrauded by an individual purporting to provide legal representation”); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (discussing claims of ineffective assistance of counsel before the Board). Specifically, she alleges ineffective assistance of counsel on the part of the nonattorney for failure to argue that her failure to appear was due to “exceptional circumstances” (Respondent’s Motion at 10).
 
*2 The 180-filing deadline imposed on motions to reopen and rescind may be equitably tolled based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Avagyan v. Holder, 646 F.3d 672, 679 (9th Cir. 2011). The alien, however, must establish, inter alia, that she suffered prejudice because of the alleged ineffective assistance of counsel. See Mohammed v. Gonzales, 400 F.3d 785, 793 (9th Cir. 2005); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 562 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, supra, at 640. In the context of an ineffective assistance claim, prejudice results when the performance of counsel was so inadequate that it may have affected the outcome of the proceedings. See Maravilla Maravilla v. Ashcroft, 381 F.3d 855, 858 (9th Cir. 2004).
 
The respondent has not established that she suffered prejudice due to ineffective assistance of counsel because, upon consideration of the totality of the circumstances, we find that she has not shown that exceptional circumstance prevented her from appearing at the July 21, 2016, hearing. The respondent claims that although “[she] knew that [she] needed to appear in court that morning,” she was concerned about the minor respondent’s health because the child was “very sick” with fever and vomiting (Respondent’s Declaration). The respondent has offered a receipt from a clinic in support of her claim (Motion Tab E). The document bears the name “Angelina Critobal.” It does not, however, reflect a diagnosis, prognosis, course of treatment, or any other information regarding the nature and seriousness of any illness. See Celis-Castellano v. Ashcroft, 298 F.3d 888 (9th Cir. 2002) (finding that the alien’s lack of evidence regarding the severity of his asthma attack restricted Board’s ability to determine if illness was exceptional); Matter of J-P-, 22 I&N Dec. 33 (BIA 1998) (holding that the lack of medical evidence or other evidence establishing, in detail, the seriousness of the alien’s illness is a factor to consider in determining if he or she has met the burden of proof for exceptional circumstances excusing a failure to appear). Further, the respondent, who knew of her obligation to appear at the scheduled hearing, has neither asserted nor offered any evidence that she attempted to inform the Immigration Court of absence or of the reasons for the absence. See Celis-Castellano v. Ashcroft, supra (finding that the Board’s consideration of the alien’s failure to notify the Immigration Court of the reason for his absence was not improper).
 
In sum, equitable tolling of the 180-day filing deadline imposed on motions to reopen and rescind based on the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel is not warranted where the respondent has not demonstrated prejudice as a result of the ineffective assistance of counsel. Further, we find that no other exception to the regulatory restrictions imposed on motions to reopen applies to this motion. We recognize that the respondent has offered an application for asylum and withholding of removal (Form I-589) with the instant motion. She has not, however, offered evidence regarding changed circumstances or conditions in her native country, Guatemala, since the scheduled hearing on July 21, 2016, to warrant reopening pursuant to section 240(c)(7)(C)(ii) of the Act. See Matter of J-G-, 26 I&N Dec. 161 (BIA 2013) (holding that proceedings conducted in absentia may be reopened where the alien seeks asylum and withholding of removal based on changed country conditions); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3)(ii). Therefore, to the extent that the respondent seeks reopening to apply for asylum and withholding of removal, the instant motion will be denied as untimely and number-barred. See sections 240(c)(7)(A), (C)(i) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).
 
*3 For the foregoing reasons, the respondents’ motion to reopen the proceedings and rescind the in absentia order of removal will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen and rescind is denied.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent does not allege that her failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel. Cf. Matter of Grijalva-Barrera, 21 I&N Dec. 472, 473-74 (BIA 1996).

IN RE: YOLANDA ELIZABETH PENA-REYES MEYLIN VANESSA SOLIS-PENA

AXXX XX3 727

APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Rodolfo S. Zepeda, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Joy Lampley
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: None
 
*1 The respondents, natives and citizens of El Salvador, have appealed from the Immigration Judge’s October 26, 2016, decision denying the lead respondent’s request for a continuance and ordering the respondents’ removal from the United States.1 The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed an opposition to the appeal and motion for summary affirmance of the Immigration Judge’s decision. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
The respondent does not challenge the Immigration Judge’s determination that she is removable as charged (Tr. at 6;Resp. Br. at 2). At the hearing, the respondent’s counsel indicated that the respondent applied prosecutorial discretion and no other applications for relief (Tr. at 7). The DHS stated that it declined to exercise prosecutorial discretion (Id.). As the respondent conceded that she was subject to removal and did not apply for any relief from removal, the Immigration Judge ordered her removed from the United States (I.J. at 1-2; Tr. at 8).
 
On appeal, the respondent, through new counsel, asserts that her prior counsel was ineffective in that she “unilaterally decided not to allow the [r]respondent to file an asylum application” (Resp. Br. at 2). The respondent moves to remand the case for a new hearing.
 
The respondent has raised a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel in the instant motion. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988) (setting forth the procedural requirements for ineffective assistance of counsel claims before the Board). In this case, the respondent has not complied with any of Lozada’s procedural requirements. The respondent did not provide detailed affidavits attesting to the facts establishing ineffective assistance and setting forth the agreements that she reportedly entered into with her attorney. She has not informed her prior counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned of the allegations leveled against her and given her an opportunity to respond. Nor have she indicated whether a complaint has been filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities, and if not, why not. The respondent has failed entirely to comply with any of Lozada’s procedural requirements, which the Eleventh Circuit has determined are not unduly onerous. See Gbaya v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 342 F.3d 1219, 1222-23 (11th Cir.2003). Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s motion to will be remand is denied.
 
Edward R. Grant

MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:

ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Hana A. Sato
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed from the United States after determining that he had abandoned his opportunity to seek relief. This matter was before the Board on October 5, 2016, when we affirmed without opinion the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings in the Immigration Court. On November 28, 2016, the respondent filed the present motion with the Board, seeking to reopen proceedings on grounds of ineffective assistance of counsel. The Board has considered the opposition of Department of Homeland Security to the motion, but in consideration of the entirety of the circumstances presented in this matter, the motion will be granted.
 
It appears from the record that counsel wholly failed to file an application for relief and made misrepresentations concerning her failure to act. We are satisfied that the respondent has substantially met applicable procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). As counsel’s performance may have affected the outcome of the case before the Immigration Judge, the motion makes a showing of prejudice to the respondent. See Maravilla Maravilla v. Ashcroft, 381 F.3d 855, 858 (9th Cir. 2004). The Board will, therefore, reopen these proceedings under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a) and remand the record to the Immigration Judge to afford the respondent the opportunity to apply for any relief for which he may be eligible. We express no opinion as to the outcome, and on remand the parties should have the opportunity to present any relevant evidence and argument that they may have. The following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: These removal proceedings are reopened and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this decision.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JUAN CAMACHO-LUZ A.K.A. JUAN CAMACHO
File: AXXX XX6 499 - Chicago, IL
May 10, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Azin Arbab, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals the Immigration Judge’s decision dated June 1, 2016. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent another continuance and ordered him removed from the United States to Mexico. The record will be remanded.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that he received ineffective assistance of counsel where one of his previous attorneys from the Katz Law Office, Jessica Hernandez, Esquire, failed to submit pertinent information and documents on the date the respondent’s individual hearing was scheduled, as requested by the Immigration Judge. At the hearing on September 9, 2015, the Immigration Judge granted a continuance based on a visa petition filed on October 24, 2014, on the respondent’s behalf by his United States citizen wife, who is the mother of his United States citizen child (I.J. at 3; Tr. at 8-9). Due to the respondent’s criminal history, the Immigration Judge also requested that the respondent’s attorney submit several documents at the next hearing, including a copy of the respondent’s criminal conviction records.
 
At the next hearing, on June 1, 2016, the respondent appeared with another attorney from the Katz Law Office, who stated that he had been assigned the case only one week earlier, after the prior attorney assigned to this case, Jessica Hernandez, had suddenly left the law practice and not left detailed notes about the case. The Immigration Judge noted that a number of attorneys from that firm had filed Notices of Appearance on the respondent’s behalf. He further noted that the respondent did not comply with the request for documents made by the Immigration Court and found no good cause to grant a further continuance (I.J. at 3; Tr. at 13 and 16).
 
Although we understand the frustration noted by the Immigration Judge in the handling of this case by the respondent’s prior attorneys, all from the same law firm, the respondent’s arguments on appeal, including his affidavit, are persuasive that he may have received ineffective assistance of counsel from Ms. Hernandez. Even though it does not appear that the respondent has met all the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), we are persuaded by the arguments on appeal that Ms. Hernandez appears to have abandoned his case and there was insufficient time for the following attorney assigned to the respondent’s case to prepare and submit the required documents. Under these circumstances, we find that a remand is justified to provide the respondent with an opportunity to pursue his claims for relief.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings and the entry of a new decision consistent with the foregoing opinion.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Board Member Ana Mann respectfully dissents without separate opinion.
 
2017 WL 2570237 (BIA)

2017 WL 4736614 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SERGIO LEVARIO
File: AXXX XX4 208 - Chicago, IL
May 8, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Elizabeth I. Treacy
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 In an order dated January 18, 2017, the United States Court of Appeals for the Seventh Circuit granted the Government’s motion to remand. The Government sought a remand for the Board to “clarify its conclusion that [respondent] did not comply with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N 637 (BIA 1988).” After further consideration, we will again dismiss the appeal.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent, who claims ineffective assistance by his former counsel, was ordered removed from the United States in absentia on September 21, 2011. Notice is not at issue. As such, a motion to reopen based upon “exceptional circumstances” causing his failure to appear must have been filed within 180 days of the in absentia order of removal. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii); see also Matter of A-A-, 22 I&N Dec. 140 (BIA 1998); Matter of Lei, 22 I&N Dec. 113 (BIA 1998) (a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel does not constitute an exception to the 180-day time limit). The respondent, however, did not file his motion to reopen until December 21, 2015. Thus, his motion was untimely.
 
Upon further review, we find that the respondent has satisfied the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra. See Marinov v. Holder, 687 F.3d 365 (7th Cir. 2012) (requiring compliance with the Matter of Lozada procedures to establish ineffective assistance of counsel). Specifically, in the instant case, the respondent (1) submitted two personal affidavits regarding his former counsel’s representation of him; (2) presented evidence that he advised her of his allegations of ineffectiveness and submitted her response to those allegations; and (3) provided an explanation as to why he did not file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authorities.
 
However, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent has not demonstrated ineffective assistance by his former counsel (I.J. at 5). The respondent argues that his former counsel provided ineffective assistance by not advising him of the consequences of failing to appear at his scheduled September 21, 2011, hearing. However, the record reflects that the respondent did attend his June 8, 2011, hearing with his former counsel, and was served with a notice of hearing advising him of the consequences of failing to appear (I.J. at 4). The respondent has not presented documentary evidence to establish that he was in any way prevented from reading the advisals, or that his former attorney advised him in contradiction to those advisals. Thus, the respondent’s claim that he was unaware of the consequences for not appearing at his September 21, 2011, hearing is unfounded and contradicted by the record of this case. Moreover, the respondent has not demonstrated that equitable tolling is warranted in this case. See El- Gazawy v. Holder, 690 F.3d 852, 859 (7th Cir. 2012); Johnson v. Gonzales, 478 F.3d 795, 799 (7th Cir. 2007); Patel v. Gonzales, 442 F.3d 1011, 1016 (7th Cir. 2006). The respondent has not articulated what effort, if any, he made to pursue his rights during the 4 year period from the entry of his in absentia removal order and the filing of his motion to reopen.
 
*2 Finally, the respondent has not established that sua sponte reopening should be invoked to overcome the untimeliness of his motion. See Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, as we again decline to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s motion to reopen, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD
2017 WL 4736614 (BIA)
End of Document

2017 WL 2570245 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RUTILO DE LA SANCHA-TORRES
File: AXXX XX0 947 - New Orleans, LA
May 5, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Linda L. Walker, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of the Mexico, appeals from the November 2, 2016, decision of an Immigration Judge denying the respondent’s motion to reopen removal proceedings conducted in absentia on June 30, 2015. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not filed a reply to the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
Written notice is considered sufficient if provided at the most recent address provided under section 239(a)(1)(F) of the Act. The record reflects that the notice of hearing (NOH) was mailed to the respondent at the address he provided in the Notice to Appear (NTA), but it was returned as undeliverable by the postal service (I.J. at 1; Exhs. 1-3). While the respondent in his motion alleges that the address was entered incorrectly, the respondent signed the NTA, signifying the correctness of the address. Due process was satisfied here because the method of service was reasonably calculated to ensure that notice reached the alien. See United States v. Estrada-Trochez, 66 F.3d 733, 733-36 (5th Cir. 1995).
 
A respondent is not entitled to rescission of a removal order where the failure to receive actual notice of the time of the hearing is the result of his failure to comply with the obligation to keep the Immigration Court apprised of his current mailing address. The alien who evades notice cannot reopen the removal hearing. See Gomez-Palacios v. Holder, 560 F.3d 354, 358 (5th Cir. 2009) (finding alien’s failure to keep the Immigration Court apprised of his current mailing address is grounds for denying rescission of a removal order). Consequently, even if we accept the respondent’s assertion that the address was incorrectly entered into the NTA, he did not sufficiently show that his failure to receive actual notice was not due to his neglect of his obligation to correct his address. Moreover, as the Immigration Judge noted, the respondent failed to provide an affidavit or other evidence with the motion to establish what his correct address was at the time the NOH was mailed.
 
On appeal, the respondent through new counsel now alleges he is the victim of ineffective legal assistance from previous counsel, Brett Pouncey, who filed the motion to reopen. Specifically, the respondent alleges that Mr. Pouncey failed to advise the respondent that he needed to support the motion with an affidavit or other evidence. While the respondent’s current counsel maintains in the appeal brief that the respondent will inform Mr. Pouncey of the allegations lodged against him and a copy will be provided to the state bar, no evidence was proffered to support this assertion. Counsel’s arguments are not evidence. See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 506 (BIA 1980). Consequently, the respondent has not complied with the requirements to state a claim for ineffective assistance of counsel under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See Rodriguez-Manzano v. Holder, 666 F.3d 948, 953 (5th Cir. 2012) (court requires strict Lozada compliance); Lara v. Trominski, 216 F.3d 487, 497-98 (5th Cir. 2000) (the Board does not abuse its discretion by requiring compliance with Lozada).
 
*2 For the foregoing reasons, and those articulated by the Immigration Judge in his decision, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision, pursuant to our authority at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(5).
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward F. Kelly
FOR THE BOARD
2017 WL 2570245 (BIA)
End of Document

IN RE: ABIGAIL OSEI BOATENG A.K.A. ABIGAIL OSEI BOATENG
File: AXXX XX4 656 - Baltimore, MD
May 4, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Kwadwo Addo-Prempeh, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 The respondent’s motion to reopen is untimely filed and is denied on that basis. See section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). The motion has not been shown to qualify for any exception to the time limitations imposed by law on motions to reopen.1 The respondent’s status as the beneficiary of a relatively recently approved visa petition does not fall within any of the statutory or regulatory exceptions to the applicable filing deadlines.2 See Matter of Yauri, 25 I&N Dec. 103, 105 (BIA 2009).
 
On this record, the respondent has not provided an adequate basis to excuse the untimeliness of the present motion. Nor has she demonstrated an exceptional situation that would warrant reopening in the exercise of discretion under the Board’s sua sponte authority. See, e.g., Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997) (stating that “[t]he power to reopen on our own motion is not meant to be used as a general cure for filing defects or to otherwise circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship”). Accordingly, the respondent’s motion and related request for a stay of removal are denied.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

To the extent the respondent claims that she received ineffective assistance from former counsel, she has not complied with the procedural requirements for advancing a claim against former counsel, including the requirement that she show prejudice as a result of the alleged ineffective assistance. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). See also Matter of Compean, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009) (directing Board to apply Lozada standards to ineffective assistance of counsel claims pending the outcome of a rulemaking process).

2

The record before us does not reflect that the Department of Homeland Security has joined this motion. If such should become the case, the motion can be refiled. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3)(iii).

2017 WL 1951512 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: HAK BYONG KIM GYUM HWA KIM A.K.A. GYUM HWA JANG HAN BIT KIM KYU JUNG KIM
Files: AXXX XX2 691 - Seattle, WA
AXXX XX7 727
AXXX XX7 728
AXXX XX7 729
April 17, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Frederick P.S. Whang, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Hana A. Sato
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on June 8, 2016, when we dismissed the respondents’ appeal. On December 15, 2016, more than 6 months after the Board’s order, the respondents filed a motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has opposed the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondents’ motion is untimely, as it was not filed within 90 days of the Board’s final administrative decision. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondents argue, however, that their proceedings should be reopened based on ineffective assistance of counsel by attorney Steve Martin, who represented the lead respondent1 in 2005 for his adjustment of status application before the U.S. Citizenship and Immigration Services (USCIS) and who also represented the respondents during parts of these removal proceedings.
 
Specifically, the respondents argue that attorney Martin provided ineffective assistance because: (1) after the respondent retained him in September 2005 for the respondent’s adjustment of status application before the USCIS, he failed to advise the respondent that he should renew his employment authorization, which caused the respondent to continue in an unauthorized employment after his employment authorization expired in November 2006, which in turn resulted in the denial of the respondent’s application for adjustment of status by the USCIS; (2) after the Board dismissed the respondents’ appeal on June 8, 2016, he failed to advise the respondents that they could file a petition for review with the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit within 30 days of the Board’s decision; and (3) he relocated his law office from the State of Washington to the State of Kansas in or about 2014, and did not notify the respondents of his move (Motion, at 4-7). The respondents submitted the respondent’s statement, communications among their various attorneys, and documents showing that they complied with the requirements in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), with respect to attorney Martin (Motion Exhs. A-B). We will address the respondents’ arguments in turn.
 
First, as to attorney Martin’s failure to advise the respondent to renew his employment authorization with the USCIS in or about 2006, this was a matter that occurred outside these removal proceedings, and indeed before these removal proceedings were initiated in 2008. As noted by the Ninth Circuit, the extent to which aliens are entitled to effective assistance of counsel during deportation or removal proceedings is governed by the Fifth Amendment due process right to a fair hearing. Lara-Torres v. Ashcroft, 383 F.3d 968, 973 (9th Cir. 2004), amended sub nom. Lara-Torres v. Gonzales, 404 F.3d 1105 (9th Cir. 2005). To establish a due process violation, aliens must show, inter alia, the alleged ineffective assistance rendered “the proceeding . . . so fundamentally unfair that [they were] prevented from reasonably presenting [their] case.” Lara-Torres v. Ashcroft, supra (emphasis added); Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 894-97 (9th Cir. 2003).
 
*2 In Lara-Torres v. Ashcroft, the Ninth Circuit noted that the purported deficiencies of the attorney’s representation in that case did not “““pertain to the actual substance of the hearing (e.g., evidence presented or omitted, arguments raised or overlooked), let alone call the hearing’s fairness into question.” Lara-Torres v. Ashcroft, supra. Rather, the substance of the aliens’ claim was that, had it not been for their reliance on the attorney’s defective immigration law advice, they would not have been subject to removal proceedings at all. Id. This “unfairness,” however, did not taint the “““fairness” of the hearing. Id. The Ninth Circuit also noted that “[w]hen we have characterized counsel’s assistance as ineffective, . . . the legal services were rendered while proceedings were ongoing; that is, after a charging document was filed.” Lara-Torres v. Ashcroft, supra, at 974 (emphases original). To the extent the respondents’ ineffective assistance of counsel claim against attorney Martin relates to his failure to provide correct advice to the respondent in 2005 or 2006, and such failure did not affect the fairness of these removal proceedings initiated in 2008. Therefore, the respondents have not shown that a reopening of their removal proceedings is warranted based on the ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
In addition, the claimed ineffective assistance by attorney Martin in 2005 or 2006 long predated the respondent’s previous hearing in 2012. Therefore, these are not “new facts” supported by “new evidence” that was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the previous hearing. Section 240(c)(7)(B) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). Furthermore, the respondents have been represented by different attorneys during the course of these proceedings. After the Immigration Judge ordered the respondents removed on September 23, 2009, the respondents were represented by attorney Michael Piston on appeal and in their motion to reconsider filed in 2011. After the Board granted the motion and remanded the proceedings to the Immigration Judge in 2012, the respondents were again represented by attorney Martin before the Immigration Judge at their October 16, 2012, hearing. However, on their second appeal, the respondents were represented by attorney Michele Redburn, who appears to have been associated with attorney Piston’s law practice. The respondent has not explained why attorney Piston or attorney Redburn could not make the ineffective assistance of counsel claim against attorney Martin. If and to the extent attorney Piston and/or attorney Redburn provided ineffective assistance by failing to make an ineffective assistance of counsel claim against attorney Martin, the respondents have not made valid ineffective assistance of counsel claims against these attorneys by satisfying the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, and they have not explained why they could not do so.
 
*3 Next, as to the respondents’ claim that attorney Martin did not advise them about the Board’s June 8, 2016, decision dismissing their appeal or the possibility of filing a timely petition for review with the Ninth Circuit, as noted above the respondents were represented by attorney Redburn, not attorney Martin, on appeal. Despite the respondents’ argument in the motion and again in their Response to the DHS Opposition to their motion, the respondent admits that he was aware that another attorney was representing the respondents on appeal in 2012 (Motion Exh. A, at 14). Furthermore, although the respondent states that in November 2012 he paid a fee of at least $800 to attorney Martin for his appeal to the Board and considered attorney Martin as his “primary attorney,” the documents submitted show that the respondent made a check of $860 to attorney Piston (Motion Exh. A, at 14, 20), and it was attorney Redburn who represented him on appeal. Despite the respondent’s statement that neither attorney Martin nor attorney Piston contacted him regarding the Board’s June 8, 2016, decision, the documents submitted with the motion do not show what arrangement, if any, was in existence regarding the communication to the respondent following the Board’s decision (Motion Exh. A, at 14). The respondents, as noted above, have not made an ineffective assistance of counsel claim against attorney Redburn, nor did they explain why they cannot do so.
 
In any event, the respondent admits that he did receive a courtesy copy of the Board’s decision on his second appeal in June 2016 (Motion Exh. A, at 14). The cover letter to the courtesy copy received by the respondents, a copy of which was submitted with the motion, clearly informed the respondents that, if the attached decision orders that the respondents be removed or affirms an Immigration Judge’s decision ordering them be removed, “any petition for review of the attached decision must be filed with and received by the appropriate court of appeals within 30 days of the date of the decision” (Motion Exh. B. at 30). The respondent admits that he was aware that he could file a review of the Board’s decision in the event the Board dismissed his appeal, because he and attorney Martin had discussed the possibility of a dismissal by the Board and what the next step would be (Motion, at 14).2 Finally, despite the respondents’ assertion that attorney Martin moved his office to Kansas without informing them (Motion, at 7), the respondent’s statement does not show what difficulty, if any, he had in contacting attorney Martin or any other attorney as a result of that move (Motion Exh. A, at 13-14). Rather, the respondent’s statement shows that he only waited for attorney Martin to contact him (Motion Exh. A, at 14), until he received a call-in letter from the DHS in September 2016.
 
Based on the above, the respondents have not shown that their proceedings should be reopened based on the ineffective assistance of counsel by attorney Martin. We also decline to reopen the respondents’ proceedings in the exercise of discretion under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). Accordingly, the respondents’ motion will be denied. The motion for stay of removal is denied as moot.
 
*4 ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondents are a family of husband, wife, and their two children. The adult male respondent is the lead respondent and principal applicant for relief, and will be referred to as the “respondent” in the singular form in this decision.

2

The respondents’ motion prepared by present counsel states that the respondent was not aware that he could file for further review of the Board’s decision (Motion, at 6-7). However, counsel’s statement in a motion or brief is not evidence. See INS v. Phinpathya, 464 U.S. 183, 188-89 n.6 (1984); Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1980). Furthermore, this assertion is contradicted by the respondent’s own statement, as noted above.

2017 WL 1951511 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CHANSA KAPIJIMPANGA
File: AXXX XX0 530 - Dallas, TX
April 17, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lori B. Schoenberg, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before the Board on April 28, 2014, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s December 12, 2013, decision denying his motion to reopen and rescind his in absentia order of removal. The respondent now appeals the Immigration Judge’s December 19, 2016, decision denying his third motion to reopen and rescind his in absentia removal order. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
In our April 28, 2014, decision, we found the respondent is collaterally estopped from seeking to relitigate both his lack of notice and ineffective assistance of counsel arguments which were raised in his first motion and resolved against him in a final order from which no appeal was taken. See Amrollah v. Napolitano, 710 F.3d 568, 571 (5th Cir. 2013) (“A final decision by an immigration judge has a preclusive effect on future litigation and agency decisions.”). We further found that, because these issues have been finally resolved against him, the respondent’s in absentia removal order can be rescinded and proceedings reopened only “upon a motion filed within 180 days after the date of the order of removal [he] demonstrates that the failure to appear was because of exceptional circumstances” beyond his control. See sections 240(b)(5)(C)(i) and 240(e)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i) and 1229a(e)(1). Because the respondent’s motion was filed more than 4 years after his in absentia order was entered, we found it to be untimely, and we dismissed his appeal.
 
On November 16, 2016, the respondent filed a third untimely motion to reopen and rescind his in absentia order with the Immigration Judge. In his decision, the Immigration Judge noted again that the respondent is collaterally estopped from relitigating both his lack of notice and ineffective assistance of counsel claims, which he seeks to do in his current motion (I.J. at 1). See Amrollah v. Napolitano, supra. The Immigration Judge also found that even if the respondent’s current motion is not number-barred and subject to collateral estoppel, he did not establish he has been pursuing his rights diligently and/or some extraordinary circumstance stood in his way preventing him from timely filing such that the deadline should be equitably tolled (I.J. at 2). See Lugo-Resendez v. Lynch, 831 F.3d 337, 344 (5th Cir. 2016). As to the respondent’s claim that he has previously unavailable evidence to comply with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), the Immigration Judge noted that the evidence could have and should have been submitted with the respondent’s first motion (I.J. at 2). Therefore, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion.
 
*2 An in absentia order may be rescinded only if: (1) a motion to reopen is filed within 180 days of the date of the final order and the alien demonstrates there were exceptional circumstances excusing his failure to appear; or (2) a motion to reopen is filed at any time and the alien can demonstrate that failure to appear was due to a lack of proper notice. See section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii) (2017). As we found in our previous decision, the respondent is collaterally estopped from relitigating the issues of lack of notice and ineffective assistance of counsel. See Amrollah v. Napolitano, supra. Further, the respondent’s current motion is both time and number-barred.
 
Contrary to the respondent’s appellate assertions, the Immigration Judge did consider the issue of whether the motions deadline should be equitably tolled (I.J. at 2). See Mata v. Lynch, 135 S.Ct. 2150 (2015). In Lugo-Resendez, the Fifth Circuit instructed that the Board to should determine whether the respondent established: (1) that he has been pursuing his rights diligently, and (2) that some extraordinary circumstance stood in his way and prevented timely filing, such that equitable tolling applies. See id. (internal citations omitted). The first element requires the respondent to establish he pursued his rights with “reasonable diligence,” not “maximum feasible diligence.” See id. (internal citations omitted). The second element requires the respondent to establish an “extraordinary circumstance beyond his control” prevented him from complying with the applicable deadline. See id. (internal citations omitted). The Fifth Circuit also directed that we must consider the individual facts and circumstances of each case in determining whether equitable tolling is appropriate. See id. at 344-45 (internal citations omitted).
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that, based on the facts in this case, the respondent has not shown the required due diligence (I.J. at 2). See id. Specifically, the respondent has not shown he has been pursuing his rights diligently or that some extraordinary circumstance prevented him from timely filing. See id. The respondent was first ordered removed in January 2010 and was thereafter detained in November 2012, but he did not follow-up with his prior counsel, whom he now makes ineffective assistance of counsel claims against, during that period of time (I.J. at 2). It was only after the respondent was detained in 2012 that he took significant action (I.J. at 2). Further, the respondent did not file the instant motion until 2 years after his petition for review was summarily dismissed by the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit (I.J. at 2). See id. The respondent’s arguments on appeal are lengthy but not persuasive. The respondent’s motion is time and number-barred, subject to collateral estoppel pertaining to his lack of notice and ineffective assistance of counsel arguments, and he has not established that equitable tolling is warranted. See id.
 
*3 Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2017 WL 1951511 (BIA)
End of Document
