2017 WL 1230021 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: YAN CARLOS RAMIREZ-CORNEJO A.K.A. YAN CARLOS RAMIREZ A.K.A. YAN CARLOS CORNEJO-RAMIREZ
File: AXXX XX3 105 - Florence, AZ
February 22, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Christopher J. Stender, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
David C. Whipple
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I)] - Crime involving moral turpitude
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
On January 22, 2016, an Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen based on a claimed violation of due process, including ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, now appeals. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings regarding witness credibility and what is likely to happen to the respondent, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Vitug v. Holder, 723 F.3d 1056, 1063 (9th Cir. 2013); Ridore v. Holder, 696 F.3d 907, 915-16 (9th Cir. 2012); Matter of Z-Z-O-, 26 I&N Dec. 586 (BIA 2015). We review all other issues, including questions of law, discretion, and judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On March 21, 2006, the respondent was convicted of aggravated assault in Arizona. A Notice to Appear was issued charging the respondent with being inadmissible under sections 212(a)(6)(A)(i) and 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) of the Immigration and Nationality Act. On August 24, 2006, an Immigration Judge in Eloy, Arizona, granted the respondent’s request for voluntary departure with an alternate order of removal to Mexico. At the time the respondent was represented by attorneys from Stender & Pope (I.J. at 2; Respondent’s Br at 3; Motion at 3).1
 
The respondent re-entered the United States on January 1, 2007. On June 30, 2009, the respondent was convicted of disorderly conduct in Arizona and was sentenced to 1 year of imprisonment. He was issued a Notice to Appear on January 11, 2010.
 
On February 3, 2010, before an Immigration Judge in Florence, Arizona, the respondent admitted the allegations set forth in the Notice to Appear, and conceded the charges of removability. The Immigration Judge ordered the respondent to be removed.
 
On December 30, 2015, the respondent filed a motion to reopen the 2006 voluntary departure order with the Immigration Court in Florence (I.J. at 2; Respondent’s Br. at 3; Motion at 3). The respondent asserts that his prior attorney provided ineffective assistance of counsel during the 2006 proceedings, because his prior attorney failed to discover the existence of a visa petition that was filed in 1998 by his lawful permanent resident father and approved on October 9, 2002. The respondent also asserts that the government had an obligation to inform him and his father of the visa petition’s approval, but failed to do so. He argues that, at the time of the 2006 proceedings, the approved and then-current visa petition would have provided an opportunity for him to apply for adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Act as the “child” (at that time) of a lawful permanent resident, in conjunction with a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Act. Thus, he asserts that his 2006 decision to accept voluntary departure and waive his appeal rights was not considered and intelligent because his prior counsel and the government did not make him aware of his eligibility for relief (Respondent’s Br. at 6-7, 8, 9-10; Motion at 6-7, 8, 9-10; Attached Exh. K).
 
*2 The Immigration Judge denied the motion to reopen the 2006 voluntary departure order, because he determined that the motion was not properly before him. The Immigration Judge, citing regulations, determined that the motion should have been filed with the Eloy Immigration Court because that court had created the record of proceedings for the 2006 order and, thus, had administrative control over the record of proceedings (I.J. at 2). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23; see also 8 C.F.R. § 1003.31(a) (providing that all documents and applications that are to be considered in a proceeding before an Immigration Judge must be filed with the Immigration Court having administrative control over the record of proceedings).
 
The respondent also argues that he has substantially complied with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (Respondent’s Br. at 11; Motion at 11). Further, the respondent argues that the proceedings in 2010 violated due process because the 2006 proceedings were invalid and because a knowing and voluntary waiver of rights was not obtained. He contends that either the general deadline for seeking reopening should be equitably tolled or proceedings should be reopened sua sponte.
 
Even if the Immigration Judge incorrectly determined that the motion to reopen the 2006 voluntary departure order was not properly before him, the respondent did not demonstrate prejudice for purposes of his due process violation claim, specifically regarding his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Thus, we will affirm the decision to deny the motion to reopen.
 
As stated, the respondent contends that he was not aware of his father’s approved visa petition when he accepted pre-conclusion voluntary departure in 2006. Our review of the 2006 record reflects that there was no discussion of the 2002 approved petition by either party.
 
Nonetheless, we are not persuaded that the respondent’s acceptance of pre-conclusion voluntary departure was not considered and intelligent. The record reflects that the respondent knew about the approved petition filed by his mother; indeed, it was admitted into the record as Exhibit 2. Yet, at the hearing on August 24, 2006, the respondent’s prior counsel informed the Immigration Judge that the parties agreed to pre-conclusion voluntary departure (Tr. dated August 24, 2006, at 33). The respondent informed the Immigration Judge that he accepted the voluntary departure order and waived appeal (DHS Br. in Opposition to Motion to Reopen at 1-3; Tr. dated August 24, 2006, at 34-35). Thus, the record reflects that the respondent’s acceptance of pre-conclusion voluntary departure was considered and intelligent, and his waiver of appeal rights was knowing and voluntary.
 
Based on this information, we conclude that the respondent did not establish ineffective assistance of counsel or prejudice. It appears from the record that the respondent’s decision to accept pre-conclusion voluntary departure was strategic. As the beneficiary of his mother’s approved visa petition, the respondent could have applied for an immigrant visa from the United States consulate in Mexico. Thus, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the motion to reopen the 2006 voluntary departure order.
 
*3 We next address the Immigration Judge’s denial of the aspect of the motion to reopen relating to the validity of the 2010 proceedings. The Immigration Judge assumed for purposes of analysis that the 2010 proceedings violated the respondent’s due process rights, but ultimately concluded that reopening was not warranted because the respondent was not eligible for the relief sought. Specifically, the Immigration Judge found that the respondent re-entered the United States after his 2006 voluntary departure, had accrued 1 year of unlawful presence before the voluntary departure,2 and was “““seeking admission” via the adjustment of status application. The Immigration Judge concluded that the respondent therefore was inadmissible under section 212(a)(9)(B)(i)(II) of the Act, and thus, was not eligible for the relief sought, i.e., adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Act (I.J. at 3).
 
We held in Matter of Lemus-Losa, 25 I&N Dec. 734 (BIA 2012), that adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Act is unavailable to an alien who is inadmissible under section 212(a)(9)(B)(i)(II) of the Act, absent a waiver. See id. at 734, 744-45. The respondent did not request a waiver under section 212(a)(9)(B)(v) of the Act in conjunction with his motion to reopen. Indeed, the respondent did not address on appeal the Immigration Judge’s determination that he was inadmissible under section 212(a)(9)(B)(i)(II) of the Act, and thus, was not eligible for adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Act. We discern no reason to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the motion to reopen with respect to the 2010 removal order.
 
Finally, inasmuch as the respondent did not apply for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(a)(9)(B)(v) of the Act, and, is not otherwise eligible for adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Act based on his inadmissibility under section 212(a)(9)(B)(i)(II) of the Act, there is no basis to reopen sua sponte either by the Immigration Judge or the Board (Respondent’s Br. at 16-17; Motion at 16-17).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

2

The Immigration Judge found, in pertinent part, that before the respondent’s 2006 voluntary departure, he was unlawfully present in the United States for approximately 1 year and 8 months after reaching the age of 18 years (I.J. at 3).

IN RE: EDIS CELENIA GODOY-JIMENEZ KARLA SUYAPA GODOY-JIMENEZ KIANA CELENIA HERNANDEZ-GODOY
Files: AXXX XX1 085 - Charlotte, NC1 AXXX XXX XXX AXXX XXX XXX
February 14, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Daniel W. Thomann, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Scott D. Criss
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondents, natives and citizens of Honduras, have filed an appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision dated July 18, 2016, denying their request for a continuance and deeming their applications for relief abandoned. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed a motion opposing the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Immigration Judge properly concluded that the respondents failed to establish good cause for a continuance in their case (I.J. at 2). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29; Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354 (BIA 1983). The Immigration Judge noted below that counsel made only a verbal request for a continuance with no supporting documentary evidence (I.J. at 1-2; Tr. at 20-22, 24).2 Compare Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009) (setting forth factors to consider when adjudicating a motion for a continuance based on a pending visa petition, noting that focus should be on ultimate likelihood of success on adjustment application). On appeal, counsel argues that the respondents were denied due process. However, to date counsel has still not submitted evidence that he timely mailed the respondents’ asylum application (Form I-589) to the court, and it is not contained in the record before us. We conclude that the respondents have failed to exercise due diligence in pursuing any claims to relief. See Matter of Sibrun, supra, at 356.
 
The denial of a continuance will not be overturned on appeal unless it appears that the respondent was deprived of a full and fair hearing, and here the respondents have not shown such (I.J. at 1-2; Tr. at 1-25). See Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433 (BIA 1987). Furthermore, the respondents have failed to show prejudice. See Matter of Sibrun, supra, at 356-58. As noted above, to date they have not shown the pendency of any applications for relief. Id. Finally, we are not persuaded that sua sponte reopening would be appropriate in this case, where they have failed to establish due diligence in pursuing relief from removal. See generally Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992) (party seeking reopening bears heavy burden); see also Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (requirements for filing motion to reopen based on claim of ineffective assistance of counsel).
 
*2 We also agree with the Immigration Judge deeming all applications for relief abandoned (I.J. at 2). A potential applicant must file all application forms on or before a filing deadline set by the Immigration Court. See Matter of R-R-, 20 I&N Dec. 547, 549 (BIA 1992); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.31(c). The respondents were represented by the same attorney below and on appeal, who was informed of the filing deadline of July 18, 2016 on the record (I.J. at 1-2; Tr. at 17). To date, counsel for the respondents has not proffered a complete Form I-589 or supporting documents, and has not submitted evidence that the application was timely filed.3
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

2

Although counsel appeared telephonically, he could have faxed the supporting documentary evidence to the court, for example. Moreover, he could have submitted documentary evidence attached to his notice of appeal (Form EOIR-26).

3

As noted above, the record before us does not contain a Form I-589 for the respondents, nor does it contain proof of timely mailing as claimed by counsel. The lead respondent stated on the record that she understood the Immigration Judge’s decision deeming all applications as abandoned due to failure to file by the court’s deadline (Tr. at 24).

2017 WL 1230062 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DORILA MEDRANO-HENRIQUEZ
File: AXXX XX5 937 - Dallas, TX
February 13, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Aaron Chenault, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s August 8, 2016, decision denying her motion to reopen her removal proceedings. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the appeal, which will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3).
 
The record reflects that the Notice to Appear (NTA) was served personally on the respondent on January 22, 2016 (Exh. 1). At a hearing on February 29, 2016, the Immigration Judge informed the respondent and her attorney that her failure to appear for her next scheduled hearing and to meet the filing deadline for her application for relief would result in an order of removal (I.J. at 2). On March 8, 2016, the respondent filed a motion for a change of venue. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion and specifically noted that the application for relief was still due by March 10, 2016. Id. As the respondent did not appear for her hearing on March 10, 2016, and did not file an application for relief, the Immigration Judge ordered that all claims for relief were deemed abandoned and ordered the respondent removed. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.31(c).
 
On July 26, 2016, the respondent filed a motion to reopen. The respondent argued that exceptional circumstances caused her failure to appear for her hearing, as she did not believe she was required to appear for it or that she was required to submit her application by that date due to her release from immigration custody (I.J. at 2). The Immigration Judge determined that the respondent had not demonstrated that exceptional circumstances caused her to miss her filing deadline and that the record did not warrant sua sponte reopening (I.J. at 3).1
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s motion to reopen. A failure to request relief in accordance with a schedule set by the Immigration Judge constitutes a waiver of the opportunity to file for such relief. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.31(c) (stating that if an application is not filed within the time limit set by the Immigration Judge, the opportunity to file that application shall be deemed abandoned). The Board has long held that applications for benefits under the Act are properly denied as abandoned when the alien fails to timely file them. See Matter of R-R-, 20 I&N Dec. 547, 549 (BIA 1992); Matter of Jean, 17 I&N Dec. 100, 102 (BIA 1979).
 
*2 The administrative record reflects that the Immigration Judge informed both the respondent and her attorney that she must file the application for relief by her next hearing on March 10, 2016, or her application for relief would be deemed abandoned and she would be ordered removed (I.J. at 2). See Respondent’s Motion to Change Venue at 1. The respondent and her attorney did not file any applications for relief by the filing deadline. Accordingly, the Immigration Judge properly determined that the respondent’s claims for relief were deemed abandoned.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent and her counsel’s “““purported ignorance” do not warrant reopening of her proceedings (I.J. at 2). The respondent argued that her counsel’s clerk informed her that she did not need to appear for her March 10, 2016, hearing, and that she was unaware she was required to adhere to a filing deadline. See Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 2-3. The respondent’s counsel argued that he understood the Immigration Judge’s instructions to mean that the respondent must adhere to the filing deadline if she were still detained and on the detained docket. Id. at 6.
 
An attorney’s administrative error is not a basis for reopening absent compliance with the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1998); see also Hernandez-Ortez v. Holder, 741 F.3d 644, 647-48 (5th Cir. 2014) (making mandatory strict compliance with the Lozada requirements and emphasizing that former counsel must be informed of the allegations against them before the allegations are presented in a brief to the Board). The respondent has not established ineffective assistance of counsel for her failure to timely file her applications for relief. The respondent has not submitted an affidavit setting forth the facts and her agreement with her counsel, nor has she provided any evidence that her counsel has been made aware of the allegations against him and given an opportunity to respond, either after or before she filed her brief to the Board, nor did she indicate that a disciplinary complaint had been filed against that attorney. See Hernandez-Ortez v. Holder, supra, at 647. Thus, reopening is not warranted in this matter, as the respondent has not complied with the requirements set forth in Lozada. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

In his decision dated March 10, 2016, the Immigration Judge did not issue an in absentia order of removal. As the Immigration Judge did not actually order the respondent removed in absentia, the respondent’s motion to reopen on the basis of exceptional circumstances for failure to appear is inapplicable. Moreover, in a deportation case pursuant to section 242(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252(b), the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit held that where an alien failed to appear for a hearing and the Immigration Judge issued an order stating that all claims for relief were deemed abandoned, the hearing was not held in absentia. See Williams-Igwonobe v. Gonzales, 437 F.3d 453 (5th Cir. 2006).

2017 WL 1130641 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DAVINDER SINGH A.K.A. DAVINDER THAPA
File: AXXX XX3 371 - Harlingen, TX
February 6, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Suraj R. Singh, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Rod Wolthoff
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s May 26, 2016, decision denying his motion to reopen his removal proceedings after the issuance of an in absentia order of removal. The respondent’s appeal, which is opposed by the Department of Homeland Security, will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3).
 
The record reflects that the Notice to Appear (NTA) was served personally on the respondent on February 16, 2016 (Exh. 1). A Notice of Hearing (NOH) dated March 11, 2016, was sent to the respondent’s former counsel scheduling a hearing for April 6, 2016. As the respondent did not appear for his hearing on April 6, 2016, the Immigration Judge ordered him removed in absentia.
 
On May 9, 2016, the respondent filed a motion to reopen, alleging lack of notice of his April 6, 2016, hearing. The respondent claimed that he did not receive an NOH and that his prior attorney told him that his case would automatically change venue from Harlingen, Texas, to New York, New York, upon the respondent’s release from immigration custody (I.J. at unnumbered 1). The Immigration Judge found that the respondent received constructive notice of the hearing, as the record showed that the Immigration Court mailed the respondent’s prior attorney an NOH informing him of his April 6, 2016, hearing. Id.
 
The respondent filed his appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision on July 19, 2016. On appeal, through new counsel, the respondent admits that he was aware of his scheduled hearing date of April 6, 2016, but asserts that the Immigration Judge did not properly consider his claim that his former counsel gave him wrong advice not to appear for that hearing. See Respondent’s Brief at unnumbered 3-4. In addition, the respondent alleges that his prior attorney’s misleading advice constituted exceptional circumstances that caused his failure to appear. See Respondent’s Brief at unnumbered 4.
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s motion to reopen. As the respondent’s attorney received notice of the hearing, we agree with the Immigration Judge that proper notice was provided to the respondent. See Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a). The respondent did not establish that he failed to appear for the hearing due to “““exceptional circumstances,” as defined in section 240(e)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(e)(1). The Board has held that the mere filing of a motion for a change of venue does not relieve the respondent of his responsibility to appear, and unless the Immigration Judge grants the motion, the respondent remains obligated to appear at the appointed date and time. Matter of Rivera, 19 I&N Dec. 688 (BIA 1988); Matter of Patel, 19 I&N Dec. 260 (BIA 1985), aff’d, Patel v. INS, 803 F.2d 804 (5th Cir. 1986).
 
*2 We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “““exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), are satisfied. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec.472 (BIA 1996); see also Matter of Rivera, 21 I&N Dec. 599 (BIA 1996).
 
An attorney’s administrative error or his failure to advise a client that a change of venue motion had not been granted and that he must appear for the hearing is not a basis for reopening absent compliance with Lozada; see also Hernandez-Ortez v. Holder, 741 F.3d 644, 647-48 (5th Cir. 2014) (making mandatory strict compliance with the Lozada requirements and emphasizing that former counsel must be informed of the allegations against them before the allegations are presented in a brief to the Board). We are not persuaded by the respondent’s argument that the Immigration Judge erred in not properly considering his claim of ineffective assistance concerning his former counsel. The respondent has not established ineffective assistance of counsel for his failure to appear. The respondent has not submitted an affidavit describing the facts and his agreement with his former counsel, nor has he provided any evidence that his former counsel has been made aware of the allegations against him and given an opportunity to respond, either after or before he filed his brief to the Board, nor did he indicate that a disciplinary complaint had been filed against his prior attorney. See id. Thus, reopening is not warranted in this matter, as the respondent has made no attempt to comply with the requirements set forth in Lozada. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

2017 WL 1130673 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CHRISTIANH OLUBUNMI SUTTON
File: AXXX XX1 670 - Las Vegas, NV
January 30, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
J. Oscar Shaw, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Petition to remove conditional basis of permanent residence
 
*1 This case is before us pursuant to an order of the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit dated August 2, 2016, which denied the petition for review in part but remanded for the Board “to address [the petitioner’s] contention that her former attorney provided ineffective assistance of counsel by failing to pursue a hardship waiver.” We will again dismiss the respondent’s appeal.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
In our decision dated October 10, 2014, we affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent relief from removal. The Immigration Judge found that the respondent was not credible and that she had not provided sufficient evidence to meet her burden of establishing that she entered into a “““good faith marriage” for purposes of a waiver under section 216(c)(4)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1186a(c)(4)(B) (I.J. at 2-4). The Ninth Circuit dismissed the petition for review insofar as it challenged those findings. However, the Ninth Circuit remanded on the issue of whether the respondent’s prior counsel provided ineffective assistance because she did not file for a hardship waiver under section 216(c)(4)(A) of the Act.
 
We conclude that the respondent did not establish that her former counsel provided ineffective assistance. Even assuming that failure to file an application for a waiver before the United States Citizenship and Immigration Services could constitute ineffective assistance of counsel in removal proceedings, the respondent has not presented evidence that she substantially complied with the procedural requirements for demonstrating ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); United States v. Lopez-Chavez, 757 F.3d 1033, 1044 (9th Cir. 2014); Castillo-Perez v. INS, 213 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000). The respondent’s mere assertion of what action her former counsel should have taken is not sufficient to demonstrate ineffective assistance of counsel. Id.
 
*2 Additionally, the respondent has not demonstrated that she suffered any prejudice as a result of the purported ineffective assistance of her former counsel. See Rojas-Garcia v. Ashcroft, 339 F.3d 814, 826 (9th Cir. 2003) (holding that despite a finding of ineffective assistance of counsel, prejudice had not been demonstrated and relief was correctly denied); United States v. Lopez-Chavez, supra, at 1043 (explaining that it is not only necessary to demonstrate that counsel’s ineffective assistance rendered proceedings unfair, but also that such deficient performance resulted in prejudice). She did not provide any evidence to demonstrate that she was eligible for and had a reasonable likelihood of receiving the waiver. See Rojas-Garcia v. Ashcroft, supra, at 827 (explaining that it is necessary to establish both eligibility for relief and a plausibility that relief could have been granted to substantiate a finding of prejudice).
 
The respondent represented that she would file the waiver application and associated receipt with the Board with a motion to remand (Respondent’s Brief at 16). In our prior decision, we noted that we had not received any supplemental filings and to date we have not received evidence that such a waiver application was filed (BIA Dec. dated Oct. 10, 2014, at 3 n.1). The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Ellen C Liebowitz
FOR THE BOARD

2017 WL 1130694 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CHARANJIT SINGH NARINDER KAUR AMRIT SINGH HASHAN JEET KAUR
Files: AXXX XX1 215 - Seattle, WA
AXXX XX1 216
AXXX XX1 217
AXXX XX1 218
January 26, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Richard Wenfeng Chen, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I)] - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document (lead respondent)
 
Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled (co-respondents)
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondents have appealed from the decision of the Immigration Judge dated February 5, 2016, denying their motion to reopen the proceedings and rescind the in absentia order of removal entered against them on May 4, 2015. The appeal will be sustained, and the record will be remanded.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including any determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On January 1, 2015, the respondents were personally served with Notices to Appear (Exhs. 1, 1A, 1B, 1C; I.J. at 1-2). On April 21, 2015, a hearing notice was mailed to the respondents at the address provided by them informing them of a hearing scheduled for May 4, 2015 (Exh. 2; I.J. at 2). The respondents received proper notice of their removal hearing. Section 239(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(a).
 
On December 31, 2015, the respondents filed a motion to reopen and rescind the order of removal based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The two adult respondents assert in their affidavits that former counsel had told them they did not have to attend the hearing scheduled for May 4, 2015, in Seattle, Washington, because a motion to change venue to New York would be granted. They state that on October 12, 2015, they learned from current counsel that a removal order had been entered against them five months earlier.
 
An in absentia order of removal may be rescinded if, within 180 days, the alien files a motion to reopen that demonstrates that his or her absence was due to “exceptional circumstances.” Section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). Ineffective assistance of counsel qualifies as an exceptional circumstance warranting rescission of an in absentia order of removal. Reyes v. Ashcroft, 358 F.3d 592, 596 (9th Cir. 2004); Lo v. Ashcroft, 341 F.3d 934, 936-37 (9th Cir. 2003). Additionally, the 180-day filing deadline may be equitably tolled when an attorney’s fraudulent or ineffective performance “caus[ed] an essential action in her client’s case to be undertaken ineffectively, out of time, or not at all.” Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2003). The party invoking equitable tolling must exercise due diligence in “obtain[ing] vital information bearing on the existence of the claim.” Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176, 1193 (9th Cir. 2001).
 
*2 Before making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, an alien generally must comply with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Those requirements are as follows: (1) the alien should submit an affidavit detailing the agreement with former counsel; (2) the alien must notify former counsel of the allegations and afford counsel an opportunity to respond; and (3) the motion should reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities regarding such representation, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. These requirements “are not rigidly applied, especially when the record shows a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance.” Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1227 (9th Cir. 2002). Here, the respondents’ claims that former counsel told them they did not have to appear at the May 4, 2015 hearing and did not inform them of the removal order entered against them are not clear and obvious from the record. Therefore, they must comply with the Lozada requirements for making a claim to ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
The Immigration Judge found that the respondents complied with the third Lozada requirement but did not comply with the first and second. With respect to the first Lozada requirement, the Immigration Judge stated that the respondents’ affidavits are not reflective of their own thoughts because they are nearly identical and, although they are bolstered by a cover page indicating a motion to change venue filed by their former counsel was rejected, two receipts are weaker evidence because they don’t identify the attorney’s name and are consecutively numbered despite being issued 11 days apart (I.J. at 3-4; Motion at Tabs A, B, and C). We disagree as the affidavits submitted by the respondents establish their compliance with the first Lozada requirement. The fact that the affidavits are nearly identical is neither surprising nor indicative of a lack of veracity where the respondents are a married couple who were placed in consolidated removal proceedings together, they have been represented at all times by the same attorneys, they are relying on the same set of facts, and the affidavits were prepared with the assistance of current counsel. The affidavits detailing the respondents agreement with, and interactions with, former counsel satisfy the first Lozada requirement.
 
The Immigration Judge found that the respondents did not satisfy the second Lozada requirement because their notification to former counsel of the filing of a complaint suggested that she should “forward all [of her] response [s] to the [Departmental Disciplinary] Committee, if any,” rather than with the Immigration Court. We agree with the Immigration Judge that this suggestion is in error, as the purpose of the second requirement is to prevent abusive practices before the Immigration Court by allowing the attorney “whose integrity or competence is being impugned to present his version of events if he so chooses, thereby discouraging baseless allegations.” Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639. However, because the letter to the respondents’ former counsel specified that the complaint was filed pursuant to Matter of Lozada, she was sufficiently informed that the complaint was filed in connection with the respondent’s removal proceedings and could have filed a response with the Immigration Court if she so desired. Accordingly, the respondents complied with the second Lozada requirement.
 
*3 As we noted earlier, the Immigration Judge found that the respondents had complied with the third Lozada requirement. Additionally, the allegations made by the respondents, which are supported by documentary evidence, establish that the respondents received ineffective assistance from their former counsel. Because the respondents’ motion for rescission of the in absentia removal order is grounded on ineffective assistance of counsel, a showing of prejudice is not required. Lo v. Ashcroft, supra, at 939 n.6. Additionally, the respondents acted with due diligence in pursuing their claim after finding out that an in absentia removal order had been entered against them. Therefore, equitable tolling of the 180-day period for filing the motion is warranted. Accordingly, the respondents’ appeal will be sustained, the motion will be granted, and the record will be remanded for further proceedings.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained, and the motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and the entry of a new decision.
 
Hugh G. Mullane
FOR THE BOARD
2017 WL 1130694 (BIA)
End of Document

2017 WL 1130703 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ROBERTO CHAPARRO-RODRIGUEZ
File: AXXX XX4 467 - Tucson, AZ
January 25, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Christopher J. Slender, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Peter Gannon
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The Board’s decision entered on February 29, 2016, dismissed the respondent’s appeal. The respondent filed the present untimely motion to reopen proceedings on October 19, 2016. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security opposes the motion.
 
In seeking untimely reopening, the motion claims ineffective assistance of counsel on the part of attorneys who represented him before the Immigration Judge. The United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit has held the time limit for filing a motion may be tolled and proceedings reopened when ineffective assistance of counsel prevents an alien from reasonably presenting his case, and the alien is prejudiced. See Iturribarria v. I.N.S., 321 F.3d 889, 897-99 (9th Cir. 2003). In Rojas-Garcia v. Ashcroft, 339 F.3d 814, 826 (9th Cir. 2003), the court held that an alien failed to show prejudice when he could not “show that his admissibility arguments might have been successful on appeal to the BIA.” In Maravilla Maravilla v. Ashcroft, 381 F.3d 855, 858 (9th Cir. 2004), the court explained that an alien claiming ineffective assistance of counsel must demonstrate that counsel’s performance was so inadequate that it may have affected the outcome of the proceedings. See also Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 472-73 (BIA 1988) (explaining that a party who seeks to reopen proceedings before the Immigration Judge, with all the attendant delays, bears a “heavy burden” of proof that new evidence would likely change the result in his case).
 
The record in this matter shows that the respondent had a full and fair opportunity to present his case. The Immigration Judge at the hearing, and the Board on de novo review, considered all relevant factors, both positive and negative, regarding the respondent’s application for a waiver of inadmissibility. See Decision of the Board, Feb. 29, 2016, at 2-3; I.J. at 4-8. With the present motion, the respondent has submitted additional evidence of hardship to his United States citizen relatives, primarily concerning their health. See Mot., Tabs F-L. But the relatives’ health conditions were considered by the Immigration Judge (I.J. at 7), and the hardship together with the respondent’s other positive factors were determined not to outweigh his forgery offense involving a significant sum, lack of rehabilitation, and use of another’s identity documents for employment purposes. The additional evidence, even if it had been offered by counsel at the hearing, would not have led to a different weighing of the discretionary factors. The record indicates that the respondent’s counsel presented evidence of the positive factors and the motion does not demonstrate that counsel’s performance may have affected the outcome of the proceedings. See Maravilla Maravilla v. Ashcroft, supra.
 
*2 As the respondent has not established that counsel failed to offer evidence with which he might have been successful upon the Board’s de novo review on appeal, we conclude that ineffective assistance and prejudice are not established. See Rojas-Garcia v. Ashcroft, supra; see also Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The motion to reopen does not demonstrate any “exceptional situation” that would warrant reopening these proceedings under the Board’s limited sua sponte authority. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997). The motion therefore will be denied as time-barred.1
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen and the related request for a stay are denied.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JORGE OLIVAREZ VALADEZ
File: AXXX XX7 318 - Los Angeles, CA
January 24, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Alex Galvez, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from the June 2, 2016, decision of the Immigration Judge denying his request for a continuance. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The record reflects that the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s request for a continuance to pursue Consular processing and the filing of a waiver in conjunction with his approved I-130 visa petition filed by his citizen spouse. The Immigration Judge determined that the circumstances presented by the respondent, without more, failed to establish good cause for a continuance.
 
The decision whether to grant a continuance is committed to the sound discretion of the Immigration Judge, if good cause is shown. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29 and 1240.6. Thus, an Immigration Judge’s denial of a continuance request will not be overturned on appeal unless the respondent establishes that the denial deprived him of his due process right to a full and fair hearing. See Matter of Luviano, 21 I&N Dec. 235, 237 (BIA 1996) (citing Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433 (BIA 1987)). The alien must show that actual prejudice materially affecting the outcome of the case resulted from the denial of the continuance. See Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354, 356-57 (BIA 1983).
 
In this case, the Immigration Judge considered the relevant facts and circumstances presented and concluded that the respondent had approximately 18 months to present evidence that he filed an I-601A waiver, yet none was filed. On March 20, 2014, the Immigration Judge administratively closed proceedings in order to allow the respondent to pursue the I-601A waiver. However, on October 22, 2015, the Department of Homeland Security filed a motion to re-calendar the case noting that there was no indication that the I-601A had been filed. (I.J. at 2). The Immigration Judge reset the case for June 2, 2016, whereupon the respondent explained that no action had been taken because of financial and family problems. (Tr. at 43).
 
On this record, we are not persuaded by the respondent’s arguments on appeal that good cause was shown for a further continuance of proceedings. The respondent has not established that the Immigration Judge’s denial of his continuance request has compromised the fairness of his removal proceedings. Given the above facts, we find no good cause for the proceedings to have been continued any further.
 
To the extent the respondent contends that his former counsel provided ineffective assistance of counsel, beyond his assertions he has not “““substantially” complied with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Moreover, the record does not demonstrate that the respondent received constitutionally inadequate assistance of counsel that prejudiced his case. See Hernandez-Mendoza v. Gonzales, 537 F.3d 976, 978-79 (9th Cir. 2007). The respondent raises the speculative possibility that he may have been eligible for other forms of relief, but presents no evidence or argument to support this assertion (Resp. Br. at 8-9).
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2017 WL 1130710 (BIA)
End of Document

2017 WL 1045553 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: YOLANDA BERENICE MEJIA-ROMERO EDISSON ADEMIR BERRIOS-MEJIA
Files: AXXX XX5 960 - Dallas, TX
AXXX XX5 959
January 12, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Brian J. Hoffman, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on August 17, 2016, when we dismissed the respondents’ appeal from the Immigration Judge’s February 10, 2016, decision, in which he denied the respondents’ January 8, 2016, motion to reopen.1 This timely motion to reconsider followed. The respondents’ request for a waiver of the appellate filing fee is granted. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.8(a)(3). Their motion will be denied.
 
A motion to reconsider requests review based on alleged errors of law or fact in a prior decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1); see also Ramos-Bonilla v. Mukasey, 543 F.3d 216, 219 n.3 (5th Cir. 2008) (defining a motion to reconsider and distinguishing such a motion from a motion to reopen); Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399, 402 (BIA 1991) (same). To the extent that the respondents reiterate their arguments regarding (1) their compliance with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); (2) equitable tolling; and (3) the Board’s authority to sua sponte reopen proceedings, we are unpersuaded that the restatement of these arguments qualify as identified errors of law or fact in our prior decision. However, to the extent that the respondents raise new arguments identifying such alleged errors, we are unpersuaded that any errors exist for the reasons outlined below (Respondents’ Motion to Reconsider at 4-20).
 
Starting with compliance with the procedural requirements set forth in our precedent for claims involving ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondents urge us to adopt a test for motions filed before an Immigration Judge distinct from the test employed in Matter of Lozada, supra, and applied to a motion filed with the Board of Immigration Appeals in that specific case (Respondents’ Motion to Reconsider at 6-14). See id. at 639. In this regard, the respondents specifically maintain that the requirement “““that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond” should be relaxed when a motion to reopen is filed before an Immigration Court, as Matter of Lozada, supra, did not involve this specific factual scenario (Respondents’ Motion to Reconsider at 6-14). In the alternative, they argue that notice alone to prior counsel should be sufficient because to require otherwise would gut a respondent’s ability to timely file a motion before the Immigration Court or otherwise demonstrate due diligence (Respondents’ Motion to Reconsider at 11-13).
 
*2 We are unpersuaded that an attorney’s opportunity to respond where the attorney is being accused of ineffective assistance is less important when a motion is filed directly with the Immigration Court than it is where jurisdiction over the motion lies with this Board. Moreover, imposing such a requirement does not, as the respondents suggest, force the respondents to wait indefinitely for a response from former counsel (Respondents’ Motion to Reconsider at 8-10). It only requires that former counsel be furnished an opportunity to respond, and does not require a respondent to wait until an actual response is forthcoming. Accordingly, we thus conclude that our prior decision--following the specific language outlining procedural compliance with Matter of Lozada, supra--is not in error. See also Hernandez-Ortez v. Holder, 741 F.3d 644, 648 (5th Cir. 2014).
 
Nor are we persuaded that the respondents presented a sufficient basis for not strictly complying with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, for purposes of evaluating their ineffective assistance claim (Respondents’ Motion to Reconsider at 11-13). Instead, as we noted in our August 17, 2016, order, the lead respondent has not made clear what steps she took (and when she took such steps) in discovering the ineffective assistance alleged. As a result, we do not agree with the respondents’ assertion that the lead respondent demonstrated an adequate basis for not providing notice and an opportunity to respond for the respondents’ former counsel prior to filing their motion to reopen. In light of the foregoing, we again conclude that it is unnecessary to reach the substance of the respondents’ ineffective assistance claim, inasmuch as they maintain that the attorney’s deficient performance contributed to a notice problem and the respondents’ non-appearance at their final scheduled hearing (Respondents’ Motion to Reconsider at 13-14).
 
Turning to the issue of equitable tolling, we acknowledge that the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit, the jurisdiction in which this case arises, clarified its stance on this concept during the pendency of the respondents’ original appeal (Respondents’ Motion to Reconsider at 14-17). See Lugo-Resendez v. Lynch, 831 F.3d 337 (5th Cir. 2016) (explicitly explaining that the concept of equitable tolling is available within the Fifth Circuit and applies to the 90-day deadline generally applicable to motions to reopen in immigration proceedings). However, we conclude that the publication of Lugo-Resendez v. Lynch, supra, and the fact that we did not cite to or apply it in our August 17, 2016, decision does not alter the outcome in these proceedings.
 
*3 In this regard, the respondents assert in their motion to reconsider that we overlooked their appellate arguments on this topic, but this is a mischaracterization of our decision (Respondents’ Motion to Reconsider at 16-17). First, our decision noted that the respondents did not raise this issue before the Immigration Judge. The decision not to raise the issue was significant, as that is a basis for finding a waiver of the issue on appeal. See Matter of J-Y-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 260, 261 n.1 (BIA 2007). However, in considering the issue raised for the first time on appeal here, and assuming the availability of equitable tolling within the Fifth Circuit, we concluded that the respondents did not persuasively establish that they benefit from such tolling. Accordingly, we conclude that Lugo-Resendez v. Lynch, supra, does not alter the analysis in our August 17, 2016, decision. Nor does the absence of a citation to that decision demonstrate an error of law therein.
 
Finally, to the extent that the respondents maintain that we erred in declining to independently exercise our authority to reopen proceedings sua sponte (as opposed to reversing the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny sua sponte reopening), we are unpersuaded by their argument that the regulations accorded us such authority in the context of our August 17, 2016, decision (Respondents’ Motion to Reconsider at 20). However, notwithstanding the jurisdictional challenge, we independently determined that sua sponte reopening was not warranted in this case when reviewing the Immigration Judge’s decision. Because we concluded de novo that no exceptional circumstances exist, we agreed with the Immigration Judge that sua sponte reopening was not warranted.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondents’ motion to reconsider is denied.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

2017 WL 1045560 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: KAZI ABUL HASAN
File: AXXX XX9 522 - New York, NY
January 10, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Russell W. Hasan, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(II)] - Controlled substance violation
 
 
APPLICATION: Continuance; remand
 
The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s January 6, 2016, decision denying his request for a continuance and ordering him removed from the United States to Bangladesh. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact, including determinations as to credibility and the likelihood of future events, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); see also Hui Lin Huang v. Holder, 677 F.3d 130 (2d Cir. 2012); Matter of Z-Z-O-, 26 I&N Dec. 586 (BIA 2015); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). We review all other issues under a de novo standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent maintains that the Immigration Judge should have realized that his prior attorney was “a sub-standard immigration lawyer” because he filed the wrong form to seek a waiver of inadmissibility in March 2012, and suggests that she should have granted a continuance due to “hints of facts on the record for satisfying both 212(h) and 212(c) such that an Immigration Judge should be curious to see what evidence [the] respondent could introduce if given a further opportunity to do so” (Respondent’s Brief at 13-14).
 
As noted by the Immigration Judge, the respondent has been in removal proceedings for more than 5 years, and has not submitted a valid application or evidence establishing his eligibility for a waiver of inadmissibility under either section 212(c) or section 212(h) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (“Act”) (I.J. at 1-3). Absent a showing of a readily available form of relief from removal, the Immigration Judge acted properly in ordering the respondent’s removal from the United States. See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 505 (BIA 1980) (noting that once the DHS institutes proceedings, an Immigration Judge must order an alien deported if the evidence supports the charge). Based on the foregoing, we find that the respondent has not established good cause for a continuance. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29; Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433 (BIA 1987) (the decision to grant or deny a continuance is within the discretion of the Immigration Judge, if good cause is shown, and that decision will not be overturned on appeal unless it appears that the respondent was deprived of a full and fair hearing). Moreover, the respondent has not shown that he was denied a fair hearing.
 
*2 During the pendency of the appeal, the respondent filed a motion to remand the record for further proceedings based upon his claim that he received ineffective assistance of counsel (Respondent’s Brief at 7-13). Specifically, the respondent notes that his former attorney failed to file the appropriate form to apply for a waiver of inadmissibility in March 2012, and never corrected this mistake by filing a valid application along with supporting evidence and a copy of the filing fee receipt (Respondent’s Brief at 7-13).
 
In Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), we held that a motion to reopen based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel requires: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. While the respondent submitted an affidavit setting forth his agreement with his former counsel, he provided no evidence that he notified his prior counsel of the specific allegations leveled against him or filed a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authorities. Consequently, the respondent has not complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra. See Zheng v. U.S. Dept. of Justice, 409 F.3d 43 (2d Cir. 2005). Moreover, the respondent has offered no evidence that he would have prevailed if his prior attorney had been more effective in his representation or had filed a valid Application for Waiver of Grounds of Inadmissibility (Form I-601). Therefore, we conclude that the respondent has not demonstrated that he suffered any prejudice due to his former counsel’s actions or inactions. See Romero v. United States INS, 399 F.3d 109, 112 (2d Cir. 2005), citing United States v. Perez, 330 F.3d 97, 101 (2d Cir. 2001).
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s motion to remand is denied.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

2017 WL 1045583 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EMELDA MARINA POSAS-PALACIOS
File: AXXX XX9 959 - New Orleans, LA
January 3, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jack E. Wallace, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Charlotte S. Marquez
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Honduras, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated November 19, 2015, denying her motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia removal order dated February 8, 1999. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
An in absentia removal order may be rescinded only (1) upon a motion to reopen filed within 180 days after the date of the order of removal if the alien demonstrates that the failure to appear was because of exceptional circumstances, or (2) upon a motion to reopen filed at any time if the alien demonstrates that he or she did not receive notice of the hearing or was in Federal or State custody and did not appear through no fault of the alien.1 Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C); see also Matter of Guzman, 22 I&N Dec. 722, 723 (BIA 1999). Exceptional circumstances include serious illness of the alien or serious illness or death of an immediate relative of the alien, but not less compelling circumstances. Section 240(e)(1) of the Act.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent’s motion was untimely, as it was not filed within 180 days of the in absentia order, and that the respondent did not establish that she acted with due diligence so as to warrant equitable tolling of the filing deadline (I.J. at 3-4). See Lugo-Resendez v. Lynch, 831 F.3d 337, 343-44 (5th Cir. 2016) (requiring that an alien seeking equitable tolling show that she has been pursuing her rights diligently). The respondent’s motion to reopen was filed on October 30, 2015, more than 15 years after she was ordered removed. Moreover, although the respondent asserts that she diligently pursued her rights because she has maintained temporary protected status since 1999, the record reflects that the respondent waited approximately 6 years to file her motion to reopen after learning that she had been ordered removed in absentia, which demonstrates a lack of diligence in pursuing her rights (I.J. at 4; Motion to Reopen, Tab E). To the extent the respondent asserts that the actions of her former attorney prevented her from filing the motion sooner, she has not complied with the procedural requirements for setting forth a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); see also Mai v. Gonzales, 473 F.3d 162, 165 (5th Cir. 2006). Upon review, we agree that the motion was untimely and that equitable tolling of the time limit was unwarranted.
 
*2 The respondent also did not establish that exceptional circumstances prevented her appearance at her hearing. See Matter of J-P-, 22 I&N Dec. 33 (BIA 1998) (stating that the Board applies a totality of the circumstances test for determining whether exceptional circumstances prevented an alien’s appearance at a hearing). Considering the totality of the circumstances, including respondent’s claim that she was only in the United States for a few months before her hearing, that she had limited notice of the hearing date, and that she had difficulty obtaining transportation, we agree with the Immigration Judge that she did not demonstrate that her failure to appear at her hearing was because of “exceptional circumstances,” as contemplated in the Act (I.J. at 4).
 
Finally, we are not persuaded by the respondent’s claim that the Immigration Judge erred in declining to reopen proceedings sua sponte. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). The respondent has not provided evidence of circumstances that would justify the extraordinary remedy of reopening her removal proceedings sua sponte. See, e.g., Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999) (holding that discretion to reopen a case sua sponte is “an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations”); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997) (stating that sua sponte reopening is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations).
 
In light of the foregoing, the respondent’s appeal will be dismissed. The following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Garry D. Malphrus
FOR THE BOARD

1

The respondent does not argue that she did not receive notice of the February 8, 1999, hearing or that she failed to appear because she was in Federal or State custody.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NORMA MURILLO RODRIGUEZ
File: AXXX XX6 150 - El Paso, TX
December 30, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Amber Weeks, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Michael Pleters
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; reconsideration
 
*1 On August 31, 2016, the respondent submitted a “motion to reopen and reconsider” in proceedings in which the Board dismissed her appeal on August 26, 2014. The Board also granted a motion to reopen on January 9, 2015, for the limited purpose of reinstating voluntary departure. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the respondent’s current motion. The motion will be denied.
 
Whether treated as a motion to reopen or one seeking reconsideration, the respondent’s motion is untimely filed, and as a motion to reopen it is also number-barred. Sections 240(c)(6), (7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(6), (7); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(b), (c)(2). The respondent, however, asks that the Board exercise its sua sponte authority, also citing Lugo-Resendez v. Lynch, 831 F.3d 337(5th Cir. 2016) as supporting equitable tolling of the statutory time limitation.
 
As the respondent essentially concedes, a significant amount of the information in her motion was available, and could have been, but was not, presented at the time of her hearing. The respondent repeatedly states that this error on the part of her former counsel, Ms. Kretz, did not rise to the level of ineffective assistance of counsel, although she also describes her representation as “inadequate [and] lukewarm” (Motion at 2, 22). The respondent has not otherwise identified any extraordinary circumstances which prevented the timely filing of her motion. Rather, she alleges numerous errors on Ms. Kretz’s part in support of her motion (Motion at 22-25). The respondent also asserts that the Immigration Judge committed various errors in adjudicating her claim, none of which were addressed by prior counsel either during the proceedings below, or in a timely motion to reconsider (Motion at 25-30).
 
Given this record, the basis for the equitable tolling sought by the respondent is, in fact, a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. However, she has not complied with any of the requirements for such a claim. Hernandez-Ortez v. Holder, 741 F.3d 644 (5th Cir. 2014); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). In addition, as she has not shown that this evidence was previously unavailable and could not have been presented at her hearing, it is also not new for purposes of reopening. INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94 (1988). The respondent’s choice to not comply with the requirements for reopening based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel is not an exceptional situation supporting either sua sponte reopening or reconsideration. Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999) (“As a general matter, we invoke our sua sponte authority sparingly, treating it not as a general remedy for any hardships created by enforcement of the time and number limits in the motions regulations, but as an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations.”); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). Therefore, we find that neither reopening nor reconsideration in this regard is warranted.
 
*2 The respondent also seeks to introduce evidence that post-dates her hearing relating to medical treatment for herself, her brother and mother, and evidence that her daughter was granted Deferred Action for Childhood Arrivals, among other evidence (Motion, Tabs O-AA). However, the acquisition of equities, both positive and negative, following an order of removal is a typical, rather than exceptional, situation. Id. In this regard we note that the mother’s most recent medical records indicate that her medical conditions were chronic, and there is no evidence that they first manifested after the respondent’s hearing (Motion, Tab O). These records also reflect that the respondent’s mother was no longer experiencing anxiety related to her brother’s death in March of 2016, and that her chronic conditions were either asymptomatic or controlled with medication. The respondent’s brother’s medical issues also predated her hearing (Motion, Tabs R, S).
 
We are not unsympathetic to any worsening of the respondent’s brother’s medical issues, and recognize the medical issues faced by the respondent. However, as noted, such issues are not uncommon when a respondent remains in the United States following an order of removal. Moreover, we note that neither the respondent nor her mother or brother have mentioned what assistance other family members could provide if she was removed to Mexico. (Motion, pgs. 131 (mentioning the respondent’s “sisters and their families”), 132-34 (letters from family members referencing multiple nieces and nephews)). As we find that the respondent has not shown an exceptional situation in this regard, we decline to exercise our authority to sua sponte reopen proceedings. Id.; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a).
 
In sum, the respondent has not demonstrated that her motion is, or should be, exempt from the noted statutory bars. As such, we find that neither reopening nor reconsideration is warranted.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD

2016 WL 8471112 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CHRISTIAN RODRIGUEZ
File: AXXX XX0 226 - Hartford, CT
December 28, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Muneer Ahmad, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This proceeding was last before the Board on October 20, 2015, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal.1 On September 26, 2016, the respondent filed an untimely motion to reopen. Section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c) 97); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). The respondent does not contend that a statutory or regulatory exception to the time bar applies. Id. Nor does the respondent contend that the time limitation should be equitably tolled based on ineffective assistance of counsel.2
 
Rather, the respondent seeks either sua sponte reopening and administrative closure or sua sponte reopening and a remand for consideration of administrative closure or a continuance pending adjudication of his application for a U visa. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). We decline to exercise our sua sponte discretionary authority to reopen proceedings because the respondent has not demonstrated exceptional circumstances justifying the exercise of our sua sponte authority.
 
These proceedings need not be reopened for the respondent to pursue this application. The regulations provide exclusive jurisdiction over such applications to the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) and specifically address applications by U visa “petitioners” under final orders of removal, as is the case here. See 8 C.F.R. § 214.14(c)(1). The filing of the application has no effect on the Government’s authority to execute a final order, but a petitioner “may file a request for stay of removal pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 241.6(a) and 8 C.F.R. § 1241.6(a).” See 8 C.F.R. § 214.14(c)(1)(ii). The respondent does not assert that the DHS is seeking his removal while his application is pending or that he has found it necessary to file a request for a stay of removal.
 
We have considered the respondent’s arguments about conserving judicial resources. In addition, we have considered the additional administrative resources that may be expended if we grant reopening but the respondent’s U visa is not granted by USCIS, as well as the additional judicial resources that may be expended if the respondent then decides to again pursue his petition for review with the Second Circuit, or files another petition for review. Weighing these factors, we find that there is no guarantee that judicial resources will be conserved, and that there may be additional expenditures related to additional administrative resources and judicial resources if this motion is granted. As such, conserving judicial resources is not a discretionary factor weighing in the respondent’s favor.
 
*2 The respondent relies on several unpublished Board decisions, but these decisions are not binding precedent. Further, each decision rests on its own facts and the records of proceedings, including the specific factual scenarios, of these unpublished decisions are not available for review. Moreover, the unpublished decisions on which the respondent relies involved timely motions (Motion, Exhs. G-H), had nothing to do with a U visa (Motion, Exh. I), or involved consideration of whether to reopen an in absentia order of removal issued against an unaccompanied minor (Motion, Exh. J).
 
The respondent’s reliance on Matter of Sanchez-Sosa, 25 I&N Dec. 807 (BIA 2012), is misplaced. In Matter of Sanchez-Sosa, the respondent requested a continuance during his merits hearing before the Immigration Judge because his U visa petition was still pending. Here, in contrast, the respondent did not seek a continuance until September 26, 2016, when he filed his untimely motion to reopen almost one year after the Board issued its final administrative order of removal. As such, Matter of Sanchez-Sosa, supra, does not apply.3
 
Considering the governing provisions of 8 C.F.R. § 214.14, the regulatory scheme addressing aliens with final orders who are pursuing U visas, and the circumstances presented in this case, we decline to exercise our discretionary sua sponte authority to reopen this proceeding. Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to reopen will be denied. The following order will be issued.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD

Nor has the respondent complied with the requirements of Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), or demonstrated due diligence in pursuing his U visa claim.

2016 WL 8471180 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SERGIO LUIS DEL FIERRO-GARCIA
File: AXXX XX1 852 - Los Fresnos, TX
December 19, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Raed Olivieri Gonzalez, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)] - Convicted of controlled substance violation
 
 
APPLICATION: Motion to reopen; remand
 
The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s July 21, 2016, decision denying his untimely motion to reopen his proceedings. During the pendency of his appeal, the respondent filed a motion to remand. The appeal will be sustained, and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this opinion and for entry of a new decision.
 
The record reflects that the respondent was ordered removed on July 14, 2000, by an Immigration Judge (I.J. at 1). The respondent waived appeal (I.J. at 1). On June 29, 2016, approximately 16 years later, the respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge setting forth an ineffective assistance of counsel claim against his attorney during his prior proceedings and arguing that the motions deadline for his motion to reopen should be equitably tolled (I.J. at 1-2). In particular, the respondent argued that, during his prior proceedings, his former attorney did not advise him of his eligibility for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a), and failed to apply for said relief on his behalf. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposed the motion (I.J. at 1-2).
 
The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion as untimely (I.J. at 1). Specifically, the Immigration Judge found the respondent did not meet his burden in establishing an exception to the 90-day regulatory motions deadline applicable to his motion (I.J. at 1). See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.23(b)(4)(i)-(iv). In regard to the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, the Immigration Judge found the respondent did not show he exercised due diligence during the interstitial period after the motions deadline had passed, as required for equitable tolling to apply (I.J. at 1-2).
 
On appeal and through his motion to remand, the respondent argues his motion is subject to equitable tolling in light of the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit’s decision in Lugo-Resendez v. Lynch, 831 F.3d 337 (5th Cir. 2016). See Respondent’s Brief and Motion at 7-10. According to the respondent, under Lugo-Resendez, he established that he exercised due diligence because he “has been pursuing his rights diligently.” See id. at 9. The respondent claims he could not timely file the motion to reopen because he relied on his prior attorney’s “defective” assurances that he did not qualify for any relief from removal. See id. The respondent further argues that his case presents “exceptional circumstances” such that he can invoke equitable tolling. See id. at 9-12. In this regard, the respondent contends that his prior attorney was not competent, neglected her duties, and “emphatically” failed to identify or apply for relief for which he qualified. See id. at 10. The respondent argues that her “mishandling” of his case resulted in a denial of due process and that this amounts to “exceptional circumstances” justifying the reopening of his case. See id. at 11.
 
*2 There is no dispute the respondent’s motion to reopen is untimely inasmuch as it was filed approximately 16 years after the regulatory due date (I.J. at 1). Further, there is no dispute that the respondent complied with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), for purposes of his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel (I.J. at 1). Given the totality of the circumstances in this case, including the respondent’s lengthy residence in the United States, most of which was spent as a lawful permanent resident; his United States citizen wife and mother who both suffer from health problems; his United States citizen children, one with autism; and, his employment history, coupled with his Lozada compliance, we find that reopening is warranted. See Respondent’s Brief at 12.
 
Accordingly, the appeal will be sustained, and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this opinion and for entry of a new decision.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this opinion and for entry of a new decision.
 
Linda S. Wendtland
FOR THE BOARD
Board Member Roger A. Pauley respectfully dissents and would affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision for the reasons stated therein.
