2016 WL 8471210 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SANDRA ELIZABETH FLORES-FLORES
File: AXXX XX4 902 - Miami, FL
December 15, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Michelle Gonzalez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Michele Drucker
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, appeals from the October 14, 2015, decision of an Immigration Judge denying the respondent’s motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed a brief opposing the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
The respondent was personally served with a Notice to Appear (NTA) on March 14, 2006, which included notice of a January 11, 2007, hearing; a subsequent notice of hearing (NOH) for a June 6, 2007, hearing was mailed on March 8, 2007, to the respondent at the Florida address she provided and which is contained in the NTA, but the NOH was returned as undeliverable marked “““unclaimed, unable to forward” (I.J. at 1-2; Exhs. 1, 2). The respondent failed to appear at her June 6, 2007, hearing, and an in absentia order was properly entered against her.
 
Written notice is considered sufficient if provided at the most recent address provided under section 239(a)(1)(F) of the Act. The record reflects that the NOH was mailed to the respondent at the address she provided. Due process was satisfied here because the method of service was reasonably calculated to ensure that notice reached the alien. See Dominguez v. U.S. Atty. Gen., 284 F.3d 1258 (11th Cir. 2002) (notice of a removal hearing satisfies due process so long as the method of notice is conducted in a manner reasonably calculated to ensure that notice reaches the alien).
 
In the respondent’s motion to reopen, she admitted that she moved to California two months after being served the NTA, but she does not indicate that she filed a change of address with the Immigration Court. The duty to provide the immigration court with an updated address attached upon personal service of the NTA upon the respondent. See Matter of Anyelo, 25 I&N Dec. 337, 338-39 (BIA 2010). The respondent did not provide a change of address pursuant to this known duty until she filed her motion to reopen in 2015, over eight years after her removal hearing, and is therefore precluded from claiming that she did not receive notice. See Dominguez v. U.S. Att’y Gen., supra, at 1260 (“[N]otice [mailed] to the alien at the most recent address provided by the alien is sufficient notice” for in absentia removal). Because the immigration court properly mailed the NOH to the most recent address provided by the respondent at the Florida address listed on the NTA, she could not rely on the fact that the NOH was returned by the Postal Service as undeliverable.
 
*2 We find without merit the respondent’s appellate contention that the Immigration Judge erred in denying sua sponte reopening. Pursuant to our holding in Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997), the power to reopen sua sponte is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations where enforcing them might result in hardship. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). While the respondent has presented some positive factors, these factors either individually or in the aggregate are not so exceptional as to trigger the limited authority to reopen proceedings sua sponte. Indeed, many aliens who have been present in the United States for a period of years have accumulated favorable equities subsequent to being ordered removed. See Matter of Beckford, 22 I&N Dec. 1216 (BIA 2000) (burden is on the movant to show exceptional circumstances).
 
In addition, while the respondent argues that she was misled by a notario in 2007, as the Immigration Judge found, the respondent has not identified the notario, she has not averred that the notario held himself out as an attorney, and she has not shown that she filed a complaint with the relevant state disciplinary agency (I.J. at 2). See generally Montano Cisneros v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 514 F.3d 1224, 1225-26 (11th Cir. 2008) (suggesting that Lozada applies to ineffective assistance of counsel claims rendered by non-attorneys). Moreover, contrary to the respondent’s appellate contention, she has not adequately established diligence in filing the motion to reopen, despite having personally received and signed the NTA (I.J. at 2-3; Exh. 1).
 
For the foregoing reasons, and those articulated by the Immigration Judge in her decision, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision, pursuant to our authority at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(5).
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 8471210 (BIA)

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PEDRO OROZCO-YANEZ A.K.A. PEDRO YANEZ OROZCO
File: AXXX XX0 352 - Houston, TX
December 13, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Raed Gonzalez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
John Donovan
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on April 29, 2002, when we summarily dismissed the respondent’s appeal for failure to file a brief. This motion, submitted on May 27, 2016, is untimely filed. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security opposes the motion. The motion to reopen will be denied.
 
The respondent contends that reopening is warranted due to ineffective assistance of prior counsel. To support an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, an alien must comply with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988). The alien must also show that he was prejudiced by the prior counsel’s deficient representation. Id. at 640. Additionally, to establish that equitable tolling of the deadline for motions to reopen is warranted, the alien must show that he pursued his rights with reasonable diligence, and that some extraordinary circumstance beyond his control prevented timely filing. See Lugo-Resendez v. Lynch, 831 F.3d 337, 344 (5th Cir. 2016).
 
Equitable tolling is not warranted in this case because the respondent did not demonstrate that he exercised due diligence. The respondent was ordered removed in 1999 and his appeal was summarily dismissed in 2002. However, the evidence submitted with the motion indicates that the respondent did not inform his prior counsel of his grievances until September 1, 2015, and did not file this motion until May 27, 2016 (Respondent’s Motion, Tab F). The respondent has not explained this 13-year delay.
 
Because equitable tolling of the time limits is not warranted, the respondent’s motion is untimely. Further, the DHS’s records indicate that the respondent was removed from the United States on March 8, 2011 (DHS Opposition to Motion). Thus, the departure bar divests us of jurisdiction to consider his motion sua sponte. See Ovalles v. Holder, 577 F.3d 288, 295-97 (5th Cir. 2009); Matter of Armendarez, 24 I&N Dec. 646 (BIA 2008); see also Lugo-Resendez v. Lynch, supra, at 342-44 (holding that if an alien is entitled to equitable tolling of the time limitations for a motion to reopen, the motion is a timely statutory motion not subject to the departure bar, as opposed to a regulatory motion to sua sponte reopen that is subject to the departure bar). Accordingly, the motion to reopen will be denied as untimely.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is denied.
 
Molly Kendall-Clark
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 8471225 (BIA)

2016 WL 8468276 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ELENA VLADIMIROVNA HILDEBRANDT
File: AXXX XX6 362 - Chicago, IL
December 9, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Margaret W. Wong, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Alexandra Kostich
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Russia, appeals the Immigration Judge’s February 8, 2016, decision denying her motion to reopen her removal proceedings. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has submitted a response in opposition to the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact, including credibility findings, under the “““clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, discretion, or judgment, and all other issues de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On August 30, 2012, the Immigration Judge granted the respondent voluntary departure under section 240B(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c, allowing the respondent to leave the United States voluntarily on or before December 28, 2012, before an alternate order of removal would attach. The respondent did not leave the United States during the voluntary departure period (I.J. at 1). On February 1, 2016, the respondent filed a motion to reopen proceedings in order for the Immigration Court to reopen and terminate her case because she alleges that she is not subject to removal as charged in the Notice to Appear (“NTA”), or alternatively, to rescind her voluntary departure order and issue an alternative order of removal. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion, and the respondent appealed the denial. We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision in this case.
 
The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s request for reopening because the respondent was given numerous continuances to await the adjudication of different Alien Relative Petitions (Form I-130) filed on her behalf by different United States citizen spouses (I.J. at 1). Moreover, the Immigration Judge found that the respondent’s prior counsel was not ineffective in seeking voluntary departure because the respondent was not eligible for any other relief (I.J. at 1). Finally, the Immigration Judge noted that the respondent is barred from adjustment of status for not having departed the United States under the voluntary departure order (I.J. at 1).
 
On appeal, the respondent asserts that neither her prior counsel nor the Immigration Judge advised her of the consequences of failing to depart under a voluntary departure order (Respondent’s Brief at 6). Additionally, she alleges that but for her prior counsel’s ineffective assistance, she would be eligible to apply for adjustment of status with United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (“USCIS”) as an arriving alien (Respondent’s Brief at 6-9).
 
*2 There is no dispute that the respondent’s motion to reopen is untimely. Furthermore, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the voluntary departure admonishments were provided to the respondent in the decision dated August 30, 2012 (I.J. at 1). See section 240B(d) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(d). In regards to her claim of ineffective assistance of counsel during her removal proceedings, the respondent did not demonstrate that she was prejudiced by her prior counsel’s actions (I.J. at 1). See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 638-40 (BIA 1988) (explaining that in order to succeed on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien must show that she was prejudiced by her counsel’s performance). At the time of the August 30, 2012, hearing, the respondent had divorced her second husband who had filed the second, now revoked, petition on her behalf. The second visa petition filed on behalf of the respondent was revoked pursuant to the bar under section 204(c) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1154(c), which also bars approval of all future visa petitions filed on her behalf. Thus, the respondent did not make a prima facie showing that she was eligible for adjustment of status or any other type of relief, except for voluntary departure (I.J. at 1).
 
The respondent on appeal asserts that but for her prior counsel’s ineffective assistance, she would be eligible to apply for adjustment of status (Respondent’s Brief at 6-9). However, we note that during the pendency of this appeal, the respondent’s third visa petition filed on February 19, 2016, by her third and current husband has been denied (Respondent’s Brief at 5). The respondent is therefore not eligible for adjustment of status, which is the relief that she seeks. See Mansour v. INS, 230 F.3d 902, 907 (7th Cir. 2000) (explaining that a motion to reopen will be denied if the alien fails to establish prima facie eligibility for the relief sought, fails to introduce previously unavailable, material evidence, or is not entitled to the grant of relief which he sought); Matter of S-Y-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 247, 258 (BIA 2007) (same).
 
Therefore, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s motion to reopen. Because the respondent did not leave the United States voluntarily by December 28, 2012, her order of pre-conclusion voluntary departure was terminated and the alternative order of removal to Russia went into effect (I.J. at 1). See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(d), (e)(2). Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
*3 Hugh G. Mullane
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 8468276 (BIA)

2016 WL 8468279 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RAFAEL ARMANDO ROCANO BRITO A.K.A. RAFAEL ROCANO A.K.A. RAFAEL BRITO
File: AXXX XX5 418 - Hartford, CT
December 8, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Gerald Karikari, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Termination; administrative closure; remand
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Ecuador, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s September 8, 2015, decision denying his motion to terminate proceedings and ordering him removed to Ecuador. In the alternative, the respondent requests that we exercise our authority to administratively close proceedings, or remand the record to the Immigration Judge to terminate or administratively close proceedings. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has not filed a response to the appeal, the request for administrative closure, or the motion to remand. The appeal will be dismissed. The request for administrative closure and motion to remand will be denied.1
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On May 26, 2015, the respondent submitted written pleadings admitting to the allegations and conceding that he is subject to removal as set forth in the Notice to Appear (Exhs. 1 and 2). Based on the respondent’s admissions and concessions, the Immigration Judge found him subject to removal as charged (I.J. at 1, 3). See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.10(c). On September 8, 2015, the respondent filed a motion to terminate in which he argued that an order of removal would violate his constitutional rights (Respondent’s Motion to Terminate at 2, 4-12). On that same date, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to terminate (I.J. at 2-3).
 
On appeal, the respondent, through counsel, alleges that the Immigration Judge failed to provide a basis for denying the respondent’s motion to terminate other than the DHS’s opposition to the motion (Respondent’s Brief at 6). Accordingly, the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge did not make an informed adjudication of the motion (Respondent’s Brief at 6). However, the record does not support the allegations of the respondent’s counsel (I.J. at 2-3). Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 505-06 (BIA 1980) (finding that uncorroborated declarations of counsel are not evidence).
 
The Immigration Judge does not reference the DHS’s opposition position as a basis for denying the respondent’s motion to terminate (I.J. at 2-3). Rather, the Immigration Judge’s decision reflects that he considered the respondent’s arguments (I.J. at 2-3). The Immigration Judge, however, declined to reach the constitutional issues raised by the respondent and dismissed his argument that the Immigration and Nationality Act requires a proportionality review before an order of removal can be entered (I.J. at 2-3; Respondent’s Motion to Terminate at 2, 4-12). See Matter of Yanez, 23 I&N Dec. 390 (BIA 2002). As the respondent does not otherwise challenge this determination, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s motion to terminate. See Matter of J-S-, 24 I&N Dec. 520, 526 n.4 (A.G. 2008) (declining to address determinations not challenged on appeal).
 
*2 For the first time on appeal, the respondent argues that proceedings should be terminated so that he may pursue a provisional unlawful presence waiver of inadmissibility (Form I-601A) before United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (“USCIS”) as part of his application for consular processing based on an approved Alien Relative Petition (Form I-130) filed on his behalf by his United States citizen wife (Respondent’s Brief at 5-9). See generally 8 C.F.R. § 212(e)(7). We disagree.
 
The authority to terminate removal proceedings is limited by regulation to a few narrow circumstances not applicable here. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1238.1(e), 1239.2(f), 1240.10(d); cf. Matter of Rodriguez-Ruiz, 22 I&N Dec. 1378, 1380 (BIA 2000) (requiring termination where no charge of removability is sustained). Here, the DHS did not agree to terminate proceedings and the Immigration Judge found that removability has been established by the respondent’s admissions and concessions (I.J. at 1, 3; Tr. at 17, Exh. 2). See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.10(c). Accordingly, termination of these proceedings is not appropriate.
 
In the alternative, the respondent requests that we administratively close proceedings or remand proceedings to the Immigration Judge to consider administrative closure based on his prima facie eligibility for a provisional unlawful presence waiver of inadmissibility (Respondent’s Brief at 7-9). See 8 C.F.R. § 212.7(e)(4)(v). In evaluating whether administrative closure is appropriate, we consider, among other things, the reason administrative closure is sought, the likelihood the applicant will succeed on any petition, application, or other action he is pursuing outside of removal proceedings, and the ultimate outcome of removal proceedings when the case is recalendared before the Immigration Judge or the appeal is reinstated before the Board. See Matter of Avetisyan, 25 I&N Dec. 688 (BIA 2012). Administrative closure is not appropriate for a purely speculative event or action, such as the approval of an application for which prima facie eligibility has not been established. Id.
 
To support his request for administrative closure on appeal, the respondent proffers evidence of his eligibility for a provisional unlawful presence waiver of inadmissibility pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 212.7(e)(3) (Respondent’s Brief at Tabs A-D, G). However this evidence was not provided to the Immigration Judge and the Board does not consider new evidence presented for the first time on appeal. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(iv); Matter of Fedorenko, 19 I&N Dec. 57, 73-74 & n.10 (BIA 1984). As the respondent has not established that he is prima facie eligible for a provisional unlawful presence waiver of inadmissibility, we are not persuaded to exercise our authority to administratively close proceedings. See Matter of Avetisyan, supra.
 
*3 Additionally, the respondent has not demonstrated that proceedings should be remanded for the Immigration Judge to consider administratively closing proceedings based on the proffered evidence (Respondent’s Brief at 8-9). “A motion to remand for the purpose of presenting additional evidence must conform to the same standards as a motion to reopen and will only be granted if the evidence was previously unavailable and would likely change the result in the case.” Matter of L-A-C-, 26 I&N Dec. 516, 526 (BIA 2015) (citing Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 471-72 (BIA 1992)).
 
However, the respondent has not established that the documents attached to his appeal were previously unavailable (Respondent’s Brief at 8-9). Rather, the proffered evidence pre-dates the Immigration Judge’s September 8, 2015, decision (Respondent’s Brief at Tabs A-D, G). Moreover, while the respondent concedes that his prior counsel did not submit this evidence to the Immigration Judge or request administrative closure based on a provisional unlawful presence waiver of inadmissibility, he does not allege ineffective assistance of counsel or present evidence which meets the procedural requirements for such a claim. (Respondent’s Brief at 5, 9). See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); see also Yang v. Gonzales, 478 F.3d 133, 142-43 (2d Cir. 2007) (requiring substantial compliance with Matter of Lozada). Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to remand will also be denied.
 
Lastly, we are not persuaded by the respondent’s due process arguments raised on appeal. In particular, he asserts that the Immigration Judge violated his due process rights by failing to advise him of his right to request that proceedings be administratively closed based on his eligibility for a provisional unlawful presence waiver of inadmissibility (Respondent’s Brief at 9-10). To establish a due process violation, the respondent must show that the proceeding was so fundamentally unfair that he was prevented from reasonably presenting his case. See section 240(b)(4) of the Act. 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(4); Jiang v. Mukasey, 522 F.3d 266, 270-71 (2d Cir. 2008). He must also show prejudice, which means that the outcome of the proceeding may have been affected by the alleged violation. See Waldron v. INS, 17 F.3d 511, 518 (2d Cir. 1993) (requiring that prejudice be established to prevail on a due process claim). On appeal, the respondent has not alleged that the Immigration Judge prevented him from reasonably presenting his case, nor has he articulated how the outcome of the proceeding might have been affected by the Immigration Judge’s inaction (Respondent’s Brief at 9-10). Thus, the respondent has not established that the Immigration Judge violated his due process rights.
 
*4 Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s motion to administratively close is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s motion to remand is denied.
 
Garry D. Malphrus
FOR THE BOARD

2016 WL 8468323 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: HENRY JOEL CRUZ-SIERRA
File: AXXX XX4 643 - Fort Snelling, MN
December 1, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ignatius Udeani, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Laura W. Trosen
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
 
APPLICATION: Motion to suppress evidence; termination
 
The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s denial of his motion to suppress evidence and terminate removal proceedings. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact under a “clearly erroneous” standard. See Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); see also Matter of Z-Z-O-, 26 I&N Dec. 586 (BIA 2015). We review all other issues, including issues of law, judgment, or discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent challenges the Immigration Judge’s denial of his motion to suppress. Further, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred in failing to allow him to amend his pleadings.
 
Regarding the respondent’s challenge of the Immigration Judge’s denial of his request to amend pleadings, the regulation at 8 C.F.R. § 1240.10(c), reads that an Immigration Judge shall require the respondent to plead to the Notice to Appear (NTA), Form I-862, and, if the Immigration Judge is satisfied that no issues of law or fact remain, the Immigration Judge may determine that removability has been established by the admissions of the respondent. In this matter, on September 16, 2014, the respondent admitted through counsel all the factual allegations on the NTA and conceded the charge of removability (Tr. at 4). Nevertheless, at the respondent’s subsequent master calendar hearing held on September 22, 2015, the respondent through new counsel, sought to withdraw his pleadings. The respondent did not assert that the facts, as previously admitted, were incorrect. Rather, the respondent asserted before the Immigration Judge that he wished to file a motion to suppress the evidence as the information relating to his removability was obtained as a result of an unlawful detention, interrogation, and seizure. Also, the respondent suggested that he was ineffectively represented by his former counsel as she did not discuss with him the charges of removability and manner in which he was apprehended by immigration officials (Tr. at 12).
 
We concur with the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s request to amend his pleadings. The respondent has not shown that he was ineffectively represented or complied with any of the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), to support his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Moreover, the respondent has not shown egregious circumstances warranting the amendment of his pleadings. The respondent does not assert that the pleadings provided by his initial attorney were a result of coercion or duress or that the charges and factual allegation in the NTA are inaccurate. See Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377 (BIA 1986) (“Absent egregious circumstances, a distinct and formal admission made before, during, or even after a proceeding by an attorney acting in his professional capacity binds his client as a judicial admission.”). Accordingly, we conclude that the Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s motion to amend his pleadings. See Perez-Mejia v. Holder, 641 F.3d 1143 (9th Cir. 2011).
 
*2 Regarding the respondent’s motion to suppress, in a written decision dated December 2, 2015, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to suppress the evidence of his inadmissibility obtained by Immigration and Customs Enforcement (ICE) agents. In an order dated January 12, 2016, the Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged and ordered him removed to Honduras. The “exclusionary rule,” which requires a court to suppress evidence that is the fruit of an unlawful arrest or of other official conduct which violates the Fourth Amendment, arose in the context of criminal proceedings. It is well-established that the exclusionary rule is generally not applicable in deportation (or removal) proceedings. INS v. Lopez-Mendoza, 468 U.S. 1032, 1038 (1984). The Supreme Court, however, has warned that egregious violations of Fourth Amendment or other liberties that might transgress notions of fundamental fairness and undermine the probative value of the evidence obtained may justify use of the exclusionary rule. Id. at 1050-51. In expressing reluctance to apply the exclusionary rule in the deportation context, the Supreme Court balanced the likely social benefits of excluding unlawfully seized evidence against the likely costs. Id. at 1050; see Lopez-Fernandez v. Holder, 735 F.3d 1043, 1046 (8th Cir. 2013).
 
In particular, the Supreme Court reasoned that the deterrent value of the exclusionary rule is low due to the availability of alternative deportation procedures, while the social cost is high because courts would be compelled to suppress reliable evidence and then “release from custody persons who would then immediately resume their commission of a crime through their continuing, unlawful presence in this country.” See INS v. Lopez-Mendoza, supra, at 1050; Lopez-Fernandez v. Holder, supra, at 1046. “Even the objective of deterring Fourth Amendment violations should not require such a result.” See Lopez Fernandez v. Holder, supra, at 1046. In Lopez-Gabriel v. Holder, 653 F.3d 683, 686 (8th Cir. 2011), the Eighth Circuit in which this case arises noted that the case for exclusion of evidence is even weaker where the alleged misconduct was committed by an agent of a separate sovereign. The court found that if evidence were suppressed in a federal civil immigration proceeding, any deterrent effect on a local police officer would be highly attenuated. See Lopez-Gabriel v. Holder, supra, at 686; citing United States v. Janis, 428 U.S. 433, 457-58 (1976). The Eighth Circuit has not developed “an exhaustive list of the conduct that could constitute an egregious constitutional violation,” but looks at the totality of the circumstances. See Lopez-Fernandez v. Holder, supra, at 1047.
 
*3 In the present case, we find no error in the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s motion for suppression because the respondent’s motion did not establish a prima facie case of an egregious violation of the Fourth Amendment. The respondent claimed in his motion that he was questioned, arrested, and detained by the police and ICE because of his race. However, the respondent did not offer any facts or evidence to support his assumption that he was questioned and detained based on his race. The respondent does not describe any unreasonable show or use of force, or any otherwise egregious conduct during his questioning and arrest by the police. As the record does not contain any evidence to corroborate the respondent’s assertion that he was questioned and arrested based on his race, we conclude that the Immigration Judge was correct in denying the respondent’s motion to suppress evidence. See Lopez-Gabriel v. Holder, supra, at 686-87 (evidence of police officer’s alleged egregious violation during a traffic stop of Guatemalan alien was insufficient to warrant a hearing on the alien’s motion to suppress, which was filed during removal proceedings: no facts supported the alien’s assumption that he was stopped and arrested based on his Hispanic appearance, and the unrebutted police report declared that the officer stopped the alien for driving with a heavily cracked windshield and arrested him for lack of proper identification). On the whole, the record supports the Immigration Judge’s resolution in this matter.
 
Finally, the record reflects that the respondent submitted timely proof of having paid the voluntary departure bond. Hence, the period of voluntary departure will be reinstated.
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart the United States, without expense to the Government, within 60 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”). See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b); see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty as provided by the regulations and the statute and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.
 
*4 WARNING: If the respondent files a motion to reopen or reconsider prior to the expiration of the voluntary departure period set forth above, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated; the period allowed for voluntary departure is not stayed, tolled, or extended. If the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated upon the filing of a motion, the penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(e)(1).
 
WARNING: If, prior to departing the United States, the respondent files any judicial challenge to this administratively final order, such as a petition for review pursuant to section 242 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated, and the alternate order of removal shall immediately take effect. However, if the respondent files a petition for review and then departs the United States within 30 days of such filing, the respondent will not be deemed to have departed under an order of removal if the alien provides to the DHS such evidence of his or her departure that the Immigration and Customs Enforcement Field Office Director of the DHS may require and provides evidence DHS deems sufficient that he or she has remained outside of the United States. The penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply to an alien who files a petition for review, notwithstanding any period of time that he or she remains in the United States while the petition for review is pending. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(i).
 
Roger Pauley

2016 WL 8188581 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FRANCISCO ARGUELLES-NOLASCO
File: AXXX XX1 503 - Louisville, KY
November 18, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jeffrey Kyle McClain, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated December 22, 2015, denying his motion for a continuance. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact made by the Immigration Judge under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); see also Matter of Z-Z-O-, 26 I&N Dec. 586 (BIA 2015). We review all other issues, including issues of law, judgment or discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent challenges the Immigration Judge denial of his motion to continue. Also, the respondent argues that he was ineffectively represented by his former counsel and seeks to remand proceedings to the Immigration Judge.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s request for a continuance of his removal proceedings. The decision to grant or deny a continuance is within the discretion of the Immigration Judge, if good cause is shown, and that decision will not be overturned on appeal unless it appears that the respondent was deprived of a full and fair hearing. Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433 (BIA 1987); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6.
 
In the instant case, on January 13, 2014, the Immigration Judge granted the respondent a continuance of nearly two years for attorney preparation. At a hearing held on December 22, 2015, the respondent sought an additional continuance in order to petition the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) for prosecutorial discretion and to provide DHS with certified dispositions of his criminal record (Tr. at 9). However, we note that the DHS attorney specifically stated at the hearing, when asked for his position with regard to the respondent’s intention to seek prosecutorial discretion that he would not agree to a continuance or a request for prosecutorial discretion as the respondent had approximately two years to seek such relief and failed to do so (Tr. at 10-11). We observe that neither the Board nor the Immigration Judge has jurisdiction over issues of prosecutorial discretion. See Matter of Quintero, 18 I&N Dec. 348, 349-50 (BIA 1982) (stating that deferred action status is a function of DHS’s prosecutorial discretion). Furthermore, we note that the DHS may choose to exercise prosecutorial discretion at any stage of the respondent’s proceedings. On this record, we find that the Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s motion for a continuance.
 
*2 Regarding the respondent’s motion for remand of proceedings based on ineffective assistance of counsel, we will deny such motion. The respondent has not sufficiently complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See Pepaj v. Mukasey, 509 F.3d 725 (6th Cir. 2007); Matter of Rivera-Claros, 21 I&N Dec. 599 (BIA 1996).1 Furthermore, the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim does not prevail as he has not established prejudice. The record does not reflect that the alleged ineffective representation of the respondent’s former counsel affected the final outcome of the respondent’s removal hearing. The respondent made no application for relief other than voluntary departure, which the DHS did not oppose, and the Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s motion to continue. See Matter of Sanchez-Sosa, 25 I&N Dec. 807, 815 (BIA 2012) (recognizing that an alien should not be granted a continuance “as a dilatory tactic to forestall the conclusion of removal proceedings”). To date, the respondent has not provided any evidence to show that he is eligible for any other relief from removal. See Sako v. Gonzales, 434 F.3d 857 (6th Cir. 2006) (successful motion by alien to reopen immigration proceedings due to ineffective assistance of counsel before the Board must meet the Matter of Lozada requirements, and the alien must show that he was prejudiced by the actions or inactions of counsel); Huicochea-Gomez v. INS, 237 F.3d 696 (6th Cir. 2001) (stating that the alien carries the burden of establishing that ineffective assistance of counsel prejudiced him or denied him fundamental fairness in order to prove that he has suffered denial of due process); Denko v. INS, 351 F.3d 717 (6th Cir. 2003) (the alien will succeed on an ineffective of assistance of counsel claim in deportation proceedings if he meets his burden of showing more than mere ineffective assistance of counsel, but assistance which was so ineffective as to have impinged upon the fundamental fairness of the hearing in violation of the fifth amendment due process clause).
 
Finally, the record reflects that the respondent submitted timely proof of having paid the voluntary departure bond. Hence, the period of voluntary departure will be reinstated.
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered:
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s motion to remand is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart the United States, without expense to the Government, within 60 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”). See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b); see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
*3 NOTICE: If the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty as provided by the regulations and the statute and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.
 
WARNING: If the respondent files a motion to reopen or reconsider prior to the expiration of the voluntary departure period set forth above, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated; the period allowed for voluntary departure is not stayed, tolled, or extended. If the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated upon the filing of a motion, the penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(e)(1).
 
WARNING: If, prior to departing the United States, the respondent files any judicial challenge to this administratively final order, such as a petition for review pursuant to section 242 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated, and the alternate order of removal shall immediately take effect. However, if the respondent files a petition for review and then departs the United States within 30 days of such filing, the respondent will not be deemed to have departed under an order of removal if the alien provides to the DHS such evidence of his or her departure that the Immigration and Customs Enforcement Field Office Director of the DHS may require and provides evidence DHS deems sufficient that he or she has remained outside of the United States. The penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply to an alien who files a petition for review, notwithstanding any period of time that he or she remains in the United States while the petition for review is pending. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.260).
 
Patricia A. Cole
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

In Matter of Rivera-Claros, supra, we held that an alien seeking to reopen in absentia proceedings based on her unsuccessful communications with her attorney did not establish exceptional circumstances pursuant to former section 242B(c)(3)(A) of the Act, where she failed to satisfy all of the requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim set out in Matter of Lozada. The applicant in Matter of Rivera-Claros declined to file a bar complaint against her former attorney and the Board found the applicant’s explanation to be inadequate under Lozada. Id. at 603. The Board addressed the importance of the “bar complaint” and provided numerous justifications for its requirement. Id. at 605. Specifically, the Board relies on the disciplinary process of the relevant jurisdiction’s bar as the first, and ordinarily the fastest, means of identifying and correcting possible misconduct. Id. at 604. As the Board stated in Matter of Lozada, this process not only serves to deter meritless claims of ineffective assistance of counsel but also highlights the standards which should be expected of attorneys who represent aliens in immigration proceedings. Id. at 604. The filing of the complaint also increases our confidence in the validity of the particular claim, protects against collusion between the alien and counsel, and helps to prevent unnecessary delay in immigration proceedings, including by reducing the need for an evidentiary hearing on matters that are collateral to the merits of substantive immigration law determinations. Id. at 604-05.

IN RE: JULIO ROSENDO-SERRANO A.K.A. JULIO CESAR SERRANO
File: AXXX XX2 000 - Charlotte, NC
November 10, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Amanda Bethea Keaveny, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Susan Lecker
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
 
APPLICATION: Continuance; administrative closure
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has appealed from an Immigration Judge’s November 17, 2015, decision ordering him removed from the United States to Mexico. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has filed a response in opposition to the respondent’s appeal. The record will be remanded.
 
On July 14, 2015, the respondent admitted the factual allegations in the Notice to Appear and conceded removability (Tr. at 1). The respondent’s attorney requested several continuances to try to obtain agreement with the DHS to terminate or administratively close the proceedings because he had an approved immediate relative petition and was pursuing a provisional unlawful presence waiver from the United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (Tr. at 6-7, 13, 15-17). At a hearing on November 17, 2015, the DHS stated that it would not agree to administrative closure because the respondent is not eligible for a provisional waiver based on his forgery conviction (Tr. at 17). The Immigration Judge stated that he would not grant administrative closure over the DHS’s objection and allowed the respondent’s attorney time off the record to talk to the respondent about either accepting a voluntary departure order or a removal order (Tr. at 19). After consulting with the respondent off the record, the respondent’s attorney stated that the respondent would accept an order of removal and would waive appeal (Tr. at 19).
 
The respondent alleges on appeal that his acceptance of a removal order and waiver of appeal was not knowing and voluntary. He states that his attorney advised him to accept a removal order so that he could appeal the decision and that he told the attorney that he wanted to appeal the Immigration Judge’s decision. He further argues that his prior attorney provided ineffective assistance by waiving his right to appeal, by improperly advising him to plead guilty to forgery, by failing to file for cancellation of removal and failing to pursue parole and a provisional waiver with the DHS.
 
We conclude that the respondent has substantially complied with the requirements for making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel and has stated a valid claim of ineffective assistance. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); Barry v. Gonzales, 445 F.3d 741 (4th Cir. 2006). Further, we find that the respondent’s waiver of appeal was not knowing and intelligent. See Narine v. Holder, 559 F.3d 246 (4th Cir. 2009). He states in his affidavit that he told his attorney he wanted to accept a removal order so that he would not have to waive his appeal rights, and he was not aware that his former attorney told the Immigration Judge that he agreed to waive his right to appeal (Tr. at 19). In addition, his former attorney has filed an affidavit stating that he mistakenly waived the respondent’s appeal rights and that the respondent did not intend to do so. Thus, we accept the respondent’s appeal on certification.
 
*2 The respondent has raised a claim on appeal that the Immigration Judge should have granted his request for administrative closure to allow for processing of his immigrant visa petition and to allow him to pursue a provisional waiver of unlawful presence under section 212(a)(9) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(9). The DHS opposed administrative closure on the grounds that the respondent has a forgery conviction and is not eligible for a provisional waiver (Tr. at 17-18).
 
Therefore, we will remand these proceedings for the parties to be given an opportunity to present further evidence regarding the respondent’s eligibility for administrative closure, and for the Immigration Judge to make findings of fact regarding the respondent’s conviction or analyze his request for administrative closure under the standards outlined in Matter of Avetisyan, 25 I&N Dec. 688 (BIA 2012).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the issuance of a new decision.
 
Patricia A. Cole
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 8188600 (BIA)

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LETICIA NOHEMY CRUZ-VIERA
File: AXXX XX4 965 - Dallas, TX
November 4, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Alanna M. McCoy, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; termination
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge dated May 9, 2016, denying her motion to reopen and to terminate proceedings. The appeal will be dismissed.1
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the Immigration Judge’s determination of credibility. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent was ordered removed on August 4, 2010. On April 21, 2016, she filed a motion requesting sua sponte reopening and termination of her proceedings to allow her to return to El Salvador and apply for the Central American Minors (CAM) Refugee/Parole Program. The Immigration Judge denied the motion and this appeal followed.
 
On appeal, the respondent alleges that the Immigration Judge erred by declining to sua sponte reopen her case. Specifically, the respondent claims that exceptional circumstances apply because (1) the program under which she seeks to apply did not exist at the time she accepted her order of removal and (2) she was young at the time she accepted her order of removal and her attorney did not seek to file relief.2 She further contends that without reopening and termination of these proceedings, she is ineligible for the CAM program.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondent’s case does not warrant sua sponte reopening. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). Sua sponte reopening of proceedings is invoked sparingly and reserved for truly exceptional circumstances. See Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-35 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997) (explaining that the Board’s power to reopen or reconsider cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional situations and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations where enforcing them might result in hardship). While the respondent has presented sympathetic circumstances, her circumstances are not sufficiently different from ordinary hardship so as to qualify as exceptional and trigger the limited authority to reopen proceedings sua sponte. Matter of J-J-, supra.
 
*2 Moreover, the Immigration Judge properly noted that neither the Immigration Court nor the Board have jurisdiction to adjudicate the respondent’s application. We have concluded that administratively final removal proceedings should not be reopened for matters over which neither the Immigration Judge, nor the Board, has jurisdiction. Matter of Yauri, 25 I&N Dec. 103 (BIA 2009). We thus affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen. Accordingly, the following order shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Margaret M O’Herron
FOR THE BOARD

1

The respondent’s request for a stay of removal is denied.

2

The respondent has not made an ineffective assistance of counsel claim against her former counsel, complied with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), or argued that she was eligible for any form of relief at that time.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JULIAN OROZCO-GARCIA
File: AXXX XX2 728 - Orlando, FL
November 3, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Antonio Bugge, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Chelsea Braden
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Guatemala, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision of October 8, 2015. In that decision, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings in which he was ordered removed in absentia on April 1, 2015. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed a brief opposing the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
The Notice of Hearing (NOH) for the April 1, 2015, hearing was mailed to the respondent’s counsel, Antonio Bugge, Esquire, on February 9, 2015.1 The respondent failed to appear at his hearing and an in absentia order was entered on April 1, 2015. The respondent filed a motion to reopen in absentia proceedings on September 22, 2015, alleging that the respondent’s friend did not inform him of his hearing, and further alleging that counsel could not reach the respondent through his friend. The respondent further alleged that he has a United States citizen child and he seeks prosecutorial discretion and cancellation of removal. The Immigration Judge denied the motion on October 8, 2015, noting that the respondent should have contacted counsel and finding that the respondent’s evidence did not establish paternity of the child he claimed is a qualifying relative for cancellation of removal.
 
Notice to counsel constitutes notice to the respondent. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.26(c)(2); see also Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a). Receipt of notice by counsel is sufficient to deny a motion to reopen based on a lack of notice. See Anin v. Reno, 188 F.3d 1273, 1277 (11th Cir. 1999) (per curiam). The respondent also failed to demonstrate that “exceptional circumstances” prevented him from appearing at the April 1, 2015, hearing. The failure in communication between the respondent and his counsel has not been shown to be an exceptional circumstance beyond his control and sufficient to excuse his failure to appear at his scheduled hearing. See section 240(e)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(e)(1); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(4)(iii)(A)(1). In view of these circumstances, the Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. See generally Vukmirovic v. Holder, 640 F.3d 977, 978-79 (9th Cir. 2011) (alien’s failure to know about removal hearing because he had moved without advising his lawyer or immigration court of his whereabouts did not constitute exceptional circumstances).
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent alleges that he and counsel were not able to communicate with one another because the respondent speaks neither Spanish nor English, and only speaks a native Guatemalan dialect (Respondent’s Brief, page 3). However, the respondent did not raise this in his motion to reopen. We further note that the respondent has not alleged or shown ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). For ineffective assistance to serve as an exceptional circumstance, an alien must prove counsel’s deficient performance resulted in prejudice and satisfy certain procedural requirements. See Dakane v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 371 F.3d 771, 775-76 (11th Cir. 2004) (per curiam). Here, while the respondent contends that he would seek cancellation of removal, as the Immigration Judge noted, he has not established that he has a qualifying relative; nor has he provided the requisite application (Form EOIR 42B). The respondent has not demonstrated that he is prima facie eligible for any relief, and thus he has failed to establish he suffered any prejudice, even had there been ineffective representation. See Dakane v. U.S. Attorney Gen., supra, at 1274 (“Prejudice exists when the performance of counsel is so inadequate that there is a reasonable probability that but for the attorney’s error, the outcome of the proceedings would have been different”).
 
The formal rescission requirements need not be satisfied in certain circumstances where the respondent is eligible for relief that was unavailable at the time of the in absentia hearing and did not receive oral warnings in his native language regarding the bar to future discretionary relief that arises from a failure to appear. See generally Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998). In such cases, the motion to reopen is subject to the 90-day filing deadline set forth at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3). The respondent’s in absentia removal order was entered on April 1, 2015. The September 22, 2015, motion was thus past the 90-day deadline for the filing of a motion to reopen seeking new relief. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b). Consequently, the motion is untimely. Moreover, as noted above, the respondent has not established prima facie eligibility for any relief.
 
For the foregoing reasons, and those articulated by the Immigration Judge in his decision, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision, pursuant to our authority at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(5).
 
*3 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

The agency database indicates that a Form EOIR-28 from Antonio Bugge, Esquire, was filed on August 29, 2014.

Given the totality of circumstances and evidence presented, including the Department of Homeland Security’s lack of response or opposition to the pending motion, and the evidence reflecting that former counsel(s)’ representation may have affected the outcome of the respondent’s proceedings, we will reopen the proceedings under the provisions of 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a), and will remand the record to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings on the respondent’s applications for relief. 2016 WL 7032763 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALFREDO TREJO BALLEZA
File: AXXX XX8 675 - Charlotte, NC
October 24, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Mark J. Deyine, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Susan Lecker
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Motion to reopen
 
*1 This matter is before us on certification from the Immigration Judge, upon issuance of his decision of January 5, 2016. The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision denying his motion to reopen proceedings. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposes the appeal. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact, including credibility findings, for clear error. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); see also Matter of J-Y-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 260 (BIA 2007); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). We review questions of law, discretion, or judgment, and all other issues de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that he received ineffective assistance of counsel and was prejudiced by his former attorney, who did not present his cancellation of removal application nor his request for prosecutorial discretion (Respondent’s Brief at 11-21). The respondent requests a remand to present his application for cancellation of removal and request for prosecutorial discretion (Respondent’s Brief at 22).
 
The record shows that the respondent has supported his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel with the appropriate documentation required under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). However, the respondent must also demonstrate that he was substantially prejudiced by the ineffective assistance. See Figeroa v. INS, 886 F.2d 76, 78 (4th Cir. 1989) (stating that to prevail on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien must show not only ineffective representation, but also prejudice to him or her which occurred as a result of that ineffectiveness).
 
The respondent has not demonstrated that he is prima facie eligible for cancellation of removal (I.J. at 1). To date, the respondent has not submitted his application for cancellation of removal or sufficient supporting documentation to establish his eligibility for said relief. See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 506 (BIA 1980) (assertions of counsel are not evidence). Specifically, as the Immigration Judge noted, the respondent has not submitted evidence of his qualifying relatives nor provided proof of his continuous physical presence in the United States since 2004 (I.J. at 1). Thus, the respondent has not established that he is eligible for cancellation of removal.1
 
*2 In light of the foregoing, we do not find that the ineffective assistant of counsel described by the respondent established prejudice such that the outcome of his proceedings would have been different but for the ineffective assistance. Therefore, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

Any request for a favorable exercise of prosecutorial discretion would have to be addressed to the DHS.

2016 WL 7032785 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOHN ADRIAN MALDONADO-GUINANZACA
File: AXXX XX3 454 - New Orleans, LA
October 18, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Pablo Fernandez-Herrera, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Charlotte Marquez
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Motion to reopen
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Ecuador, appeals from the decision of the Immigration Judge dated December 5, 2014, denying the respondent’s motion to reopen to rescind the in absentia removal order that was entered on June 2, 2014. See section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A). The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposes the respondent’s appeal. The record will be remanded.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We are unable to meaningfully review the December 5, 2014, decision of the Immigration Judge as that order consists of a two-sentence conclusion of law without any findings of fact or analysis (Resp. Br. at 7-8). See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (d)(3)(iv); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462, 463-65 (BIA 2002) (Immigration Judges must include in their decisions clear and complete findings of fact that are supported by the record and comply with controlling law). The Immigration Judge determined that the respondent had not met his burden to establish ineffective assistance of his former counsel in order to toll the filing deadline for a motion to reopen. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); see also Matter of Compean, Bangaly, J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009).
 
However, the Immigration Judge did not render any findings regarding what requirements of Matter Lozada, supra, for an ineffective assistance claim, have or have not been met.1 In this regard, there is evidence of record that the respondent submitted an affidavit setting forth his agreement with his former counsel, that the respondent has informed his former counsel of the allegations of ineffective assistance through a complaint with a state bar, and the respondent has submitted a formal complaint against his former counsel with the appropriate disciplinary authority (Resp. Br. at 12; DHS Br. at 2-4).2 Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639. Moreover, the Immigration Judge should determine whether the respondent has met his burden to establish that his former counsel’s ineffective assistance resulted in actual prejudice (Resp. Br. at 12-13). Id. at 640. Additionally, if the Immigration Judge finds that the ineffective assistance of counsel claim has been established, he should then determine if equitable tolling applies. Therefore, the record must be remanded for the preparation of a decision by the Immigration Judge that will permit meaningful appellate review. By remanding the record, the Board does not express any opinion on the final outcome of this matter.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Michael J. Creppy
FOR THE BOARD

Holder, 741 F.3d 644, 647 (5th Cir. 2014) (finding an alien’s argument that strict compliance with the Lozada requirements is not necessary was without merit and distinguished the Fifth Circuit’s precedent decisions from those of the Ninth Circuit that adopted a different approach in applying the Lozada requirements, where the Ninth Circuit hears ineffective assistance of counsel claims even when a petitioner has arguably failed to comply with the Lozada requirements).

2

The respondent claims that his former counsel was Dalbir Singh, Esquire, but his affidavit indicates that the only individual he talked with associated with the law firm was a Ms. Veronica Piedra. The respondent has not identified if Ms. Piedra is a licensed attorney. See Motion to Re-Open Removal Proceedings (12/02/14), Affidavit of John Adrian Maldonado-Guinanzaca (11/25/14).

KeyCite Yellow Flag - Negative Treatment
  Remanded July 27, 2017
2016 WL 7032796 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALMA SUSANA CORTEZ MENDOZA
File: AXXX XX9 975 - Phoenix, AZ
October 14, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ray A. Ybarra Maldonado, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
 
APPLICATION: Motion to administratively close proceedings
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from the April 24, 2015, decision of the Immigration Judge denying her motion to administratively close proceedings.1 The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has not filed a response. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3).
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge’s discretionary decision to deny the respondent’s request for administrative closure. See Matter of Avetisyan, 25 I&N Dec. 688, 696 (BIA 2012) (outlining factors for evaluating whether administrative closure is warranted, including the likelihood of success on any underlying requests for relief).2 We observe that administrative closure is used to temporarily remove a case from an Immigration Judge’s active calendar or from the Board’s docket. See Matter of Gutierrez, 21 I&N Dec. 479, 480 (BIA 1996); see also Matter of Avetisyan, supra, at 692. The administrative closure of a case does not result in a final order. It is merely an administrative convenience that allows cases to be removed from the calendar in appropriate situations, subject to being recalendared at a later date. See Matter of Amico, 19 I&N Dec. 652, 654 n.1 (BIA 1988).
 
In the present case, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to administratively close proceedings because she had no apparent relief available to her other than voluntary departure. The DHS opposed administrative closure in this case, and the DHS Office of the Chief Counsel had declined to exercise prosecutorial discretion (I.J. at 6; Tr. at 46). While the respondent argues that she is eligible for prosecutorial discretion, the DHS has sole authority to exercise prosecutorial discretion in this matter (Respondent’s Br. at 12-13). Neither the Board nor the Immigration Judge has jurisdiction over issues of prosecutorial discretion. See Matter of Quintero, 18 I&N Dec. 348, 349-50 (BIA 1982).
 
The respondent also contends that her case should be administratively closed in light of pending litigation in Puente Arizona v. Arpaio, 76 F.Supp.3d 833 (D. Ariz. 2015), or in the alternative, be held in abeyance. On January 5, 2015, the District Court for Arizona found that Arizona’s identity theft laws were facially preempted by federal immigration policy and granted the plaintiffs--an immigrant advocacy organization, individual “unauthorized aliens,” and taxpayers of Maricopa County--a preliminary injunction preventing the Arizona government defendants from enforcing the challenged provisions. Id. at 869. The respondent argues that a favorable decision in this case could result in the vacation of her conviction, where she was convicted of criminal impersonation in Arizona (Respondent’s Br. at 7; Exhs. 4-5). After the respondent submitted her brief on August 26, 2015, however, the Ninth Circuit reversed the district court’s holding that the laws are likely facially preempted and vacated the preliminary injunction. Puente Arizona v. Arpaio, 821 F.3d 1098 (9th Cir. 2016). As such, the respondent’s argument on this issue is moot.
 
*2 Finally, to the extent the respondent asserts that her due process rights were violated in the proceedings below, we are unpersuaded. See Cruz Rendon v. Holder, 603 F.3d 1104 (9th Cir. 2010) (holding that in order to prevail on a due process claim that she was denied a full and fair removal hearing, an alien must demonstrate that the challenged proceeding was so fundamentally unfair that she was prevented from reasonably presenting her case; and that she suffered prejudice, such that the outcome of the proceeding may have been affected by the alleged violation). The respondent contends that the Immigration Judge acted in a prosecutorial manner rather than remaining neutral, and that she was questioned by the Immigration Judge, without the presence of counsel, despite appearing in court under the belief that she would be represented (Respondent’s Br. at 4-5, 11).
 
We do not find a basis to conclude that the Immigration Judge’s management of the record establishes a due process violation. We have reviewed the record and find no basis to conclude that the Immigration Judge’s questioning or comments in any way exceeded judicial bounds. An Immigration Judge has the authority to “““interrogate, examine, and cross-examine the alien” in removal proceedings. See section 240(b)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(1); see generally Matter of Rhee, 16 I&N Dec. 607 (BIA 1978).
 
Furthermore, to the extent that the respondent made statements without the presence of counsel, such as admitting to her convictions, the record supports those statements, and the respondent has not argued otherwise (Tr. at 19-21 (Mar. 8, 2013); Exhs. 4-5). Insofar as the respondent claims that the counsel she hired did not properly represent her, the record does not reflect that she was in fact represented at the time of her final hearing; instead, it reflects that the court granted a motion to withdraw from her former counsel, but no Notice of Appearance on her behalf was filed subsequently by another representative until her appeal to the Board (Exhs. 8-9). The respondent has also not complied with the requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, or demonstrated that she suffered resultant prejudice.3 Overall, we conclude that remanded proceedings are not warranted on this basis.
 
Based on the foregoing, the discretionary benefit of administrative closure was not appropriate in this case, and the motion to administrative close was properly denied. See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, supra, at 505 (once the DHS institutes proceedings, an Immigration Judge must order an alien deported if the evidence supports the charge); Matter of Avetisyan, supra, at 696. The respondent provided evidence that she paid the required voluntary departure bond; therefore, we will reinstate the period of voluntary departure. Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
*3 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart the United States, without expense to the Government, within 60 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the DHS. See section 240B(b) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b); see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty as provided by the regulations and the statute and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.
 
WARNING: If the respondent files a motion to reopen or reconsider prior to the expiration of the voluntary departure period set forth above, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated; the period allowed for voluntary departure is not stayed, tolled, or extended. If the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated upon the filing of a motion, the penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(e)(1).
 
WARNING: If, prior to departing the United States, the respondent files any judicial challenge to this administratively final order, such as a petition for review pursuant to section 242 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated, and the alternate order of removal shall immediately take effect. However, if the respondent files a petition for review and then departs the United States within 30 days of such filing, the respondent will not be deemed to have departed under an order of removal if she provides to the DHS such evidence of her departure that the Immigration and Customs Enforcement Field Office Director of the DHS may require and provides evidence DHS deems sufficient that she has remained outside of the United States. The penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply to an alien who files a petition for review, notwithstanding any period of time that he or she remains in the United States while the petition for review is pending. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(i).
 
*4 Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

At the final hearing on April 24, 2015, the Immigration Judge orally denied the respondent’s motion for administrative closure. In an oral decision and form order issued the same day, the Immigration Judge also granted post-conclusion voluntary departure to the respondent.

2

In Matter of Avetisyan, the Board held that, in determining whether administrative closure of proceedings is appropriate, an Immigration Judge or the Board should weigh all the relevant factors presented in the case. These factors include, but are not limited to: (1) the reason administrative closure is sought; (2) the basis for any opposition to administrative closure; (3) the likelihood the respondent will succeed on any petition, application, or other action he or she is pursuing outside of removal proceedings; (4) the anticipated duration of the closure; (5) the responsibility of either party, if any, in contributing to any current or anticipated delay; and (6) the ultimate outcome of removal proceedings (for example, termination of the proceedings or entry of a removal order) when the case is recalendared before the Immigration Judge or the appeal is reinstated before the Board. Id. at 695.

3

Specifically, the respondent has not presented an affidavit setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with her prior counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations the prior counsel did or did not make, the respondent does not indicate that her prior counsel was informed of the allegations leveled against him or her and has been given an opportunity to respond, and the respondent does not indicate whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988). Additionally, because the respondent has not shown that, but for her prior counsel’s alleged ineffective assistance of counsel, she would have obtained post-conviction relief, she has not demonstrated that she was prejudiced by her prior counsel’s ineffective assistance.
