2016 WL 4976650 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LILIA JUDITH RAMIREZ-HERNANDEZ
File: AXXX XX2 004 - El Paso, TX
August 3, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Andres J. Ortiz, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Guatemala, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated February 10, 2016, denying her motion to reconsider the denial of her motion to reopen. The respondent had previously been ordered removed in absentia on December 13, 2005, for her failure to appear for her hearing. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has filed no reply to the appeal. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
The Notice to Appear (“NTA”) was personally served on the respondent on June 18, 2005, and the NTA indicates that the respondent was provided oral notice in the Spanish language of the consequences of a failure to appear (I.J. at 1; Exh. 1). The NTA also reflects that the respondent was informed of, among other things, the statutory address obligations associated with removal proceedings and of the consequences of failing to provide a current address.
 
Two Notices of Hearing (“NOH”) dated August 10, 2005, for a December 13, 2005, hearing were mailed to the Redwood, CA address reflected in the respondent’s NTA, and both were returned by the postal service (I.J. at 1). The respondent failed to appear for that hearing. Service by mail is sufficient if the record contains proof of attempted delivery to the last address provided by the alien in accordance with section 239(a)(1)(F) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(a)(1)(F). Section 239(c) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(c). As notice was properly provided, and as the respondent failed to appear for her hearing, the Immigration Judge properly issued an in absentia order of removal.
 
The respondent admits that she never resided at the Redwood, CA address, and further admits that she never filed any corrected address with the Immigration Court. See Matter of M-R-A-, 24 I&N Dec. 665, 674-75 (BIA 2008) (stating that an alien must comply with the statutory responsibility to provide current address information). As the respondent has not established that she complied with her duty to inform the Immigration Court, within 5 days, of a proper address, she is not entitled to rescission of the order of removal. See section 240 (b)(5)(C)(ii); 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(ii). See also Gomez-Palacios v. Holder, 560 F.3d 354, 360 (5th Cir. 2009) (an alien’s failure to receive actual notice of a removal hearing due to his neglect of his obligation to keep the immigration court apprised of his current mailing address does not mean that the alien did not receive notice).
 
*2 We find without merit the respondent’s contention that she was not provided oral advisements in the Spanish language. The NTA was personally served on the respondent and the NTA reflects that the respondent was informed of, among other things, the statutory address obligations associated with removal proceedings and of the consequences of failing to provide a current address (Exh. 1). The NTA specifically informed the respondent that if she failed to provide updated contact information “then the Government shall not be required to provide you with written notice of your hearing,” and that if she failed to appear at her hearing, which could be held at “any date and time later directed by the Immigration Court, a removal order may be made by the immigration judge in your absence, and you may be arrested and detained by the INS.” Exh. 1. Even accepting as true the respondent’s averments that Spanish oral advisals were not provided, current law does not require that the NTA be in any language other than English to constitute effective notice. See section 239 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229. Moreover, the requirement of oral notice in an alien’s native language is only necessary to trigger the sanctions in section 240(b)(7) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(7), which limits an alien’s eligibility for discretionary relief for failure to appear at removal proceedings. Consequently, the NTA and NOH complied with statutory requirements. See Gomez-Palacios v. Holder, supra.
 
The respondent argues that prior counsel, who filed the motion to reopen, failed to raise the issues the respondent raises in her motion to reconsider. However, the respondent has not specifically alleged ineffective assistance of counsel; nor has she complied with any of the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See also Mai v. Gonzales, 473 F.3d 162, 165 (5th Cir. 2006); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). The respondent has not established that her former counsel did not employ a reasonable tactical decision, and as such is not amenable to a finding that the attorney was ineffective. See Strickland v. Washington, 466 U.S. 668, 681 (1984) (noting that counsel’s informed strategic decisions must be respected if they are based on professional judgment). Subsequent dissatisfaction with a strategic decision of counsel is not grounds for reopening. Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309, 310 (BIA 1998).
 
Nor is reopening warranted under Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998) as the motion was untimely for purposes of new relief. Id.
 
*3 Pursuant to our holding in Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997), the power to reopen sua sponte is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations where enforcing them might result in hardship. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). While the respondent has presented some positive factors in support of sua sponte reopening, these factors either individually or in the aggregate do not trigger the limited authority to reopen proceedings sua sponte. Indeed, many aliens who have been present in the United States for a period of years have accumulated favorable equities subsequent to being ordered removed. See Matter of Beckford, 22 I&N Dec. 1216 (BIA 2000) (burden is on the movant to show exceptional circumstances). In reaching this conclusion we note that the respondent did not diligently pursue her immigration status as she first filed her motion to reopen proceedings almost 10 years after being ordered removed in absentia, and 2 years after discovering her in absentia order.
 
For the foregoing reasons, and those articulated by the Immigration Judge in his decision, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision, pursuant to our authority at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(5).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 4976650 (BIA)
End of Document

2016 WL 4976664 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: WILSON ANTONIO RIVERA-TORRES
File: AXXX XX1 038 - San Antonio, TX
August 1, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Charlie E. Carrillo, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision of July 15, 2015. In that decision, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s May 28, 2015, motion to reopen proceedings in which he was ordered removed in absentia on October 24, 2006. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not filed a response to the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
The Notice of Hearing (NOH) for the October 24, 2006, hearing was mailed to the respondent’s former counsel, Jose Guerrero, Esquire, on September 18, 2006 (I.J. at 2; Exh. 2). The respondent failed to appear at his hearing and an in absentia order was entered on October 24, 2006. The respondent filed a motion to reopen in absentia proceedings through present counsel on May 28, 2015, alleging that he did not receive notice of his hearing. The motion further alleged that prior counsel failed to inform the respondent of his hearing and the respondent noted that he had attempted to file a change of venue request which was rejected by the Immigration Court. The Immigration Judge denied the motion, finding the motion based upon exceptional circumstances was untimely and finding reopening not warranted based upon lack of notice because notice to counsel constitutes notice to the respondent. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.26(c)(2); see also Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a).
 
Contrary to the respondent’s contention in his motion to reopen, the filing of a Form EOIR-28 constitutes an entrance of appearance for all proceedings, including removal and bond. There is no “limited” appearance of counsel in immigration proceedings. See Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377 (BIA 1986). The respondent has not contested the Immigration Judge’s finding in this regard (I.J. at 3), and therefore the issue is deemed abandoned. See Matter of Edwards, 20 I&N Dec. 191, 196-197 n.4 (BIA 1990) (noting that issues not addressed on appeal are deemed waived).
 
On appeal, the respondent argues, inter alia, that it was procedural error not to send the rejection notice of the respondent’s change of venue request to the respondent. However, the record indicates that the rejection notice was indeed mailed to the respondent at the address he provided in the request. Moreover, the fact that the respondent attempted to file a change of venue does not excuse the respondent’s failure to appear at his October 24, 2006, hearing. It is well established that the mere filing of a motion for a change of venue does not relieve the respondent from his responsibility to appear, and unless the Immigration Judge grants the motion, the respondent remains obligated to appear at the appointed date and time. See Matter of Patel, 19 I&N Dec. 260, 262 (BIA 1985), aff’d, 803 F.2d 804 (5th Cir. 1986) (the submission of a motion for a continuance does not relieve an alien or his attorney of the responsibility to attend a deportation hearing of which they have been given notice). Nor has the respondent related how rejection of his request to change venue is relevant to whether prior counsel received notice of the hearing.
 
*2 The respondent also maintains on appeal that the Immigration Judge failed to consider equitable tolling. The respondent did not expressly argue in his motion to reopen before the Immigration Judge that his motion should be equitably tolled and thus the Immigration Judge did not err in not addressing this issue. We further note that even if the immigration statutes are subject to equitable tolling, the respondent has failed to show that such tolling would apply here. See Mata v. Lynch, __ U.S. __, 135 S.Ct. 2150, 2156 & n. 3 (2015) (expressing no opinion as to whether the immigration statutes allow equitable tolling). Specifically, the respondent admits that he became aware of his 2006 in absentia order when he was removed in 2010 (Respondent’s Brief, page 5). Consequently, contrary to the respondent’s contention on appeal, we find that he has not established due diligence, because he has not explained why he waited approximately 5 years after discovering his in absentia order to file a motion to reopen. See Medina-Herrera v. Gonzales, 162 Fed. Appx. 329 (5th Cir. 2006) (unpublished) (although generally the determination of whether party has exercised due diligence for purposes of equitable tolling requires finding of fact, Board of Immigration Appeals may determine as a matter of law that party failed to exercise due diligence, where facts on the record are undisputed and the result is inarguable). See also Mendoza-Cortez v. Lynch, ___ F.3d ___, No. 15-60332, 2016 WL 3383417, at 1 (5th Cir. June 17, 2016) (even if the time period for filing a motion to reopen may be equitably tolled, alien has not demonstrated that she would be entitled to equitable tolling because she has not shown that she acted with due diligence).
 
An alien may raise ineffective assistance of counsel as a ground for reopening a deportation case “in ‘egregious circumstances.”DD’ Mai v. Gonzales, 473 F.3d 162, 165 (5th Cir. 2006) (quoting Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988). However, as a procedural prerequisite for the Board or Immigration Judge to consider a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien must provide (1) his own affidavit attesting to the facts, including a statement of the terms of the attorney-client agreement; (2) evidence that counsel was informed of the ineffectiveness allegations and allowed an opportunity to respond; and (3) evidence of a disciplinary filing against the offending attorney or an explanation as to why a grievance was not filed.
 
Here, the respondent has not shown that he has fully complied with the Lozada requirements. See Lara v. Trominski, 216 F.3d 487, 497-98 (5th Cir. 2000) (finding that the Board does not abuse its discretion by requiring compliance with Lozada). First, while the respondent proffered statements regarding his fear of returning to El Salvador (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, Tab O), none outline the terms of the attorney-client agreement he had with his former counsel. Second, while the respondent has proffered evidence on appeal that he communicated with former counsel requesting information, he has not shown that his former counsel has been informed of the allegations of ineffective assistance. Third, while the respondent avers that a complaint against his former counsel has been drafted, he has not shown that the complaint was in fact filed with the state bar. Nor has the respondent explained why the grievance has not been filed. See Hernandez-Ortez v. Holder, 741 F.3d 644, 648 (5th Cir. 2014) (finding that Lozada makes clear that the second requirement is only met when counsel is informed of the charge before allegations of ineffective assistance are presented to the Board and finding the alien did not offer sufficient proof to show that his attorney was informed or had a reasonable opportunity to respond before the allegations were presented in his appeal brief to the Board; nor did the alien offer any adequate reason that his failure to follow the rules should be excused).
 
*3 While the Immigration Judge did not expressly address the respondent’s ineffective assistance claim, he found the motion based upon exceptional circumstances to be untimely. Even if there was error by the Immigration Judge, we find that the respondent has failed to show prejudice therefrom. Specifically, the respondent has not established that he is entitled to equitable tolling, and he has not complied with the Lozada procedural requirements, and thus we find the respondent has failed to show prejudice stemming from the Immigration Judge’s failure to address the ineffective assistance claim. See Ogunfuye v. Holder, 610 F.3d 303, 306-07 (5th Cir. 2010) (explaining that the respondent must make a showing of substantial prejudice before he can prevail on a due process claim); De Zavala v. Ashcroft, 385 F.3d 879, 883 (5th Cir. 2004) (same). See also Chen v. Gonzales, 157 Fed. Appx. 739, 741 (5th Cir. 2005) (unpublished) (finding that once the Immigration Judge held that the alien’s motion, filed more than four years after the 1999 removal order, was untimely, his discussion of the existence vel non of exceptional circumstances was unnecessary, and any error he may have committed on that score is therefore harmless).
 
Moreover, regarding the respondent’s claimed fear of persecution or torture in El Salvador, the regulations provide specific procedures through which an alien subject to reinstatement may pursue such a claim.1 See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1208.31, 1241.8(e) (describing procedures for those subject to reinstatement who expresses fear of returning to country of removal); Matter of W-C-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 118, 123 (BIA 2007) (denying motion to remand, noting that procedures are in place for those subject to reinstatement who claimed a fear of returning to country of removal); see also 8 C.F.R. § 1241.8(a)(1) (an immigration officer is required to determine whether “the alien is in fact an alien ...”). The respondent may pursue these applications in such proceedings.
 
Contrary to the Immigration Judge’s finding otherwise, the respondent is not barred by section 241(a)(5) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1231(a)(5), from filing a motion to reopen in absentia proceedings when alleging a lack of notice. See Matter of Bulnes, 25 I&N Dec. 57 (BIA 2009) (Board held that an alien’s departure from the United States while under an outstanding order of deportation or removal issued in absentia does not deprive the Immigration Judge of jurisdiction to entertain a motion to reopen to rescind the order if the motion is premised on lack of notice). Given our findings above, however, we find no prejudicial error.
 
*4 Finally, as noted above, the respondent proffers evidence on appeal. Even if we were to construe the respondent’s additional evidence as a motion to remand, the respondent has not shown that this evidence is new or previously unavailable, or that it could possibly alter the decision in this case. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992) (holding that a party who seeks a remand to pursue relief bears a “heavy burden” of proving that if proceedings before the Immigration Judge were reopened, with all the attendant delays, the new evidence would likely change the result in the case). Therefore, we find that a remand is not warranted.
 
For the foregoing reasons, and those articulated by the Immigration Judge in his decision, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision, pursuant to our authority at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(5).
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

The respondent averred in his motion to reopen that the 2006 removal order has been reinstated, and he has been placed in “Withholding Only” proceedings in Baltimore Immigration Court (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, pages 5, 11).

KeyCite Yellow Flag - Negative Treatment
  Review Granted, Cause Remanded by Sylejmani v. Sessions, 5th Cir., April 12, 2018
2016 WL 4976727 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DRIN SYLEJMANI
File: AXXX XX2 016 - Dallas, TX
July 22, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
David Swaim, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 The respondent’s motion to reopen is denied. This case was last before the Board on October 23, 2015, when the Board, finding that the respondent knowingly and voluntarily waived his right to appeal, dismissed the respondent’s appeal for lack of jurisdiction. On April 18, 2016, the respondent filed the present motion. However, the motion is untimely filed and has not been shown to qualify for any exception to the time limitations imposed by law on motions to reopen removal proceedings. See section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c).
 
Moreover, we decline to equitably toll the applicable time limits based on the respondent’s alleged ineffective assistance of former counsel(s) claim. The evidence proffered along with the respondent’s motion does not sufficiently demonstrate that further hearings on his asserted eligibility for adjustment of status are warranted. See also Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 472-74 (BIA 1992) (recognizing that the respondent bears a “heavy burden” of demonstrating that if proceedings before the Immigration Judge were reopened, with all the attendant delays, the new evidence would likely change the result in the case). To date, only minimal evidence of the bona fides of the respondent’s current marriage, which was entered into during the pendency of these removal proceedings, has been proffered and there is no evidence that the respondent’s I-130 has been approved.
 
On this record we do not find that the respondent has sufficiently demonstrated patent error or prejudice stemming from former counsel(s) alleged deficient performance. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); see also Goonsuwan v. Ashcroft, 252 F.3d 383, 385 n.2 (5th Cir. 2001) (noting that to establish ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien must demonstrate that counsel’s performance resulted in substantial prejudice); Miranda-Lores v. INS, 17 F. 3d 84, 85 (5th Cir. 1994) (proving prejudice requires that the petitioner make a prima facie showing that, absent counsel’s deficient performance, he would have been entitled to the relief the sought). Nor are we persuaded that reconsideration of the prior Board decision or reopening of these proceedings is warranted in the exercise of discretion under the Board’s sua sponte authority. Accordingly, the respondent’s motion is denied as untimely filed.
 
*2 Molly Kendall-Clark
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 4976727 (BIA)
End of Document

© 2020 Thomson Reuters. No claim to original U.S. Government Works.






2016 WL 4976722 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CARLA ESTEFANIA MARTIN-MEJIA
File: AXXX XX2 764 - Atlanta, GA
July 22, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Damian J. Miklosovic, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Wylly Jordan
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated October 15, 2014, denying her motion to reopen. The respondent had previously been ordered removed in absentia for her failure to appear for the hearing on March 19, 2002. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to credibility determinations and the likelihood of future events, under a “““clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of Z-Z-O-, 26 I&N Dec. 586 (BIA 2015). We review all other issues, including questions of law, judgment, or discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The record reflects that on January 10, 1999, the respondent’s mother was served with the Notice to Appear on behalf of the respondent, who was a minor at the time (Exhs. 1, 3). See Matter of Gomez-Gomez, 23 I&N Dec. 522 (BIA 2002) (finding that the Service had met its burden of proving a minor respondent’s removability where the father of an 8-year-old respondent signed the child’s Notice to Appear and furnished data on the Form I-213). The respondent’s mother received oral and written notice in the Spanish language of the consequences of a failure to appear and of the requirement to notify the Immigration Court immediately by using Form EOIR-33 whenever she changed her address or telephone number during the proceeding (Exh. 1). The respondent did not appear for the hearing on March 19, 2002, and the Immigration Judge issued an in absentia order of removal.
 
An alien may not be entitled to receive notice of her hearing if she does not provide an address at which she may be contacted during proceedings. See section 240(b)(5)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(B) (eliminating the notice requirement where the alien fails to provide the required contact information); see also Matter of M-R-A-, 24 I&N Dec. 665, 674-75 (BIA 2008) (stating that an alien must comply with the statutory responsibility to provide current address information).
 
Upon de novo review, we affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. He acknowledged the respondent’s contention in her motion to reopen that she was eight years old and living with her mother when the notice of hearing was issued, and her mother never told her about a letter or an immigration hearing (I.J. Dec. at 2; Exh. 2). The notice of hearing was sent to the respondent’s last known address, but it was returned to the Immigration Court as undeliverable, stamped with the notation “Not Deliverable as Addressed, Unable to Forward.” We agree with the Immigration Judge that this evidence indicates either the address provided by the respondent’s mother was inaccurate, or the respondent and her mother moved without notifying the court (I.J. Dec. at 2-3; Exh. 3). Consequently, the Immigration Judge correctly determined that the respondent received proper notice of her hearing. See United States v. Zelaya, 293 F.3d 1294, 1298 (11th Cir. 2012) (holding that mailing a notice to the last known address is sufficient to satisfy the government’s duty to provide an alien with notice of a removal hearing).
 
*2 On appeal the respondent requests that her case be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further consideration. However, the respondent has not submitted any evidence that she is eligible to apply for any relief, nor has she submitted any applications for relief. To the extent the respondent’s appeal implies that the attorney who assisted her in filing a motion to reopen provided ineffective assistance of counsel, she has failed to establish such a claim. The respondent has not complied with the procedural requirements for filing an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as enunciated in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Consequently, the respondent has not submitted any evidence to address the issue further.
 
The respondent also states that she was the victim of abuse at different times in her life, and she is distressed at the prospect of returning to Honduras with her children, as crime and violence are rampant there. The time and number limits for motions to reopen do not apply to a motion to reopen proceedings if the basis of the motion is to apply or reapply for asylum or withholding of removal based on changed country conditions or changed circumstances arising in the country of nationality, if such evidence is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing. 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(ii); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(3)(ii); 1003.23(b)(4)(i); see also Matter of J-G-, 26 I&N Dec. 161 (BIA 2013) (holding that proceedings conducted in absentia may be reopened where the alien seeks asylum and withholding of removal based on changed country conditions). However, the respondent has not submitted an asylum application, she does not allege or discuss any changed country conditions in Honduras to support her claim, and she has not presented any evidence of a change in country conditions which is material to her claim for asylum. Therefore, the respondent is not exempt from the 90-day time limitation because she has not shown that it is based on changed country conditions arising in Honduras since the date of her removal order. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(ii) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(i).
 
We further find no basis for exercising our sua sponte authority to reopen proceedings in this case. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); see also Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (stating that the Board’s power to reopen cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations). Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
*3 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Brian M. O’Leary
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 4976722 (BIA)
End of Document

2016 WL 4976731 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JUAN C. PACHECO VILLATORO
File: AXXX XX0 302 - Baltimore, MD
July 21, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
John J. Garzon, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s October 22, 2014, decision ordering the respondent removed from the United States to El Salvador. The Immigration Judge’s decision is affirmed, pursuant to this Board’s authority under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(5), for the reasons set forth therein, and the respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
Under 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3(i), (ii), the Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding pure questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts. On appeal, the respondent contends that his prior counsel rendered ineffective assistance and that the Immigration Judge should have continued the respondent’s October 22, 2014, hearing based upon prior counsel’s stated desire to withdraw from representing the respondent.
 
The averments of respondent’s counsel on appeal that are not present in the record are not evidence. See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 506 (BIA 1980) (statements of counsel in a brief are not evidence). Furthermore, the respondent’s claim, through counsel, that prior counsel rendered ineffective assistance has not been established. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988) (setting forth the general criteria for establishing a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel).
 
Finally, prior counsel’s effort to withdraw, standing alone did not establish good cause for a continuance of the respondent’s removal proceeding pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29 where the record establishes that the respondent and prior counsel were aware of the scheduled hearing and the obligation to be prepared to proceed with any application for relief before the Immigration Judge (I.J. at 2-3; Tr. at 5-9, 11-22). In this regard, prior counsel indicated on the record that pursuing voluntary departure and withholding of removal were also being considered but were not pursued. Also, at the final hearing, respondent’s prior counsel informed the Immigration Judge that the respondent was aware of his options. Thus, good cause to continue the respondent’s hearing did not exist as the respondent had notice of the timing and purpose of the hearing and did not comply with the deadline for submission of applications set by the Immigration Judge, thereby abandoning any application for relief from removal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.31(c). On appeal, the respondent does not indicate what relief he is seeking or what he was prevented from presenting to the Immigration Court. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Ana Mann
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 4976731 (BIA)
End of Document

2016 WL 4120561 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NELY YOHANA PENA-GARCIA GAVI SULEYMA GALVEZ-PENA
Files: AXXX XX0 919 - Charlotte, NC
AXXX XX0 920
July 13, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Kathryn E. Shepherd, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Christopher Coxe
Deputy Chief Counsel
*1 The respondents are natives and citizens of Guatemala who were placed in removal proceedings. Represented by counsel, they requested an order of removal, and did not request any relief from removal. On April 5, 2016, the Immigration Judge issued an order of removal against the respondents. The order indicated that no other application for relief was requested, and that the appeal was waived.
 
The respondents did not file a timely Notice of Appeal. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.38 (Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26) must be filed within 30 calendar days of an Immigration Judge’s oral decision or the mailing of a written decision). The Department of Homeland Security represents that the respondents were detained on or about May 16, 2014. Thereafter, on May 24, 2016, the respondents filed a Notice of Appeal, as well as a request for a stay. Since the Notice of Appeal was filed after expiration of the 30 day time limit for filing a Notice of Appeal, the appeal is untimely.1
 
The respondents request that we accept the untimely appeal on certification, see 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(c); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997), and remand the record because the respondents’ former counsel provided ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondents have not complied with Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The respondents assert that they wanted to file for asylum but their attorney did not comply with their request. However, their former attorney has not been notified of this claim, or been given an opportunity to respond.
 
The respondents also contend that permitting them to file an untimely Notice of Appeal would be a reasonable accommodation under the Rehabilitation Act of 1973. The respondents have not demonstrated any specific disabilities applicable to them, or further demonstrated that the Rehabilitation Act specifically applies to them. Given these circumstances, we decline to accept the appeal on certification. The request for a stay is denied as moot.
 
Since we find that the appeal is untimely, the Immigration Judge’s decision is accordingly now final, and the record will be returned to the Immigration Court without further action. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a), 1003.38, 1003.39, 1240.14 and 1240.15.
 
*2 ORDER: The record is returned to the Immigration Court without further action.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondents were removed from the United States before the stay request was able to be adjudicated.


2016 WL 4120561 (BIA)

2016 WL 4120559 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FRANCISCO RONALDI JUAREZ-MUNOZ
File: AXXX XX2 138 - Buffalo, NY
July 13, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jose E. Perez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Joy A. Merriman
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 The respondent timely appeals the Immigration Judge’s June 4, 2015, decision denying his motion to reopen and reconsider. The respondent has filed a motion to accept his late filed brief, which we grant. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed a brief in opposition. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review issues of law, discretion, or judgment de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that the motion to reopen and reconsider were untimely filed (IJ at 3). See sections 240(c)(6) and (7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(6) and (7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.23(b)(1). Further, the respondent has not demonstrated that any exception to the time limitation on reopening applies. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4). On appeal, the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge erred in declining to exercise his discretionary sua sponte authority to reopen and/or reconsider his final order of removal. We agree with the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the motions.
 
We find no error in the Immigration Judge’s discretionary denial of reopening for the reasons set forth in his decision denying reopening (IJ at 3-5). See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). The respondent requested sua sponte reopening so that DHS may exercise its prosecutorial discretion (Motion to Reopen Sua Sponte filed 4/8/15). The DHS has not indicated that it will exercise its prosecutorial discretion in this case, and neither the Immigration Judge nor the Board have the authority to order DHS to do so. Further, the relief that the respondent seeks from DHS may not be available. See United States v. Texas, -- S.Ct.--, 2016 WL 3434401 (June 23, 2016), aff’g, 809 F.3d 134 (5th Cir. 2015). Since the respondent did not demonstrate exceptional circumstances for reopening proceedings, the Immigration Judge did not err in declining to exercise his discretionary sua sponte authority to reopen.
 
We also find no error in the Immigration Judge’s denial of reconsideration as moot (IJ Dec. dated 6/4/15 at 5). During proceedings before the Immigration Judge, the respondent, through counsel, admitted that he was not a citizen of the United States, that he was a citizen of Mexico, that he entered the United States illegally, and that he was removable as charged (Exh. 1; IJ Dec. dated 6/4/15 at 2, 4; IJ Order dated 10/20/14). His concession of removability was a condition precedent for the only relief he sought See section 240B(a)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(a)(1); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(b)(1)(i). Based on the respondent’s concessions, inter alia, the Immigration Judge granted the respondent the right to voluntarily depart within 120 days of the issuance of his order. Since the respondent did not voluntarily depart, the Immigration Judge’s order converted to an order of removal effective as of October 20, 2014.
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent does not dispute the Immigration Judge’s finding as to alienage. Rather, the respondent argues, as he did in his motion to reconsider, that his Fourth Amendment rights were violated during a stop, which subsequently brought him to the attention of DHS and caused him to be placed in proceedings (Motion to Reconsider filed 3/19/15).1 The respondent contends, as he did before the Immigration Judge, that equitable tolling applies because he pursued his rights diligently and some extraordinary circumstance stood in his way (Respondent’s Appeal Brief at 8-11). The respondent argues that he was not aware of the constitutional violation of his rights until he spoke to his new lawyer.
 
Evidence obtained as a result of a Fourth Amendment violation is suppressible in the course of a civil deportation proceeding only if the violation is egregious or undermines the reliability of the evidence in dispute. See Almeida-Amoral v. Gonzales, 461 F.3d 231, 235 (2d Cir. 2006); see also INS v. Lopez-Mendoza, 468 U.S. 1032, 1050-51 (1984). An individual in removal proceedings who raises a question about the admissibility of evidence must come forward with proof establishing a prima facie case before the Government will be called on to assume the burden of justifying the manner in which it obtained the evidence. Cotzojay v. Holder, 725 F.3d 172, 178 (2d Cir. 2013).
 
Here, the respondent did not submit a signed, dated affidavit in support of his assertions (IJ Dec. dated 10/20/14 at 4 n. 1). See Motion to Reconsider Sua Sponte at pp. 23-24 (26-27). Moreover, it is well settled that arguments of counsel are not evidence. See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 506 (BIA 1980). As such, the respondent did not meet his burden of proving a prima facie case. Cotzojay v. Holder, supra.
 
Even assuming, purely for the sake of argument, that the information was obtained through an egregious constitutional violation, the respondent’s alienage was established through means independent of any evidence of the stop he wished to suppress. See Vanegas-Ramirez v. Holder, 768 F.3d 226, 236 (2d Cir. 2014) (an alien’s concessions of removability are not fruit of the illegality, but an intervening act of free will); Pretzantzin v. Holder, 736 F.3d 641, 651 (2d Cir. 2013) (evidence of alienage is admissible if obtained through means independent of or sufficiently attenuated from the primary illegality).
 
The Immigration Judge found, and the respondent does not dispute on appeal, that the respondent admitted during his removal proceeding that he was not a citizen or national of the United States and was a native and citizen of Mexico (IJ Dec. dated 6/4/15 at 2, 4). The respondent conceded alienage about two years after the date of the alleged violations, with the assistance of counsel.2 As a result, the concession of alienage was sufficiently independent of any violation that may have previously occurred. Accordingly, the Immigration Judge correctly denied the motion to reconsider sua sponte as moot and the appeal shall be dismissed.
 
*3 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent’s motion to suppress was attached as Exhibit B to the Motion to Reconsider Sua Sponte.

2

The respondent does not allege that his former counsel provided ineffective assistance of counsel. Nor has the respondent complied with the requirements of Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LEONARDO DAVID LUNA-VINAMAO
File: AXXX XX6 613 - El Paso, TX
July 12, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jason A. Nielson, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
William M. Hunt
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 The respondent’s motion is untimely filed and is denied on that basis. See section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). The motion has not been shown to satisfy any exception to the time limitations imposed by law on motions to reopen removal proceedings. Further, untimely motions to reopen to apply for adjustment do not fall within any statutory or regulatory exception to the time limits on motions and generally will be denied. See Matter of Yauri, 25 I&N Dec. 103, 105 (BIA 2009).
 
Moreover, we decline to equitably toll the applicable time limits based on the respondent’s asserted ineffective assistance of counsel claim. The respondent has not complied with the procedural requirements for advancing such a claim, or the requirement that he show prejudice resulting from former counsel’s alleged deficient performance. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); Goonsuwan v. Ashcroft, 252 F.3d 383, 385 n.2 (5th Cir. 2001). See also Matter of Compean, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009) (directing Board to apply Lozada standards to ineffective assistance of counsel claims pending the outcome of a rulemaking process). Further, the respondent does not dispute that at the very least, he was aware of the Board’s February 7, 2012, decision approximately 3 months after the decision was issued. Other than hire current counsel, for which the respondent provides no dates or details, the respondent has not offered a persuasive explanation for waiting more than 4 years to file the present motion.
 
On this record, the respondent has not provided an adequate basis to excuse the untimeliness of the present motion. Nor has he demonstrated an exceptional situation that would warrant the exercise of our discretion to reopen his proceedings under our sua sponte authority. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997) (stating that “[t]he power to reopen on our own motion is not meant to be used as a general cure for filing defects or to otherwise circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship”). Accordingly, the respondent’s motion is denied.
 
*2 Molly Kendall-Clark
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 4120563 (BIA)
End of Document

2016 WL 4120567 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: YONG TEAK LEE A.K.A. YONG LEE
File: AXXX XX6 569 - Atlanta, Georgia
July 12, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Hiba Ghalib, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of South Korea, appeals from an Immigration Judge’s decision dated March 24, 2016, denying his motion to reopen the removal proceedings. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, and the likelihood of future events, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of Z-Z-O-, 26 I&N Dec. 586 (BIA 2015); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). The Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in an appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The record reflects that the respondent and his attorney appeared at a continued Master Calendar hearing on November 5, 2015. Based on the respondent’s admissions and concession of removability (Exh. 2) entered at the May 15, 2015, hearing, the Immigration Judge entered an order granting the respondent’s request for pre-conclusion voluntary departure pursuant to section 240B(a)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(a), with an alternate order of removal if he failed to depart on or before March 4, 2016. However, the respondent failed to voluntarily depart by the March 4, 2016, deadline. Instead, on March 4, 2016, he filed a motion alleging ineffective assistance of counsel, and seeking reopening in order to apply for adjustment of status under section 245 of the Act, 8 C.F.R. § 1255, based on his recent marriage to a United States citizen who had filed an immediate relative immigrant visa petition (Form I-130) on his behalf.
 
The record includes a document (Exh. 3) explaining the conditions attendant to the grant of the request for pre-conclusion voluntary departure, including the waiver of appeal, signed by both the respondent and his attorney on November 5, 2015, indicating their understanding and acceptance of the terms of the agreement. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(b)(1)(i). The Immigration Judge’s November 5, 2015, decision became an administratively final order on that date when the respondent agreed to the terms of the grant of pre-conclusion voluntary departure, waived appeal and accepted that decision as final. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.14 and 1003.39.
 
*2 With certain limited exceptions, an alien is permitted to file one motion to reopen a final administrative order of removal, not later than 90 days after the date on which the final administrative decision was rendered. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). The respondent filed his motion to reopen on March 4, 2016, more than 90 days after the Immigration Judge’s administratively final order entered on November 5, 2015. Thus, the Immigration Judge properly denied the motion as time-barred. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1).
 
Moreover, to the extent that respondent is arguing that his legal representation before the Immigration Court was deficient, we agree with the Immigration Judge (3-4) that the respondent has not satisfied the procedural requirements for raising a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); accord Gbaya v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 342 F.3d 1219, 1222 (11th Cir. 2003). Notwithstanding, even if we were to find the respondent had complied with the Lozada requirements, in order to establish ineffective assistance of prior counsel, the respondent must establish that his former counsel’s performance was so deficient that it impinged the “fundamental fairness” of the hearing and he suffered prejudice thereby. See Ali v. U.S. Atty. Gen., 643 F.3d 1324, 1329 (11th Cir. 2011) (alien must establish prejudice in order to prevail on ineffective assistance claim), citing Mejia Rodriguez v. Reno, 178 F.3d 1139, 1146 (11th Cir.1999); see also Dakane v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 399 F.3d 1269, 1273-74 (11th Cir.2005) (“Prejudice exists when the performance of counsel is so inadequate that there is a reasonable probability that but for the attorney’s error, the outcome of the proceedings would have been different.”); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) (establishing ineffective assistance of counsel requires an alien to demonstrate prejudice from counsel’s conduct, such that it compromised the fundamental fairness of the hearing). The term “prejudice,” refers to actual prejudice. Matter of Assaad, supra at 561. Here, no such prejudice has been shown.
 
Although the respondent now claims prejudice in that he would not have agreed to the grant of voluntary departure, if he had known he had other options, he does not identify in his motion what were those other options. Rather, the record reflects respondent’s former counsel elected to pursue the only apparent form of relief from removal then available to the respondent, pre-conclusion voluntary departure. An attorney’s tactical decisions in the preparation and presentation of an alien’s case, including accepting an extended period of voluntary departure, do not rise to the level of ineffective assistance of counsel. See, e.g., Magallanes-Damian v. INS, 783 F.2d 931, 934 (9th Cir. 1986). We find no violation of due process or a denial of a fair hearing, as the respondent, represented by counsel, had ample opportunity to meet with him; and prepare for and present his case before the Immigration Court.
 
*3 Further, while an Immigration Judge retains limited discretionary authority to reopen proceedings sua sponte in “exceptional situations,” see Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997), we agree with the Immigration Judge (I.J. at 4-5) that the respondent’s motion presented no such situation.
 
Therefore, in light of the foregoing, we find that the Immigration Judge was correct in denying the respondent’s motion to reopen. Finally, in view of our disposition, the respondent’s request for a stay of removal will be denied. Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s motion for a stay of removal is denied.
 
Brian M. O’Leary
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 4120567 (BIA)
End of Document

2016 WL 4120581 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JONATHAN POSSO CARMEN
File: AXXX XX9 746 - Charlotte, NC
July 8, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Joseph A. Baker, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Hilary Rainone
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Continuance; remand
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Colombia, appeals from the May 19, 2015, decision of the Immigration Judge denying a continuance. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including those on credibility, for clear error, and review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues on appeal on a de novo basis. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3).
 
The respondent was granted multiple continuances to await a decision on his application for deferred status pursuant to Deferred Action for Childhood Arrivals (DACA) which was ultimately denied. At his hearing before the Immigration Judge in May 2015, the respondent requested a further continuance to file a motion to vacate a criminal conviction. However, the pursuit of post-conviction relief does not constitute good cause to delay proceedings since the outcome of such an endeavor would be speculative, and a conviction is considered final for immigration purposes unless and until it is overturned. See Matter of Cuellar, 25 I&N Dec. 850, 855 (BIA 2012); Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433 (BIA 1987) (the decision to grant or deny a continuance for good cause is within the sound discretion of the Immigration Judge and will not be overturned on appeal unless it is shown that the respondent was denied a full and fair hearing); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29 and 1240.6. Therefore, we find no error in the decision of the Immigration Judge to deny the respondent’s motion for a further continuance. Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
To the extent that the respondent argues that he received ineffective assistance of counsel in his removal proceeding, we find that he has failed to substantially comply with the procedural requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). By the respondent’s own admission, he has taken only initial steps to file a formal complaint with the state bar where his prior counsel is licensed (Brief at 4). Furthermore, former counsel’s alleged failure to file a motion in state court regarding the respondent’s criminal conviction is not relevant to these removal proceedings. We thus find that he has not established that prior counsel’s representation resulted in the prejudice necessary to prevail on such a claim. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). Accordingly, the motion to remand will be denied.
 
*2 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to remand is denied.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 4120581 (BIA)
End of Document

2016 WL 4035749 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FALGUNIBEN PATEL
File: AXXX XX4 914 - Seattle, WA
June 30, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Margaret W. Wong, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Hana A. Sato
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; remand
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of India who was ordered removed from the United States in absentia on July 14, 2005, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge dated July 1, 2015, denying her motion to reopen. The respondent has filed an appeal brief, as well as a supplemental brief with additional evidence and argument, which we have construed as a motion to remand. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has expressed opposition to the appeal and the motion. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed and her motion will be denied.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
An in absentia order of removal may be rescinded only where (1) the alien files a motion to reopen within 180 days of the date of the order of removal and demonstrates that the failure to appear was due to exceptional circumstances as defined in section 240(e)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(e)(1), or (2) at any time if the alien demonstrates that he or she did not receive notice in accordance with sections 239(a)(1) and (2) of the Act, or was in Federal or State custody and the failure to appear was through no fault of the respondent. See sections 240(b)(5)(C)(i)-(ii) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii); Matter of Guzman, 22 I&N Dec. 722, 722-23 (BIA 1999).
 
Proper notification under section 239(a)(1) of the Act includes informing an alien of the time and place for his or her scheduled appearance before an Immigration Judge. See section 239(a)(1)(G)(i) of the Act. Where a hearing notice is properly addressed and sent by regular mail according to normal office procedures, a presumption of delivery attaches, although it is weaker than the presumption that applies to documents sent by certified mail. See Matter of M-R-A-, 24 I&N Dec. 665, 671-73 (BIA 2008). Additionally, a hearing notice is properly served on an alien if served on the alien’s counsel of record. See section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Act; see also Garcia v. INS, 222 F.3d 1208, 1209 (9th Cir. 2000).
 
In her motion, which she filed on March 13, 2015, the respondent sought reopening of her proceedings on the basis of lack of notice. Specifically, the respondent alleged that she received no notice of her July 14, 2005, hearing, which demonstrated inadequate representation by her former counsel preventing her from having an opportunity to present her case. The respondent also alleged in her motion that the consequences for failing to appear at her hearing were not adequately represented to her at any time after her proceedings were initiated because the warnings in the Notice to Appear were given in a language that she barely spoke (Hindi) and she did not speak, write, or read English in 2005.1 She further alleged that her former attorney’s assertion (per the tape recordings of her in absentia hearing) that he informed her of her hearing date was not true as she never heard from her attorney after she last saw him at her bond hearing. According to the respondent, she first learned of her removal order in March or April 2013 after she hired an attorney to help her look into her immigration status and she never saw a copy of the in absentia removal order until her current attorney, with whom she started speaking in July 2014, showed it to her (Respondent’s Motion at 1-9 and Tab B, Affidavit).
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge failed to consider whether she overcame the presumption of receipt under Matter of M-R-A-, 24 I&N Dec. 665 (BIA 2008), and instead simply concluded that she received proper notice because the Notice of Hearing (“NOH”) was sent to her attorney, without addressing her allegations regarding lack of notice (Respondent’s Brief at 4-7). The respondent also argues on appeal that the Immigration Judge completely ignored her contentions that her former counsel’s actions were shockingly inadequate and his performance so prejudicial as to warrant [a finding of] ineffective assistance. She asserts that she has a lack of understanding of the terms of her agreement with her former counsel and that she never filed a complaint against him but argues that, because she never signed an agreement with her former counsel, she should not be held to the same strict compliance [requirements] of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), as an individual who personally hired and signed an agreement with her attorney (Id. at 7-9).
 
The respondent also challenges on appeal the Immigration Judge’s finding that, by filing her motion almost 10 years after she was ordered removed in absentia, she did not demonstrate due diligence. According to the respondent, the delay was not unreasonable as she diligently pursued relief “since she discovered the circumstances of her claim” (Id. at 9-11). Finally, the respondent argues that sua sponte reopening of her proceedings is warranted because she intends to seek a provisional waiver, followed by a request for lawful permanent residence through consular processing, based on an approved visa petition; she is the primary caretaker of her United States citizen husband, who lost his right eye in 2000 and has had four eye surgeries in 14 years, as well as of their two United States citizen children; and, as a person of good moral character with no criminal record, is not a priority for removal under the DHS’s current guidelines (Id. at 11-13).
 
We are not persuaded by the respondent’s appellate arguments and we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision for the reasons stated in the decision (I.J. at 1-5). The respondent has not alleged that the NOH was not delivered as mailed and there are no indications on this record that that notice did not reach the respondent’s former attorney. Thus, as there is no question that the presumption of delivery attaches (section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Act), the respondent’s argument that she did not receive notice of her hearing does not support a claim for reopening (Id. at 2-3).
 
Additionally, the respondent did not file her motion within 180 days of her removal order nor establish a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel so as to obtain equitable tolling of the filing deadline (Id. at 3-4). We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, are satisfied. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec.472 (BIA 1996); see also Matter of Rivera, 21 I&N Dec. 599 (BIA 1996). Precedent from the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, within whose jurisdiction this case arises, provides that while the requirements of Lozada are generally reasonable, they need not be rigidly enforced where their purpose is fully served by other means (I.J. at 4). See Correa-Rivera v. Holder, 706 F.3d 1128, 1131 (9th Cir. 2013) (quoting Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1227 (9th Cir. 2002)); see also Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 526 (9th Cir. 2000) (“Lozada is intended to ensure both that an adequate factual basis exists in the record for an ineffectiveness complaint and that the complaint is a legitimate and substantial one”).
 
*3 We agree with the Immigration Judge that the general statements proffered by the respondent in her motion do not provide a clear indication of ineffective assistance of counsel and that the respondent has not complied with any of the Lozada requirements. First, the respondent’s affidavit does not detail the agreement that was entered into with her former counsel. Instead, it offers a vague reference of the nature of the respondent’s agreement with her attorney. Second, the respondent has conceded that she has not informed her former counsel of the allegations she has made against him, which has prevented that counsel from having an opportunity to respond to those allegations. Third, the respondent has not filed a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority against her former counsel or explained why such a complaint has not been filed (Id. at 3).
 
We further agree with the Immigration Judge that the evidence in this case shows a lack of due diligence by the respondent. The respondent could have discovered her former counsel’s alleged ineffectiveness when she hired her second attorney in March or April 2013 or, at the latest, in July 2014, when she spoke with her current attorney about the documents she has obtained pursuant to a FOIA request, yet she filed her motion in May 2015 (Id. at 4).
 
We find that, under the circumstances of this case, the respondent has not established a basis to rescind the Immigration Judge’s in absentia removal order and reopen her proceedings.
 
We also find that the respondent has not established that sua sponte reopening of these proceedings is warranted. Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). We do not find that the information provided by the respondent in this regard establishes that her case presents a truly exceptional situation warranting reopening.
 
In her motion, the respondent asks that we consider one of our unpublished decisions, dated June 25, 2015, in which we issued a remand after concluding that administrative closure to allow the alien to pursue an I-601 provisional waiver application may constitute relief for purposes of reopening. We will deny the motion for the reasons stated by the DHS in its brief opposing the respondent’s motion. See DHS’s brief titled “Department’s Opposition to the Respondent’s ‘Supplement to Respondent’s Brief in Support of Appeal.”DD’ First, the respondent’s submission, which was received after the September 17, 2015, deadline we set when we granted a briefing extension request on August 27, 2015, does not comply with the Board Practice Manual’s requirement that a brief filed outside the period granted in a briefing schedule be accompanied by a Motion to Accept Supplemental Brief that complies generally with the rules for motions, including service on the opposing party. See Board Practice Manual, section 4.7(g). Second, the respondent is asking the Board to rely on an unpublished decision, which has no precedential value. Third, even if we were to consider the non-precedential decision submitted with the motion, the circumstances in that case are very different from those in the instant case, particularly in that the motion in that case was timely filed. Thus, the respondent in the instant case is not similarly situated as the alien in our unpublished decision that he now seeks to have admitted into the record.
 
*4 Given the foregoing, we decline to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s untimely motion to reopen and will deny the respondent’s motion to remand. Accordingly, the following orders shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Brian M. O’Leary
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

According to the respondent, her best language is Gujarati and she speaks very little, and is not fluent, in Hindi (Respondent’s Motion, Tab B, Affidavit, item no. 6).

2016 WL 4035748 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: AMITKUMAR BHIKHUBHAI PATEL
File: AXXX XX8 193 - Memphis, TN
June 30, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Margaret W. Wong, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of India, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision of June 19, 2015. In that decision, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s June 10, 2015, motion to reopen proceedings in which he was ordered removed in absentia on June 3, 2010. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not filed a response to the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
The Notice of Hearing (NOH) for the June 3, 2010, hearing was mailed to the respondent’s former counsel, Barry Frager, Esquire, on December 16, 2009 (I.J. at 1; Exh. 3). The respondent failed to appear at his hearing and an in absentia order was entered on June 3, 2010. The respondent filed a motion to reopen in absentia proceedings through present counsel on June 10, 2015, alleging that he did not receive notice of his hearing. The motion further alleged that prior counsel failed to inform the respondent of his hearing. The Immigration Judge denied the motion, finding reopening not warranted based upon lack of notice because notice to counsel constitutes notice to the respondent (I.J. at 2-3). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.26(c)(2); see also Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a).
 
While the respondent alleges his counsel did not notify the respondent of his hearing date, the Immigration Judge properly found that the respondent has not shown ineffective assistance of counsel insofar as he has not complied with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988)(I.J. at 2-3). See Sako v. Gonzales, 434 F.3d 857, 863 (6th Cir. 2006). We find without merit the respondent’s appellate contention that strict adherence to the Lozada requirements is not necessary (Respondent’s Brief, at 8), where the respondent has not explained his failure to fulfill any of the Lozada requirements. See Ibarra-Reina v. Lynch, __ F.3d __ No. 15-3395, 2016 WL 3212266, at 4 (6th Cir. June 7, 2016) (holding that although Lozada is not enforced rigidly, neither have requirements been as loosely applied as the alien suggests).1
 
*2 In Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998), the Board held that an alien may reopen for purposes of pursuing previously unavailable relief in circumstances where she was not given oral warnings in her native language of the consequences of failing to appear at a deportation hearing and where the motion complies with the general time limits governing motions to reopen. The motion is untimely, however, and thus reopening on this basis is not warranted.
 
We also agree with the Immigration Judge that no exceptional circumstances have been presented to warrant sua sponte reopening (I.J. at 11). Pursuant to our holding in Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997), the power to reopen sua sponte is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations where enforcing them might result in hardship. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). While the respondent has presented some positive factors and alleges hardship to his U.S. citizen spouse and children, these factors either individually or in the aggregate are not so exceptional as to trigger the limited authority to reopen proceedings sua sponte. Indeed, many aliens who have been present in the United States for a period of years have accumulated favorable equities subsequent to being ordered removed. See Matter of Bedford, 22 I&N Dec. 1216 (BIA 2000) (burden is on the movant to show exceptional circumstances).
 
For the foregoing reasons, and those articulated by the Immigration Judge in his decision, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision, pursuant to our authority at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(5).
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

We find meritless the respondent’s argument on appeal that the Immigration Judge failed to consider Matter of M-R-A-, 24 I&N Dec. 665, 675 (BIA 2008) (Respondent’s Brief, at 7). That decision is inapposite because the facts are distinguishable from the case here.

2016 WL 4035770 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE ANGEL GONZALEZ
File: AXXX XX3 203 - Orlando, FL
June 27, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
David Stoller, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Alexandra Rivas
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent is a native and citizen of Mexico. On August 18, 2014, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal. On April 14, 2016, he filed an untimely motion to reopen. The respondent alleges that the time limitation should be equitably tolled due to ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent contends, inter alia, that his former counsel should have disputed the charges of removability more vigorously and that he should not have been treated as an arriving alien. The respondent further argues that due to intervening precedent, his conviction is no longer an aggravated felony and he is eligible for cancellation of removal.
 
The motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel is denied as the respondent has not complied with the requirements of Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Although he attached a copy of a complaint to bar counsel, the motion does not include any evidence that it was actually filed (Motion, Exh. E). Further, the respondent has not demonstrated due diligence. See Avila-Santoyo v. Att’y Gen., 713 F.3d 1357, 1363 n. 5 (11th Cir. 2013) (en banc). The respondent has been aware of the requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, since our August 18, 2014, decision, but he still has not demonstrated compliance.
 
We will, however, grant the motion to reopen under our discretionary sua sponte power due to intervening precedent. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). See Donawa v. Att’y Gen., 735 F.3d 1275 (11th Cir. 2013)(conviction under section 893.13(1)(a) of Florida law does not qualify as an aggravated felony under the “drug trafficking crime” clause of section 101(a)(43)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act). Accordingly, we will remand this proceeding for further consideration of whether the respondent is eligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a). The following orders shall be issued.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded for further proceedings in accordance with this decision.
 
Molly Kendall-Clark
FOR THE BOARD
